# 岘泉集

**The Xianquan Collection**

*Scripture Title:* *Xianquan Collection*
*Author:* Zhang Yuchu of the Ming Dynasty
*Twelve Scrolls*
*Source Edition:* *Zhengtong Daoist Canon*, Orthodox Section, Part One

**Preface to the *Xianquan Collection* by the Celestial Master of Non-Action of Qishan**

That which is most subtle and most refined in Heaven and Earth is the Dao; that which is most luminous and most manifest is literature. The Dao is not illuminated without literature, and literature is not established without the Dao. Though analytically they may be considered two, in essence they are fundamentally one.

The covering and bearing of Heaven and Earth, the transformation of yin and yang, the alternation of cold and heat, the coming and going of the sun and moon, the flowing and standing of mountains and rivers, the flourishing and withering of grasses and trees—none of these are apart from the Dao, none apart from literature. How could they be separated into two, or forcibly united as one?

Thus, when the sages arose, they followed the Dao of spontaneity and expressed it as the literature of spontaneity, never imposing the slightest trace of their own intent. Therefore, they composed the *Book of Changes* in accordance with the principles of transformation, the *Book of Documents* in accordance with the forms of instruction, the *Book of Songs* in accordance with the traces of governance, the *Spring and Autumn Annals* in accordance with the methods of praise and blame, the *Book of Rites* in accordance with the substance of ritual decorum, and the *Book of Music* in accordance with the function of harmony. These Six Classics endure through Heaven and Earth, uniting past and present without change, precisely because they arise from spontaneity.

Where else could later writers of literature turn? After the decline of the Zhou, the Kingly Way faded and the Hundred Schools flourished, each vying with private opinions and conjectural theories, displaying rhetorical skill to outdo one another. Among them, scarcely one or two did not deviate from the Dao.

At that time, there was Laozi, whose words took non-action and quiescence as the Dao, and who composed a work of over five thousand characters. Later ages have seen those who governed by his teachings and achieved order—perhaps these came closest to the Dao.

The Perfected One of the Succession, Celestial Master Zhang of Non-Action, inherited the teachings of Laozi through his family lineage, being the forty-third transmission. By nature, the Master was keenly intelligent and of towering talent and insight. He exhaustively studied the texts of the Jade Treasury, the alchemical crucible, and the golden and jade scriptures of the Daoist canon, while also delving deeply into the Six Classics, the philosophers, the histories, and the works of the Hundred Schools. In the realm of literary composition, he brought each form to its utmost refinement.

Thus, in his successive appointments to the imperial court, he was ever esteemed for the excellence of his learning, deeply favored by the celestial canopy, and repeatedly honored with special commendations and the honor of being summoned to the imperial presence, his annual stipends ever increasing. The princes, nobles, high ministers, and scholar-officials all treated him with the utmost reverence. For in the literary heartland of Jiangyou, where the legacy of Master Wen Zheng of Wu and Master Wen Jing of Yu still flourished, the Master surpassed even their achievements. How could his attainments have been merely incidental?

When asked to inspect his collected poetry and prose—several dozen scrolls in all—and to compose a preface, I found his verse to be profound and serene, his prose rich and elegant, so that one reads with admiration unceasing. How do they compare to those of this age, who cling to their glosses and exegeses, complacently content with mere talent and skill?

Yet the Master, bearing the weighty responsibility of leading the ancestral lineage of the Dao, devoted himself wholly to transforming others, guiding them toward goodness, and aiding the state in securing its blessings. What appears here in these writings is but the overflow of his vast learning. Nevertheless, I have observed that the Master’s virtue and work are rooted in non-action, enabling him to roam the mind in the Great Beginning and unite with the Dao. Moreover, he has steeped himself in the literary arts of the Six Classics, explored the doctrines of the Hundred Schools, and thus, when he gives expression to his thoughts in writing, principle and intent converge, achieving excellence without striving for it. How could his contributions to the Way of Laozi and Zhuangzi be compared to the boasts of those who indulge in wild fantasies?

As the Master and I share a bond of lifelong friendship, whenever we discuss these matters, we do so with animated delight, clapping our hands in lively discourse until the conversation is exhausted. Thus, I have written this preface.

The work is titled *Xiànquán*, after the name of his retreat.

Preface by Wang Shen of Jinhua, Erudite of the Directorate of Education.

---

**Preface to the *Xiànquán* Literary Collection of the Celestial Master of Non-Action of Qishan**

The *Xiànquán* Collection is the work of the Perfected Zhang, the forty-third Celestial Master in succession from the Han. The Perfected One’s learning and conduct run deep, his natural endowments and insight surpassing all others. He has mastered the Three Teachings, fusing them into a single path, and has thoroughly explored the doctrines of the various philosophers and the Hundred Schools, attaining effortless clarity in all. Thus, when he gives voice to his literary compositions and discourses, they are bold, towering, and magnificent—reading them stirs one to ceaseless applause.

I have long admired his writings, which flow like clouds traversing the sky, radiant and resplendent, their transformations beyond measure, assuming myriad forms in an instant, brilliant in their artistry. They are also like autumn waters, rich and deep, stirred by a gentle breeze into waves that stretch across ten thousand acres, a single expanse of jade-like clarity, profound in their serenity. His *ci*, *fu*, poetry, and songs each achieve the utmost in grace, freshness, and purity, capturing the natural wonder of Heaven’s spontaneity—truly a work that combines every excellence and embodies all beauty.

I have heard that on the famed Dragon-Tiger Mountain, where numinous *qi* gathers and the finest essences converge, Perfected Ones have emerged in succession, transmitting the Dao and its virtue through the ages. Yet it is only in the present Perfected One that I see one whose inner richness of the Dao is matched by the outward brilliance of his literary art, making him a literary colossus of his age.

Since the founding of our august dynasty, the Great Progenitor, the High Emperor, unified the realm, and the vital *qi* of heaven and earth flourished in full. The celestial mandate has prospered, and culture has flourished for over thirty years. Since the present Emperor ascended the throne, the *qi* has grown ever more abundant, and the mandate ever more prosperous. Even in the remotest corners of the land, none fail to display their talents and present their works, striving to distinguish themselves in their time. How much more so in this land of literary heritage, this lineage of divine brilliance, where one may leisurely bask in the imperial grace and immerse oneself in the classics? Thus, the *Xiànquán* Collection, Scroll One, composed by the Celestial Master of Non-Action, Zhang Yuchu of Qishan.

*Chongdao* [Carving of the Dao]

Within the utmost void, vast and boundless, the myriad existences take form. The substantial resides within the void, desolate and without limit, as a single *qi* pervades the emptiness. Without the void, things could not transform and circulate, as if lacking a vessel to contain their subtle workings; yet within the substantial, there are indeed convergence and dispersion, presence and absence. Without the substantial, *qi* could not bind and weave, open and close, as if lacking a foundation to conceal its function; yet within the void, there are ascent and descent, growth and decline, movement and stillness.

The vastness of heaven and earth takes the Great Void as its essence, while the ceaseless generation and transformation of the myriad things between them arise from the interplay of yin and yang, movement and stillness. The alternation of cold and heat, the distinction of day and night, the ceaseless revolution of heaven—manifest as the luminous sun and stars, majestic as thunder and lightning, nourishing as wind, rain, frost, and dew. The ceaseless revolution of earth—towering as mountains and peaks, flowing as rivers and seas, flourishing as grasses, trees, birds, and beasts. From the grand and lofty to the myriad forms below, from the tiniest crawling insects to the subtlest stirrings, all are encompassed within the utmost void, their profound and wondrous workings beyond comprehension. This is what is called the Dao, the Principle.

Were the Dao not so vast, the Principle not so refined, how could it govern such supremely subtle and wondrous mechanisms? Thus it encompasses heaven and earth, engenders and nurtures the myriad things, fulfilling the Dao of participation and assistance.

Therefore, those who know the Dao do not observe things but observe the heart-mind. For the heart-mind governs nature and emotions, and the Principle resides within it; *qi* is bound to form, both flowing from the mandate of heaven. Called "numinous emptiness," "Supreme Ultimate," "the Center," or "the One," these are the heart-mind’s original state—thus the heart-mind is the Supreme Ultimate. Every thing possesses this nature. The tangled forms and colors of things, their resonant sounds, all arise from the harmonious yet unequal blending of being and non-being.

The profusion and flux of the myriad things are but the dregs and embers of *qi*. Both humans and the ten thousand things dwell together in the Void. Yet within the minute compass of a square inch, the heart-mind can fill the vastness of the cosmos and encompass the multitude of things, moving in tandem with heaven and earth without discord—this is because when the heart-mind is empty, the ten thousand existences are all contained therein.

Why should joy and anger, delight and sorrow, fleeting pleasure and gain or loss suffice to obstruct our emptiness or block our thoroughfare? When emptiness prevails, its function is effortless. As my *Laozi* says: "The Dao is hollow, yet its use is inexhaustible; profound, it seems the ancestor of the myriad things." "Hollow" is but another name for emptiness. *Zhuangzi* says: "Only the Dao abides in emptiness." *Liezi* says: "Emptiness finds its proper dwelling, for only emptiness can contain." When the Dao gathers, spirit congeals; when spirit congeals, *qi* transforms; when *qi* transforms, it becomes one with the Great Void, flows in unison with heaven and earth, and the two *qi* and five phases circulate through the six directions, ceaselessly coming and going—all in harmonious interplay, sharing the same function.

If one empties the heart-mind of contentious thoughts and preserves it with unity, then within it is empty yet not full, outwardly it is clear yet not stagnant—deep as an abyss, bright as a mirror. Thus one’s inherent nature aligns with the virtue of heaven, and how could it not be the ancestor of the myriad things? Therefore, in nurturing its substance, one discards ornamentation, forgets self and other, severs defilements, and exhausts the work of attaining emptiness and preserving stillness—thereby returning to life and reverting to the root, deepening the root and securing the stem, cleansing the mirror of the mysterious. Embracing the One and guarding the Center, the Valley Spirit endures; thoughts are purified, desires diminished; emptiness reaches its extreme, stillness its utmost—one returns to the Limitless. Then emptiness and quiescence become luminous and penetrating, and things no longer enslave us but are enslaved by us.

In fulfilling its function, one casts off the limbs and body, sets aside cleverness and brilliance, takes the fundamental as essence, the external as coarse, inquiry as the root, and restraint as the guiding principle—thus nothing remains to be seen. Before heaven and earth, at the beginning of *qi*, form, and substance, there were the Great Beginning, the Great Commencement, and the Great Simplicity—the primordial Kunlun of chaos. When clarity and turbidity separated, essence emerged and radiated, measuring things and bestowing life: essence became the Three Luminaries, the sun and moon the Five Phases; phases gave rise to emotions, emotions to the marrow within, the marrow to spirit and illumination, spirit and illumination to virtue and power, and virtue and power to patterns and ornamentation. The yang does not stir without giving rise to its teachings; the yin does not rest without accomplishing its transformations.

Applied to governing the state and cherishing the people, entrusted to the world, it renders the world clear and upright. All this takes non-action and tranquility as its foundation, humility and restraint as its root—is this not the Dao of inner sageliness and outer kingship? Yet within the formless and boundless, all is the function of the heavenly Dao. Here there are mutual stirring and engendering, mutual overthrow and shaping, mutual reliance and concealment—ineffable, unfathomable. Is this not the spirit’s boundlessness, the *Yijing*’s formlessness? Yet the mechanisms of heaven and earth, the reckonings of things and affairs, can be foreknown and hidden away. From emptiness comes numinosity, and spirit operates within, unfolding its knowledge. Though there be myriad changes and transformations, all emerge from this.

Only by serving heaven with sincerity, nurturing life with harmony, and benefiting things with compassion does the burden of heaven respond without distinction. There is no exalted deed difficult to practice, no extraordinary discourse to delight in. Why speak of cunning, deceit, or divine marvels? It is simply a matter of harmonizing *qi* with emptiness and following things as they are. Of those who applied this and saw results: Guangcheng instructed the Yellow Emperor, Cao Shen heeded Gaitong, the Yellow Stone admonished the Marquis of Liu, and the Han governed through tranquility. Some say they appropriated this to advance their own schemes, seeking personal gain without regard for righteousness—this is perhaps an overstatement.

Yet history records that Huang-Lao and the Legalists, with recluses offering unrestrained critiques, blended Lao-Zhuang with Guan-Yan, and from this emerged Shen Buhai, Han Fei, Tian Pian, Shen Dao, Zou Yan, Sunzi, Shang Yang, Lü Buwei, Chao Cuo, and Chun Yu—later degenerating into the stratagems of Su Qin, Zhang Yi, Gan Mao, and Cai Ze, causing grievous harm. It is not merely that their emptiness and annihilation were pathological; rather, during the Wei-Jin period, Liu, Ruan, Wang, and He engaged in lofty but reckless discourse, subverting ethics. Han Yu called it worse than Yang Zhu and Mo Di, claiming Lao-Zhuang caused ruin. How could those who study this not drown in bias and lose themselves in excess, ultimately inviting collapse? How could they avoid the world’s censure and reproach? Their harm was even greater than that of the Legalists and unrestrained critics!

Nevertheless, one must examine it with precision and seek it with restraint. Only then can one know the vastness and breadth of the Lao-Zhuang Dao. Alas, the Dao is one—its subtle, wondrous, and penetrating essence, the marvel of spirit’s response to the infinitesimal—how could it be divided in two? Moreover, within the profound and obscure lies essence; within the vague and indistinct lies form. Within my central yellow shoulder, inner emptiness and outer harmony flow through the four limbs and unite with the hundred spirits. The five *qi* congeal and spread, merging with the vigorous and compliant virtues of heaven and earth.

Its essentials lie in unifying one’s nature, nurturing one’s *qi*, roaming the beginning and end of the myriad things, and securing the preservation of pure *qi*. As Master Sima said: "The myriad things all originate from the Void, are born of *qi*; *qi* forms the body, the body receives nature, nature distinguishes names, names establish conduct, and conduct awaits destiny. Thus the Void is the storehouse of things." This is what he meant by the Void. The operation of the Void begins with the five phases: one and six placed behind, two and seven placed before, three and eight placed to the left, four and nine placed to the right, circulating through fifty-five phases—this is what is called "the Void’s profound mystery."

This, too, is one aspect of the art of safeguarding destiny. Only by maintaining emptiness within can one fathom the divine and comprehend transformation, thereby attaining the Dao of tracing origins and returning to endings. As for refining the corporeal soul, expelling the void to master *qi*, riding the uprightness of heaven and earth, and harnessing the fluctuations of the six *qi*; roaming the eight directions in spirit, transcending the posterior heaven to reach the end, exhausting the boundless cycles of worldly change; roaming the mind in tranquility, merging *qi* with the vastness—thus surpassing the primal state of chaos and soaring beyond the primordial mist: gazing through the ages as if in a single breath, viewing life and death as but morning and evening. Like the cicada’s molt, like the breath’s exhalation. Before the beginning of heaven and earth, after their end—all is the operation of my emptiness. How can this be compared to those who cling to life, grasp at things, and refuse transformation?

If one merely steals fame and seizes reputation in their time, their obscuration by deceit and entanglement in excess, their ceaseless toil and anxious striving—how do they differ from dust and chaff? They are but thieves of names and usurpers of praise. Alas! To know the attainment of emptiness is to be enlightened; enlightenment leads to purity, purity to penetration, penetration to the divine. Divinity achieves speed without haste, arrival without movement—responding to all, reaching all. Otherwise, how can one be deemed a true student of our Old Master? Who can speak of the ultimate emptiness with such as these?

**Vigilance in Foundations**
Learning must have a foundation—this is what is meant by "ordering the world and transcending it." If learning is not directed toward what is essential, it cannot be deemed worthy of aspiration. What is essential? The learning of ordering the world, which is the Dao of the sages and worthies. And what is the Dao of the sages and worthies? It is the Way of virtue and power, nature and destiny, benevolence and righteousness.

At the dawn of the Three Dynasties, the Dao resided in Tang and Yu. Later speakers of the Dao invariably pointed to this. For the Dao was luminous under the Three Sovereigns, virtue resplendent under the Five Emperors, and laws perfected under the Three Kings. Yao, Shun, Yu, Tang, Wen, and Wu as rulers fulfilled the Dao of rulership; Gaoyao, Yi, Fu, Zhou, and Shao as ministers fulfilled the Dao of ministership; Confucius, Yan, Zeng, Zisi, and Mencius as teachers fulfilled the Dao of teaching. What has been modeled for ten thousand generations remains unchanged.

The sages and worthies are distant, yet their Dao endures in the Six Classics. The *Book of Changes* illuminates yin and yang, deducing the transformations of creation; the *Book of Songs* guides human nature and emotions, distinguishing the orthodoxy and heterodoxy of *feng* and *ya*; the *Book of Documents* records governance and affairs, chronicling the continuities and reforms of decrees; the *Spring and Autumn Annals* demonstrates rewards and punishments, clarifying the hierarchy of honoring kings and restraining hegemons; the *Book of Rites* refines ritual and decorum, elucidating the distinctions of rank and duty; the *Book of Music* harmonizes *qi* and connection, attaining the harmony of heaven and earth. All the essentials of the sages’ transmission of the mind and imparting of the Dao are fully contained herein—nothing surpasses this.

Yet the profound subtleties of the Six Classics are all present; how could our minds, luminous and clear, not derive them from this? From Yao and Shun onward, the transmission was simply to "hold the center and maintain reverence," to dwell in the mind. Confucius spoke of benevolence, Zisi of sincerity, the *Great Learning* of reverence, Mencius of the mind, and the *Doctrine of the Mean* of the center—all converge as one. To achieve the work of unifying heaven and humanity is to attain centrality and harmony, preserve sincerity and clarity, exhaust the principles of things, and fulfill the natures of beings. Only then can one assist heaven and earth in their positions, nurture the myriad things, regulate the Dao of heaven and earth, and aid the suitability of heaven and earth. Thus, wisdom encompasses the myriad things, and the Dao aids the world. This is the Dao of the noble person: rooted in the self, verified among the people, examined in the Three Kings, established in heaven and earth, tested by ghosts and spirits, and awaited by a hundred generations of sages without doubt.

To establish the ultimate for heaven and earth, to establish destiny for the people, to uphold the constants, and to support the teachings of the world—steadfast and unyielding. Guarding it as the great constant, practicing it as the great law; manifest as ritual and music, hidden as ghosts and spirits—it cannot be abandoned for a single day or separated for a moment. One must devote oneself to the work of vigilance, reverence, and awe in the moments of solitary reflection and the junctures of action and restraint, so that the body is established and its function enacted.

First, cessation leads to stability; stability to stillness; stillness to peace; peace to contemplation; contemplation to attainment. Over time, even the obscure can achieve sincerity; sincerity takes form; form becomes manifest; manifestation becomes luminous; luminosity moves; movement transforms; transformation evolves—all derived from the self. Is this not the fulfillment of nature and destiny, dwelling in benevolence and following righteousness? Gathered, it retreats into secrecy; applied, it serves the world and the state in small matters, and governs the world and the state in great ones.

The teachings of Xun, Yang, Dong, Han, Zhou, Cheng, Shao, Zhang, Zhu, and Lü all carry the Dao, serving as wings to the Classics. The writings of Zuo, Qu, Jia, Sima, Li, Liu, Ouyang, Su, Zeng, and Wang—all composed to express the Dao, not merely to convey it. Without the Dao, however skillful, they are of no value. Thus, when cultivated as virtue and conduct, and expressed as literature, all align with the true Dao.

Yet since the Qin and Han dynasties, there have been studies of memorization, literary composition, and cunning schemes—each distinct, yet none approaching the learning of the sages and worthies. Their words aim to benefit ten thousand generations and leave an indelible legacy. Exhausting the greatness of heaven and earth, one knows not its limit; harmonizing the balance of yin and yang, one knows not its truth; plumbing the depths of ghosts and spirits, one knows not its secrecy. How can mere ornamentation, embellishment, plagiarism, or ostentation—designed to astonish the world and impress the vulgar—suffice? Without seeking true knowledge and practical realization, how can one determine the right course in action and speech, silence and stillness?

Here Confucius and Mencius traversed the states, seeking to put their Dao into practice. When the Dao could not be realized, they withdrew to cultivate their own integrity, thus preserving the balance between engagement and retreat. Therefore, those who withdraw to distant heights, aspiring to transcend illusion and rise above the dust of the world, must seek the Dao that transcends the mundane—this is what we call the teaching of Laozi and Zhuangzi.

Laozi first served as the Archivist of Zhou beneath the pillars, later becoming the Keeper of the Royal Archives. He composed the *Dao and Virtue* in two scrolls, expounding the Dao of inner sageliness and outer kingliness. Those who shared Laozi’s path included Taigong, who instructed King Wen; Guan Yin, who served as magistrate; Kang Cang, who dwelt in Mount Weilei; Zhuangzi, who governed the Lacquer Garden; and Liezi, who resided in the gardens of Zheng. Like the lofty recluses Chao Fu and Xu You, the pure Boyi and Shuqi, and the hidden Shang Hao, they held fast to their principles without bending. To them, fame was but a guest to reality; they would rather dwell in filth than be adorned like sacrificial oxen. Daily they embraced jars and carried reeds, content in their lot—whether noble or base, joyful or sorrowful, gaining or losing—nothing could burden their hearts. Thus, with single-minded focus, they entered the center of the ring and responded to the boundless. They could govern heaven and earth, store all things, take heaven and earth as their great furnace and creation as their great bellows. The cosmos was too narrow to measure their breadth, thunder too feeble to match their spirit, rivers and seas too shallow to plumb their depth, mountains and peaks too low to rival their height, ghosts and spirits too dim to fathom their mystery. They straightened crookedness into benevolence, trod the forked path as righteousness, let flow what was scattered as joy, and plucked what was obscure as ritual. They equated life and death, harmonized self and other, defied poverty and success, and stood alone in their singular way, unwavering.

Thus, the Three Powers were unified, the myriad things made equal, the principles of things mastered, and forms and vessels given abode. Governing the world was as effortless as shaping clay. In non-action, they used the world with ease; in action, they were used by the world and found it insufficient. They regarded life as an excrescence, death as the bursting of a boil, and external things as mere attachments—thus aligning with those free from desire. Their words abounded in parables and grand discourses, unbridled and vast. Yet in stillness, they were sages; in motion, they were kings. Still, they shared the virtue of yin; in motion, they rode the waves of yang. Their movement was like heaven, their stillness like earth; they commanded the transformations of things while guarding their essence. Those confined within form and qi could not disturb their constancy beyond it. In harmony with the harmony of heaven, attuned to the joy of heaven, resting in the balance of heaven, and flowing with the natural division of heaven, they entrusted themselves to the myriad transformations, roaming alone in the celestial realm—thus preserving their heavenly integrity. Had they not embodied the boundless and wandered without trace, becoming companions of heaven, how could they have achieved this?

To them, the paths of chariots and carriages, the realms of fame and profit, were as nothing. They did not flaunt falsehoods to delude the world, nor parade their conduct to mislead the masses, seeking to be seen as proud or eccentric. Such was their Dao. Yet later scholars, failing to seek the root of Dao and virtue or the foundation of nature and destiny, instead indulge in base, deluded, and absurd practices, hoping to glimpse the primordial chaos of the Great Beginning—how could they possibly succeed?

Consider the humble worm that feeds on petals, nests in thistles, wears a sorrowful countenance, and lives meagerly, starving its body and fraying its garments, so that others can scarcely bear the sight. Its dwelling is shared with woodcutters and herdsmen, deer and swine; its joy lies in mist and clouds, fish and birds. Its heart is like dead ashes, its form like withered wood. It holds itself in high esteem, regards itself from afar, and treats itself with gravity—how could mere worldly splendor move it in the slightest? Even if one arrived with a team of four horses and a chest of gold, it could not be reached. If forced to approach, it would vanish in an instant, leaving no trace, no lingering form. When the Dao aligned, it remained; when the Dao departed, it left—content with what it inherently possessed. How could lavish offerings, ornate dwellings, or positions of power ever bind or constrain it? These are the lofty ones of the world—the followers of Jie Yu and He Xian.

Yet those who feign such ways to curry favor with the world are different. In retreat, they are reckless; in advancement, they are aggressive, seeking fame and fleeting praise. Rising suddenly from obscurity, they abandon their principles at the first opportunity, indulging in extravagance—refining their attire, savoring fine foods, swelling with arrogance, boasting in speech, filling their retinues with ostentation, and adorning their dwellings with splendor. Their companions are the eminent and powerful; their attendants scurry about, vying to be their peers. With darting glances and haughty airs, they proclaim to the crowd: "Behold, this is the Dao, this is virtue, the essence of learning, the spirit of cultivation—who would not revere and exalt it?" Yet what drives their scramble and competition is power, profit—what do they know of Dao and virtue?

Alas, those who adorn reality with false names—why not simply seize the world’s clamor and exclusion? They seek to deceive the age and please the times. Who knows that even if they dazzle for a moment, their delusions will ultimately be exposed? How could they ever earn the trust of later generations? This is precisely what those of true Dao do not do.

I fear that the decline of customs and the vulgarity of mores will flow unchecked, resist all restraint, and grow ever more rampant—all because people fail to guard the root. Moreover, when the noble person errs in engagement or withdrawal, speech or silence, straying from righteousness and the Dao, how grievous and perverse it becomes! Can one not be cautious?

To know what to guard is to unite the Dao in oneness. To abandon this is to render all learning meaningless.

Someone asked in profound inquiry: What does the Daoist school mean by "profound" (xuan)?

Answer: "Profound" refers to Heaven. It is the great origin of the Dao that issues from Heaven.

Question: Does this not begin with Laozi’s phrase "profound and yet more profound"?

Answer: Precisely. I have heard from historians that the Daoist school enables one to unify spirit and focus, to move in accord with the formless, and to embrace the myriad things. In its practice of self-preservation, it follows the great harmony of yin and yang, adopts the virtues of Confucianism and Mohism, and distills the essentials of the School of Names and Legalism. Adapting to the times and responding to change, it establishes customs and implements affairs with perfect appropriateness—its principles concise and easy to grasp, its efforts minimal yet its achievements vast.

As for the essentials of the great Dao: it discards aggressiveness and desire, rejects cunning and cleverness. If one abandons these and relies on self-preservation, there is nothing to be gained. It is also said: The Daoist school practices non-action yet leaves nothing undone. Its reality is easy to practice, though its words are difficult to comprehend. Its self-preservation is rooted in emptiness and non-being, and its function lies in following the natural course. Without fixed power or constant form, it can thoroughly investigate the nature of the myriad things. It does not precede things, nor does it follow them—yet it can serve as the master of all things. This is why the Grand Historian placed the Yellow Emperor and Laozi before the Six Classics.

Laozi lived at the end of the Zhou dynasty and once served as Archivist beneath the Pillars. As the Zhou declined, he composed the *Upper* and *Lower Classics* and then withdrew into reclusion. His disciples included Guan Yin, Kang Cang, Zhuangzi, and Liezi, all of whose teachings fundamentally aimed at cultivating the Dao and virtue, preserving life and destiny. Internally, one cultivates by embracing the One and guarding the center to preserve life; externally, one acts without contention or striving to benefit others. Only by abiding in great harmony, moving in accord with nature, being cautious within and inquiring without, and maintaining purity without admixture can one attain stillness and unity without change, resting in boundless tranquility. When in motion, one follows the movement of Heaven, thereby aligning with Heaven’s virtue.

Though engaged in the world, one employs compassion, frugality, humility, and restraint, ensuring that one’s actions do not exceed what is necessary. Firmly guarding withdrawal and concealment, one does not act for the sake of things, returning instead to emptiness, simplicity, purity, stillness, non-action, softness, and plainness. Thus, when the world’s things are based in humility, they remain unburdened; when they contain emptiness, they encounter no obstacles; when they proceed with ease, they face no difficulties; and when they adapt with flexibility, they meet no blockages. The people are thus enabled to transform themselves, return to simplicity, and rectify themselves.

The aim is to preserve the original nature of things and return to the primordial unity. Therefore, it does not cling to the entanglements of benevolence, righteousness, loyalty, trust, governance, education, or custom—yet this is not to say these are incompatible with the world. Indeed, when the Zhou declined and customs grew shallow, the Daoists sought to remedy corruption and save the age, restoring it to pure antiquity. They adopted the virtues of Confucianism and Mohism.

Confucius once inquired of Laozi about ritual. Laozi said: "Those who are keenly perceptive and deeply discerning yet near to death are those who delight in criticizing others. Those who are broadly learned and far-reaching in argument yet endanger themselves are those who expose the faults of others." This is what Confucius meant by being broadly learned in antiquity and knowledgeable of the present—words he heard from the venerable Laozi.

When Yangzi rejected universal love, he met Laozi and abandoned his contentiousness to yield his seat. When Mozi advocated uniformity and the worship of spirits, his teachings bore some resemblance to Daoism, and the works of Zhuangzi and Liezi further blended these ideas. The School of Names, in governing the state and commanding armies, valued worthy men and cherished the people, approaching Daoist thought. The School of Vertical and Horizontal Alliances, in its strategies of expansion and contraction, strength and weakness, gain and loss, also bore similarities. Yet none have endured as long or served as the foundation of the Dao like the practices of unifying spirit, embracing the myriad things, discarding aggressiveness and desire, and rejecting cunning and cleverness—all of which take emptiness and non-being as their root.

If one is without desire and returns to simplicity, without pretense yet trustworthy, blunting sharpness, resolving entanglements, harmonizing light with dust, placing oneself last yet coming first, and setting oneself aside yet enduring—then one can bend to preserve the whole, straighten the crooked, know fullness yet guard humility, and know the new yet preserve the old. Thus, one may be bright yet appear dim, advance yet seem to retreat, eloquent yet appear inarticulate, skillful yet seem clumsy, and upright yet appear bent. Without cutting away one’s square edges or defiling one’s integrity, one may be direct yet not unrestrained, radiant yet not dazzling, thereby preserving one’s efficacy.

If one acts with sincerity, proceeds with simplicity, responds with forgiveness, and answers with silence, regarding worldly affairs as coarse traces and emptiness as the wondrous function—then the Confucians, with their parables and fanciful discourses, may dismiss these as absurd and impractical, unsuited to worldly use. Nevertheless, such teachings do not depart from the principles of the Three Powers, the order of the myriad things, the constancy of life and death, or the external traces of affairs. They truly reveal what lies beyond the Six Directions, before Heaven and Earth, gathering the ten thousand existences into a single breath and dispersing a single breath into the ten thousand existences.

This is called "serene quiescence," "profound depth," "lucid clarity," "empty sameness," and "obscure darkness." Truly, it is vast and boundless, wandering in the Great Beginning. Moreover, the movements of Heaven and Earth: the light and pure that rise and float are accumulations of qi; the heavy and turbid that sink and settle are accumulations of earth. Circulating through the Six Voids, they come and go without end, expanding and contracting, advancing and retreating, hard and soft, flowing through day and night, alternating with the four seasons. Wind and thunder shape forms, the myriad beings emerge and grow, self-generating and self-transforming, self-forming and self-coloring, self-dissolving and self-ceasing. The ten thousand things intermingle in chaos yet remain undivided; their black forms and material qualities all contain the essence of Heaven and Earth and are transformed into life.

Thus, they are constantly generating and constantly transforming without cease. Their alternations of darkness and light, their succession without a moment’s difference—this is why one transcends Heaven and Earth, leaves the myriad things behind, escapes the confines of principle and qi, and enters the boundless, traceless origin of creation. It cannot be measured by sound or scent, nor sought through images or numbers. Obscure, indistinct, and imperceptible, it transcends the ten thousand things and serves as their root—how could it not be the most profound and subtle?

When Yang Xiong spoke of the "profound," he aligned it with the numbers of the *Book of Changes*, establishing the divisions of regions, states, districts, and households—this was merely one aspect of calendrical studies, not what I call the profound.

Sima Qian said: Those who study Laozi disparage the Confucians, while those who study the Confucians likewise disparage Laozi—why must they mutually ridicule one another so fiercely? Yet the excellence of the profound and empty lies precisely in this: later followers who took it as their creed erred into absurdity, bias, and distortion. Could this truly be the fault of Laozi and Zhuangzi?

The true learner must therefore seek the root of emptiness and nothingness—this is what I call the profound. Someone asked: When you speak of the profound, you seem to ground it in reality, yet the classics all speak of it as existing before Heaven and Earth, humbling the cosmos, disparaging hegemonic ways, and exalting the kingly Dao. What they value is turning inward to seek within oneself, cultivating the self to enrich life, transcending form to leave illusion behind—then spiritual transformation becomes unfathomable, and one outlives Heaven to endure. How does this differ from the teachings of Laozi and Zhuangzi?

I reply: The Dao is one and the same—how could there be any distinction between emptiness and fullness, reality and unreality? I have examined the records and texts, and they do not deceive. Laozi’s transmission takes the *Daode*’s two chapters as its foundation. Later followers either err through杂乱杂糅 or through wild exaggeration. When they speak of "inner sageliness and outer kingship," they do not lack the art of ruling as a sovereign, but merely apply it differently. In cultivation, they speak of the Valley Spirit and the Mysterious Female, of attaining emptiness and guarding stillness, of preserving the center and embracing the One, of abiding in harmony—nothing more.

Later proponents of these teachings inevitably distinguish between the true and the false, the orthodox and the heterodox. The *Yinfu Jing*’s teachings on cultivating the self and governing the world align with the *Daode Jing*. The *Longhu Shangjing*’s text accords with the *Cantong Qi* of the Stone Wall. Yet those who study Laozi—what path could they follow if not that of immortality? Their "inner elixir" invariably takes spirit and qi as its foundation, while their "outer elixir" invariably takes lead and mercury as its essence. The Golden Liquor shares the same path as Heaven and Earth’s creation, for the Spirit Chamber is the pivot of the elixir, and the sun’s essence and moon’s marrow are the true lead and mercury.

Yang ascends and yin descends, never straying from the directions of *zi* and *wu*; the sun departs and the moon arrives, necessarily dwelling in the positions of *mao* and *you*. The Yang Fire rises from *zi* and ascends to *si*, where yang reaches its peak and yin is born at *wu*. The Yin Talisman descends from *wu* to *hai*, where yin reaches its peak and yang is reborn at *zi*. Within the Spirit Chamber, a hollow space an inch in diameter and round in height unites the Three Powers—this is the central crucible of our being. Emerging from yin and entering yang generates true mercury; refining the Mysterious Essence within causes fire to arise from *kan* and water to descend from *li*.

Thus, *kan* and *li* take north and south as their warp, cinnabar and mercury being their alternate names. Only water and fire serve as the functions of *qian* and *kun*—these are the two functions of metal and earth. When it speaks of "three, five, and one," the essence of Heaven and Earth, following the Three-in-One yields its principle: the three and five guard one another, and metal and fire mutually circulate. Cinnabar is born within true lead, and metal-fire is true lead itself. Thus the scriptures say: "Lead is the essence of metal; water is the pivot of the Dao." The *Cantong Qi* likewise states: *Qian* and *kun* stand above and below, while *kan* and *li* ascend and descend between them. *Kan*’s *wu* is the moon’s essence; *li*’s *ji* is the sun’s light. The Five Phases serve as the warp, with *kan*’s male metal-essence and *li*’s female fire-light—both reside in the central palace.

Earth’s virtue arises from *zhen* and fills at *qian*, passing through *xun* to wane and reaching *kun* to be exhausted. Moreover, the mutual restraint of being and non-being: the white is metal’s essence, the black is water’s foundation. Metal is water’s mother, the mother concealing the child’s embryo; water is metal’s child, the child hidden in the mother’s womb. When metal returns to its original nature, it is water and metal restoring their nature. For cinnabar is the essence of wood, and when it obtains metal, they unite. Once metal and water merge, wood and fire take the lead. Metal and wood intertwine, water and earth pair to form the elixir.

As for the "medicinal substances": *Qian*’s yang generates *kun*, and *kun*’s yin envelops *qian*. *Qian*’s metal generates *kun*’s earth, symbolizing *qian*’s central line dwelling within *kun*—this is metal and water sharing the same origin. Symbolizing *kun*’s central line dwelling within *qian* is wood and fire sharing the same position. *Qian* and *kun* lodge their bodies within *kan* and *li*, and *kan* and *li* become the functions of *qian* and *kun*. Fire’s completion number is seven, returning to reside in the east’s *zhen* as the Seven Returns. Metal’s completion number is nine, returning to reside in the north’s *kan* as the Nine Reversions. Thus fire is refined by earth, and metal enters to become water, enclosing and flying, congealing and opening the numinous gate.

Yet the elixir must be formed by refining lead with fire. As for the "fire times": the movement of the Dipper’s pivot and the bell’s pitch are based on the numbers of nine and one, combining the numbers of two, six, three, and nine. The end necessarily returns to the beginning, embracing the origin and holding the true, sowing essence at *zi*. *Yin* and *shen* are the extremes of yin and yang. The method of counting the stalks divides the month into twelve hexagrams, each hexagram receiving two and a half days. Each uses its own hexagram’s lines to perform its own hexagram’s counting. From the *guan* hexagram of the eighth month to the *tai* hexagram of the first month, yang uses the young twenty-eight stalks, and yin uses the old twenty-four stalks. From the *dazhuang* hexagram of the fourth month to the *pi* hexagram of the seventh month, yang uses the old thirty-six stalks, and yin uses the young twenty-two stalks. Twelve hexagrams complete one month’s work; twelve months complete one year’s cycle. Repeating and circulating, there is no excess or deficiency.

This aligns with Master Shao’s *Xiantian Tu*, both following Fu Xi’s arrangement of the *Yi*’s images and positions. Thus the marvel of the Heavenly Root and Lunar Grotto: yang begins at *fu* and culminates at *qian*; yin arises at *gou* and culminates at *kun*. Cycling through the sixty-four hexagrams, there is not the slightest deviation. This is how it smelts and nurtures the ten thousand kinds, beginning and ending without form, merging with the Great Thoroughfare, and blending into obscurity.

Therefore, it is profoundly vast and cannot be considered external; minutely divided, it cannot be considered internal. Yet it does not transgress the Dao.

The theory of *na jia* assigns fixed positions: *qian* governs *jia* and *ren*, *kun* governs *yi* and *gui*, *zhen* governs *geng*, *xun* governs *xin*, *gen* governs *bing*, and *dui* governs *ding*. Yet *kan* governs *wu* and *li* governs *ji*, which have no fixed positions. Moreover, the yin and yang of the six hexagrams are but the circulating ascent and descent of the central lines of *kan* and *li*. As Master Zhu observed: these are but provisional devices to embody the rhythms of advance and retreat—well said. Thus the composition of the *Huoji* has its basis.

Later generations, misled by metaphors of cinnabar and mercury, divided the art into "inner" and "outer" elixirs, each claiming textual authority to dazzle the world. Yet how could one wholly rely on herbs, minerals, or metals to achieve the mechanism of divine transformation? As the *Cantong Qi* states: "Borrowing the names of stones, cloaking itself in borrowed texts." Though rarely discussed by the masters, it concerns nothing other than the cultivation of *xing* (nature), *ming* (life), *shen* (spirit), and *xuan* (mystery). To exhaust the supreme principles of yin and yang, to seize the divine power of creation—is this not the culmination of the elixir path?

The metaphors of the elixir are but borrowed images—what need is there for terms like furnace and cauldron, fire and medicine, lead and mercury, dragon and tiger, infant and maiden, female and male? Yet texts like the *Guanyin* speak of "infant and alchemical maiden," "golden tower and crimson palace," "azure dragon and white tiger," "precious tripod and red furnace," and "clay idols"—such language did not exist in the time of Laozi. Some call these apocryphal works. Those who cling to images and obscure texts, abandoning the source to chase the stream, indulging in the bizarre and fantastical to impress the world, utter only the words of charlatans and eccentrics—men like Shaojun, Ledao, Wencheng, Wuli, and Gongsun. Works like the *Baopuzi*’s alchemical transformations, which rely on heterodox methods to deceive the masses and seek fleeting fortune, inevitably lead to ruin through reckless falsehoods.

Alas! Those who speak well of immortality say only: "See nothing, hear nothing, embrace the spirit in stillness." Thus one forgets the form to nurture *qi*, forgets *qi* to nurture the spirit, and forgets the spirit to nurture emptiness. He who holds to the Dao attains complete virtue; with complete virtue, the form is whole; with the form whole, *qi* is whole—I cannot alter this. When emptiness reaches its extreme, numinous clarity arises; with numinous clarity, divine transformation occurs, and one becomes a companion of Heaven, roaming the origins of things.

The light and pure *qi* ascends, and the essence of utmost yang rises with it. Some say: "The sage does not follow the path of immortals"—is this not a slander against the world? To seek the Dao, one must only guard the center. If asked: "Is guarding the center not sufficient for the elixir? Why then are teachings so varied, with countless methods and divergent paths? Can guarding the center alone suffice, or must one rely on external techniques?" The answer is: Is the Dao divided in two?

Heaven is the accumulation of *qi*; the Supreme Lord is the sovereign of Heaven. From the undifferentiated state before the separation of the Hongyuan, the Three *Xuan* gave rise to existence—these are Fan Qingjing and Xuan Yuanshi. The wondrously non-existent is the origin of *xing*; the wondrously existent is the origin of *xuan*. From the Three *Xuan* arise the Nine *Xuan*—these are the Nine Heavens. The realm of emptiness is boundless; the light and pure *qi* has no form, and the governing spirit has no fixed abode. Yet because the governed have their dependencies, scriptures and records descend to instruct, talismans and methods unfold to elucidate, and thus the teachings are established.

Among the most prominent transmitters are the Han Celestial Master, the Mao Perfected Lords, Xu Jingyang, Ge the Immortal Elder, and Qiu the Perfected Lord. The Three Caverns and Four Supplements, along with the schools of Qingwei, Lingbao, Shenxiao, and Deng-Yue, are the categories of the Caverns and Supplements—these are the scriptures and records. Qingwei originated with Yuanshi and was expounded by Zhenyuan; later, Wei of Nanyue and Zu of Lingjun carried on the lineage of the four branches. Those who propagated its teachings include Zhu Dongyuan, Li Shaowei, Nan Bidao, Huang Leiyuan, and Li Xuji, followed by Zhang, Xu, Ye, and Xiong, whose influence spread further.

Lingbao began with Yuchen, based on the *Duren Jing* and its methods, and was expounded by the Three Perfected Ones of Xuanyi. Later, Xu of Taiji, Zheng of Zhuyang, and Lu of Jianji promoted its teachings. Those who propagated it include Tian Zijin, Yu Dongwei, Du Dazhen, Xiang Deqian, Wang Qingjian, Jin Yunzhong, Gao Ziyuan, Du Guangting, Kou Qianzhi, and Liu Chongjing, followed by Zhao, Lin, Bai, and Chen, whose influence likewise spread—though distinctions arose between the Donghua and Nanchang lineages.

Shenxiao encompasses the various Thunder lineages, originating with the Perfected King of Yuqing and expounded by the Fire Master, Perfected Lord Wang. Later, the Lords of Shichen, Xujing, and Xihe, along with Li the Demon-Queller and Xu the Pivot, promoted its teachings. Those who propagated it include Lin Lingsu, Xu Shenweng, Liu Hunkang, Lei Mo’an, Wan Wulei, Fang Pinle, Deng Tieya, followed by Shangguan, Xu, Tan, Yang, Chen, Tang, and Mo, whose influence also spread.

Deng-Yue traces back to Zhu Ximing, Zheng Zhiwei, Lu Yanghao, and Ye Fashan, with those who propagated its teachings including Zuo, Zheng, Pan, and Li, whose lineages flourished further. Yet in essence, none depart from the wonders of the Three *Xuan* and Five Phases.

The scripture says: "The Three, Five, and One are the essence of Heaven and Earth"—these are the numbers of the *Hetu*’s Three and Five. Thus thunder and lightning combine the numbers of nine and one: yang is thunder, yin is lightning; frost arises from lightning, killing from thunder; the pivot is yin, the mechanism is yang; thunder is benevolent, lightning is malevolent—all are concealed within the Supreme Ultimate. Seeking this within the body, the shoulders are the Thunder Enclosure, the Five *Xuan* are the Five Thunders. The heart is the numinous palace, the sovereign of the five senses.

To merge one’s own numinous essence with the wonders of the Three *Xuan* and Five Phases is to illumine the obscure and shatter the dark—this is refining utmost yin with utmost yang, illuminating utmost darkness with utmost brightness. The Southern Dipper steeps the *hun*-soul, its number the six of water. The Northern Dipper forges the *po*-soul, its number the seven of fire. Through the work of refining oneself, one refines the *hun*, gathering its scattered and benighted qi, transcending its sunk and clinging thoughts, and transforms it into the realm of yang brightness, thereby escaping the realm of yin gloom.

To command the opening and closing of yin and yang is to conjoin the essence of the Two and Five, to condense the qi of the Nine and One. Cultivated within, it is called the *elixir*; deployed without, it is called the *method*. By stirring the Five *Xuan* to excoriate and produce sound, the functions of rain and sunshine are perfected in the space of a single breath. By employing one’s own spirit to command the spirits of others, in the midst of stillness, this evokes and that responds. Thus are the deadly mechanisms of Heaven and Earth activated, the transformative controls of yin and yang conjoined—all through the power of one’s own numinous essence merging with them in virtue and penetrating the divine.

To drive out and exorcise malevolent spirits, to ward off and dispel calamities and pestilence, are but minor matters. Moreover, the ancient institutions of invoking Heaven and offering sacrifices, which later devolved into the ornate rituals of *tao* and *ci*, have existed since the time of Emperor Wu of Han. As the saying goes: "Offerings to the Supreme Emperor, fasting and bathing in purification, to sacrifice to the Supreme Emperor—all are means to express one’s sincerity." Though the offerings be as humble as mugwort and reeds from the valleys, they may yet serve, for they embody sincerity. Thus it is said: "Ghosts and spirits have no constant sustenance; they are sustained by those who attain sincerity. Millet and glutinous rice are not inherently fragrant; it is bright virtue that is fragrant." Strive to cultivate the resonance of virtue, that it may be offered to ghosts and spirits.

If sincerity wanes and virtue is lacking, relying merely on external adornments—embroidered silks, admired scriptures, embellished brocades, intricate patterns, lavish feasts with crossed platters, striving to dazzle the eyes and ears to impress the world and astonish the vulgar—what then is the true purpose of such affairs? Can it truly be about bells, drums, jades, and silks? The symbolic representations of the Dao are all modeled after the celestial and terrestrial deities, their radiance displayed and arrayed in order. These are the established norms, rituals, and canons, all expressions of reverence and utmost sincerity, enabling those who gaze upon and partake in the rites to be thus guided.

Yet later generations have acted otherwise, chasing only fame and profit, prizing only ornate embellishments, all abandoning the root to pursue the branch, forsaking the real to compete in the false. How do they differ from the greedy and flattering shamans, scheming only for mouth and body, for goods and wealth? Moreover, they exalt their esoteric chambers, differentiate their paths, and mystify their mechanisms to delude the masses and incite their kind. Those who inherit these ways become habituated to such cliques, not probing within but only dazzled by externals. How far have they strayed from the Dao! What hope is there for any resonance or communion?

Alas! Though the transmissions may differ, they converge on the same path—there is no second Dao. Those who worthily inherit it must broadly study and guard its essentials, thereby resolving doubts and dispelling delusions. It is not merely about boasting of numerous fasts or extraordinary practices. Does the method not say: "Within the real lies the divine; beyond sincerity, there is no method"? From this perspective, does it not align with the incantations and revealed secrets?

Moreover, the ancients spoke of the virtue of the master: clad in grass, fed by trees, starving their bodies, tempering their minds and spirits, purifying their qi and thoughts, so that not a shred of worldly desire or emotional filth could lodge within. Thus they regulated their externals, merging the work of Heaven and humanity into one. The divine and luminous dwelled with them; their hearts united with Heaven, so that their hearts were Heaven. Therefore, to unite Heaven with Heaven—there can be no "that Heaven" and "this non-Heaven," no "that mystery" and "this non-mystery." The mechanism of resonance and response is thus singular—how could there be any interval in its issuance?

This is the Way of pure simplicity: only the divine is to be guarded. Guard it without losing it, and unite with the divine. The essence of this unity penetrates, harmonizing with the heavenly order—is this not fitting? To abandon this and wallow in worldly filth, to be swept away by clamor and ostentation, to be blinded by gaudy splendor, and to take such as teachers and friends—like ants drawn to sweetness or bees swarming, proclaiming daily, "I am an immortal! I am numinous!"—is either perverse or delusional. How could this be what Zhuangzi meant by the *Great Master*?

Alas! What the scriptures describe—without Peng and Wei, it would have nearly sunk into the realm of talismans and staves. What the methods conceal—without Wang and Bai, it would have flowed into the domain of shamans. Yet to govern Heaven and Earth, to store the myriad things, to harmonize with ghosts and spirits in fortune and misfortune—still, one must expand upon these words and seek within them. This is what is meant by "conveying the mandate and penetrating the mysterious," which may be silently comprehended.

**Explication of the Diagram of the Supreme Ultimate**
The Supreme Ultimate is the complete embodiment of the Dao. Undifferentiated and without bias, vast and without form or semblance, it is the root of nature and destiny. Nature is endowed by destiny; principle inheres in nature; the heart governs them—this is called the Dao. The body of the Dao is called the *Ultimate of Five*, residing at the center of the Nine Categories—this is the *August Ultimate*. The *Book of Changes* says, "Converge upon what has ultimate"; the *Book of Songs* says, "None are not your ultimate." Seeking it thus, it is the heart, the Dao, the center.

Master Zhou said: "In the center, it rests." Master Cheng said: "The Supreme Ultimate is the Dao." Master Shao said: "The heart is the Supreme Ultimate." Master Zhu said: "The Supreme Ultimate is principle." Master Lu said: "The center is the great root of the world—this is the Ultimate." Principle is one; when spoken of together, it is the Dao.

The Five Phases and yin-yang—yin-yang is the Supreme Ultimate. The Five are distinct, yet the Two are real; the Two and the One are fundamentally one. The Two are real: Heaven, with yang, generates the myriad things; Earth, with yin, completes the myriad things. In movement, yang; in stillness, yin. Yang transforms and yin conjoins, giving rise to the Five Qi, and from the Five Qi, the myriad things are born—hence it is said the Five are distinct. The Five are distinct yet rooted in yin and yang, which are mutually their root.

The Two Modes arise from yang, interact with yin; yin interacts with yang, giving rise to the Four Images. The Four Images divide, producing the Eight Trigrams; the Eight Trigrams intermingle, and the myriad things are born. This is called "one movement, one stillness"—is it not the utmost subtlety of Heaven and Earth?

Thus, the Five Qi spread, the Four Seasons proceed, and the myriad things are born in ceaseless generation. The Five Phases are but one yin-yang; yin-yang is but one Supreme Ultimate. When the Supreme Ultimate scatters to become the myriad things, each thing possesses its own nature, each contains a single Supreme Ultimate in its wholeness, with stillness ever as its master.

Embracing both being and non-being, preserving substance and function, containing movement and stillness, it is the source of all transformations and the root of all existence—wondrously uniting the essence of the Two and the Five. Master Zhu said: the Supreme Ultimate is principle; movement and stillness are qi. The Supreme Ultimate is the inherent subtlety; movement and stillness are the mechanism it rides. When the mechanism moves, qi flows and yin-yang operate—how could principle not be manifest? For qi bears principle in generation, and principle takes form through qi; nature presides as master, while yin-yang and the Five Phases interweave in warp and woof.

Spoken of as one, the myriad things share a single Supreme Ultimate in their totality. Spoken of separately, each thing possesses its own Supreme Ultimate. Moreover, in the primordial chaos of the Great Vastness, primordial qi was the root of the myriad things. Its essence is called principle; its ceaseless flowing of yin and yang is called qi. Thus, before division, the Dao is the Supreme Ultimate; after formation, all things possess this principle. The mind is the Supreme Ultimate—profound and without trace, yet all principles are complete within it. Once yin and yang take form, principle and qi become distinct.

When the Supreme Ultimate divides, it first generates one odd and one even; from odd and even, generation proceeds without end. Thus, one divides into two, two into four, four into eight, eight into sixteen, sixteen into thirty-two, and thirty-two into sixty-four. The sages had no means to express this until Fuxi first drew the lines: a single unbroken line to represent Qian, a broken line to represent Kun—embodying the Supreme Ultimate of our minds. One odd and one even symbolize change; layered, they form hexagrams; divided, they become lines—all the most evident and luminous subtleties of yin and yang.

This drawing is most vast, most profound, most refined, most subtle—neither visible in the semblance of qi and form, nor attainable through sound, color, shape, or appearance. Radiant and flourishing, empty yet numinous, bright yet wondrous, it scatters into the ten thousand differences and gathers into one root. Without the slightest lapse of time or hair’s-breadth of divergence, it cycles without end, boundless and inexhaustible. Like the revolutions of Heaven and Earth, the moisture of wind and rain, the might of thunder, the solemnity of frost and snow, the flow and towering of mountains and rivers, the flourishing and withering of plants and trees, the subtlety of flying and burrowing creatures, the multitude of moving and growing things—all do not transgress the flowing of Heaven’s mandate, sharing the same endowment.

What is called "having an ultimate" refers to principle; "having no ultimate" refers to form. Yet the utmost extremity of principle is fundamentally formless. If one were to speak of it as "non-being," it could not serve as the root of the myriad transformations. When Master Shao spoke of "the Ultimateless" and "having images," "having" refers to its true essence as root, while "non-being" is its formless, odorless, and transcendent nature. Those who perceive the body of the Dao surely cannot add "non-being" to "being."

As for Laozi’s "Ultimateless," it means formless and boundless; Zhuangzi’s "Dao exists prior to the Supreme Ultimate" is of the same sense. As for the numbers of the Yellow River and Luo River charts and the images of the Earlier Heaven, though advance and retreat, fullness and emptiness, growth and decline operate within them, all take emptiness at the center as their ultimate. By emptying the center, the inherent subtlety of the Supreme Ultimate is attained—what difference then remains between obscurity and clarity, communication and obstruction?

Thus, the *Book of Changes* states: "The learning of the mind encompasses all affairs; all transformations originate in the mind." What the mind possesses is the supreme principle that does not transgress Heaven, Earth, and the myriad things. Master Cheng said: "There is principle, then there are images; there are images, then there are numbers." The Way of humanity begins in yang and is completed in yin, rooted in stillness and flowing into movement—just as with the myriad things. Yet yang returns to its root in yin, stillness returns to its root in movement; in every movement and stillness, all flow together with Heaven and Earth. Only by mastering stillness is nature established; when nature is established, centrality and uprightness are achieved, and benevolence and righteousness are settled.

Thus, substance and function are one, source and manifestation are without distinction. This diagram, Master Zhu said, was received by Zhou Dunyi from Mu Bochang, who received it from Zhong Fang, who received it from Chen Tuan. Since Chen Tuan studied the teachings of Laozi, Master Lu criticized Master Zhu for deriving the Ultimateless from Laozi, while the *Book of Changes* speaks only of "having an ultimate," and Zhou Dunyi’s *Comprehensive Discourse* mentions only yin-yang and the Supreme Ultimate—this is clear. Yet Master Zhu’s gloss of "formless" does not deviate from the Dao.

Moreover, in Pan’s record, it is held without doubt that Zhou Dunyi composed it himself. Some also say that Zhou Dunyi, Hu Su, and Shao Gu studied together under a Buddhist monk in Runzhou and received it from him. Yet how could such a teaching be known to a Buddhist monk? Furthermore, earlier scholars regarded the learning of Zhou and Shao—the *Earlier Heaven* and *Supreme Ultimate* diagrams—as sharing one principle, their transmission not necessarily separate. Its essence is most great, encompassing all; its function is most divine, pervading all.

Thus it is said: from the hidden and manifest of Heaven and Earth to the insects and plants, nothing is too minute to accord with it. It is to follow the principles of nature and destiny, to exhaust the Way of transformation. The hearts of the sages of all ages are the same—this is not attained by seeking afar, but by returning to what is within oneself. Whether distant as the six directions or near as one’s own body, fleeting as a blink of the eye or subtle as movement and stillness—how could mere words, speech, or hearing suffice to know Heaven? One must devote oneself to the work of convergence and the subtleties of inquiry, so that the luminous and tranquil emptiness of one’s spirit fills the six directions and governs the myriad changes. With time, sincerity becomes refined, hence clarity; spirit responds, hence wonder; subtlety, hence profundity.

The establishment of images and the depiction of ideas herein dissect and analyze the subtlest truths, leaving nothing unaddressed. The Way of nature and destiny, the discourse on life and death, the tracing of origins and the return to endings—all are fully encompassed within this.

To weigh noble rank as a mere fraction and regard gold and jade as dust—who could alter this, or dare to cast doubt? Let this resolve such questions.

*On the Primordial Diagram*
The *Primordial Diagram* (*Xiantian Tu*) was created by Fuxi. The sequence and positions of its trigrams and lines all derive from the original drawings, unlike the later arrangement of King Wen’s post-celestial order.

The *Book of Changes* states: "There is the Supreme Ultimate, which generates the Two Modes." The Two Modes generate the Four Images, and the Four Images generate the eight trigrams. Here, yang ascends to intersect with yin, and yin descends to intersect with yang, producing the Four Images of Heaven. The firm intersects with the yielding, and the yielding intersects with the firm, producing the Four Images of Earth. The eight trigrams intermingle, and the myriad things are born therefrom.

Their numerical sequence is: Qian as one, Dui as two, Li as three, Zhen as four, Xun as five, Kan as six, Gen as seven, and Kun as eight. The Four Images intersect to form sixteen matters, and the eight trigrams intermingle to produce the sixty-four hexagrams—this is the imagery of the Primordial.

As Master Shao said: "One divides into two, two into four, four into eight." From Qian to Kun, all are trigrams of the unborn. If traced in reverse, they correspond to the four seasons. The *Commentary* states: "The *Changes* reckon in reverse"—this is what it means.

Their positions are: Qian in the south, Kun in the north, Li in the east, Kan in the west, Zhen in the northeast, Dui in the southeast, Xun in the southwest, and Gen in the northwest. From Zhen to Qian is the forward sequence; from Xun to Kun is the reverse. Yin is the mother of yang, and yang is the father of yin. The mother conceives the eldest son, giving rise to *Fu* (Return); the father begets the eldest daughter, giving rise to *Gou* (Decay). Thus, yang begins with *Fu*, and yin begins with *Tai* (Pregnancy).

The *Commentary* states: "Heaven and Earth take their positions; mountains and marshes exchange qi; thunder and wind press upon each other; water and fire do not oppose one another. The eight trigrams intermingle." The reckoning of what has passed follows the forward sequence, while the knowing of what is to come follows the reverse. The forward sequence, rotating left, consists of already-generated hexagrams; the reverse sequence, rotating right, consists of yet-to-be-generated hexagrams.

The sequence of the sixty-four hexagrams arises from the eight trigrams arranged in order and then doubled. Thus, the lower three lines correspond to the eight trigrams of the preceding diagram, while the upper three lines are each doubled in sequence, so that the lower trigram also extends into eight. If each line is generated in succession, it follows Master Shao’s principle: "Eight divides into sixteen, sixteen into thirty-two, thirty-two into sixty-four." All are modeled upon the wonders of natural patterns.

These are the four diagrams, known as the learning of the Primordial. Yang’s nature is round; when formed, it becomes square. Yin’s nature is square; when imaged, it becomes round. In the circular arrangement, Qian reaches its limit at noon, Kun at midnight, Li at dawn, and Kan at dusk. Yang is born at midnight and culminates at noon; yin is born at noon and culminates at midnight. Here, yang resides in the south, and yin in the north.

In the square arrangement, Qian begins in the northwest, and Kun ends in the southeast. Here, yang resides in the north, and yin in the south. This is the numerical interplay of yin and yang in opposition. From Kun’s expansion, passing through Gen and Dui, it culminates in Qian; from Qian’s contraction, passing through Xun and Gen, it culminates in Kun and Zhen. At the inception of yin’s intersection, yang is born—this is Zhen and Kun’s connection to Xun. At the inception of yang’s contraction, yin is born—this is Xun and Qian’s connection to the circular diagram.

The sequence of yin and yang’s waxing and waning is as follows: Zhen has one yang, Li and Dui have two yang, and Qian has three yang; Xun has one yin, Kan and Gen have two yin, and Kun has three yin—all are the principles of nature. Their numbers proceed from one to two, from two to four, from four to eight, forming the eight trigrams. The round exterior represents yang, while the square within represents yin. The round is dynamic and becomes Heaven; the square is still and becomes Earth. Though the images of square and circle differ, their arrangement of trigrams and sequences are the same across the four diagrams.

Thus, it is said that this began with Fuxi, not with Master Shao. Even if Master Shao had created it himself, he would have based it upon Fuxi’s work.

Moreover, the term "Primordial" refers to that which precedes Heaven, and Heaven does not oppose it—this is what King Wen and Confucius did not expound. Some say the Primordial is the *River Diagram*. The numbers of the *River Diagram* are: Heaven one, Earth two, Heaven three, Earth four, Heaven five, Earth six, Heaven seven, Earth eight, Heaven nine, Earth ten. The numbers of Heaven are five, and the numbers of Earth are five; the five positions interact, each having its counterpart. The numbers of Heaven total twenty-five, and the numbers of Earth total thirty—altogether, the numbers of Heaven and Earth are fifty-five, which the sages took as the basis for drawing the trigrams.

Some say the eight trigrams are the *River Diagram*—this is incorrect. Moreover, the sequence of the Primordial trigrams and the *River Diagram* are only half in accord. In the *River Diagram*, Qian and Kun are vertical, while the six children are horizontal—this is the origin of numbers. The Primordial cannot be spoken of in terms of numbers; its sequence and positions are all the generative numbers of the eight trigrams. Yang is one, and yin is two; thus, yang generates yin as two, and six becomes twelve. Yin generates yang as three, and ten becomes thirty. Therefore, Qian begins with one, and Dui becomes twelve; Li becomes twelve and thirty, totaling three hundred sixty; Zhen becomes twelve and four thousand three hundred twenty. From Xun to Kun, all are the generative numbers of odd and even. When refined into the sixty-four hexagrams, they are multiplied by their generated numbers to yield the total sum.

From midnight to noon is yang: the first four lines are yang, the two lines before the middle are both yin, the two lines after are both yang, the top line is yin, and the second line is yang.

From noon to midnight is yin: the first four lines are all yin; the two lines before the middle are yang; the two lines after are yin; the top line is yang; the second line is yin; the third line is yang; and the fourth line is yin.

In yang, the two lines at the middle and top first become yin and then yang, for yang is born of yin. In yin, the two lines at the middle and top first become yang and then yin, for yin is born of yang. That its sequence begins with Zhen and ends with Kun is because the waxing and waning of yin and yang constitute its numbers. This is what Master Cai called *Huangji Jingshi* ("Supreme Principles Governing the World"), all rooted in the Primordial Heaven. For all numbers arise from one, and the cyclic generative numbers of the sixty-four hexagrams—like the sun, moon, and stars; water, fire, earth, and stone; heat and cold; day and night; flying and walking creatures; grasses and trees—are allotted to the eight trigrams, yielding the numbers of ceaseless generation.

Thus, what responds and transforms in goodness are heat and cold, day and night, temperament and form, movement and flight, grasses and trees, colors and sounds, qi and flavors. What corresponds and transmutes in goodness are rain and wind, dew and thunder, movement and flight, grasses and trees, temperament and form, eyes and ears, nose and mouth—all the numbers of Primordial Heaven. In the governance of the world, Heaven possesses yin and yang: the Greater Yang, Greater Yin, Lesser Yang, and Lesser Yin. Earth possesses softness and hardness: the Lesser Hard, Lesser Soft, Greater Hard, and Greater Soft. These are the eight trigrams spoken of in the *Yijing*. Therefore, when yin and yang are exhausted, the four seasons are complete; when hardness and softness are exhausted, the four directions are established. The odd and even lines of Heaven and Earth, the numbers nine of yang and six of yin, all arise from the number four. This is what Master Zhu meant by viewing the myriad things as fourfold.

Thus, the categories of the sun, moon, and stars all emerge from the transformations of the eight trigrams. The changes of Heaven and Earth unfold through *yuan*, *hui*, *yun*, and *shi*; the changes of human affairs unfold through emperors, kings, hegemons, and lords. *Yuan*, *hui*, *yun*, and *shi* comprise spring, summer, autumn, and winter, corresponding to birth, growth, harvest, and storage. Emperors, kings, hegemons, and lords comprise the *Yijing*, *Book of Songs*, *Book of Documents*, and *Spring and Autumn Annals*, corresponding to virtue, the Dao, merit, and effort. Each mutually gives rise to the other, forming sixteen. Sixteen is the number of the four images mutually generating one another. All the transformations of Heaven and Earth, the responses of the myriad things, and the continuities and changes of past and present do not exceed these sixteen. With sixteen, the Dao of Heaven and Earth is complete. This is what Master Shao meant by the supreme subtlety of Heaven, Earth, and humanity lying in the interval between movement and stillness.

This is called the learning of the Primordial Heart; the learning of the Manifested Traces follows after. Thus, all diagrams begin from the center, for the myriad transformations and affairs are born of the heart. It is also said that the *Primordial Heaven Diagram* is the circle within. Though the diagram bears no text, my words throughout the day never depart from it. For the principles of Heaven, Earth, and the myriad things are all contained within it. Moreover, the circle represents the numbers of the *Hetu*; the square, the patterns of the *Luoshu*. The "juggling of pellets" speaks of the circular images of Primordial Heaven, all following the ebb and flow of yin and yang.

The winter solstice occupies the midpoint of *zi*, where yang begins in *Fu*. The "Moon Grotto" is where *Qian* meets *Xun*, that is, the yang from *Fu* to *Qian*. The "Heavenly Root" is where *Kun* meets *Zhen*, that is, the yin from *Gou* to *Kun*. Yang governs humanity; yin governs things. Thus, the five hexagrams from *Qian* to *Xun*—that is, *Gou*—are the "Moon Grotto"; the five hexagrams from *Kun* to *Zhen*—that is, *Fu*—are the "Heavenly Root." As for the thirty-six palaces, they accumulate the numbers from *Qian* one to *Kun* eight. The "Heavenly Root" corresponds to the winter solstice, midnight, what is called the heart of Heaven and Earth.

On the left, from the first line of *Zhen* is the winter solstice; the middle of *Li* and *Dui* is the spring equinox; the end of *Qian* is the summer solstice. On the right, from the first line of *Xun* is the summer solstice; the middle of *Kan* and *Gen* is the autumn equinox; reaching the end of *Kun* transitions to the winter solstice. This cycle is endless, for in one year, one month, one day, one hour, there is nowhere this principle does not reside. Its ceaseless circulation: from *Gou* to *Kun* is yin containing yang; from *Fu* to *Qian* is yang dividing yin. Between *Kun* and *Fu* lies the Wuji. From *Kun* returning to *Gou* is before the Wuji; from *Qian* connecting to *Fu* is after the appearance of images. *Qian* and *Kun* intersect to become *Tai*; *Kan* and *Li* intersect to become *Jiji*.

*Qian* is born at *zi*; *Kun* is born at *wu*; *Kan* ends at *yin*; *Li* ends at *shen*, corresponding to the celestial seasons. Yin and yang arise from the two sides; the void in the center is the Taiji. From the winter solstice to the summer solstice is forward movement; from the summer solstice to the winter solstice is reverse movement. Black and white circulate within the single ring, and all becomes spring.
