Master Zhu said: Among the twenty-eight hexagrams of the *Yijing*, when inverted they form fifty-six hexagrams, except for *Qian*, *Kun*, *Kan*, *Li*, *Daguo*, *Yi*, *Xiaoguo*, and *Zhongfu*, which when inverted remain their original hexagrams. Combining the twenty-eight hexagrams with these eight yields the thirty-six palaces. These are all the marvels of the "Heavenly Root" and "Moon Grotto," the comings and goings of their endless cycle.

This *Primordial Heaven* is the original diagram of Fuxi. Though it requires no words, its scope is vast, for every character and meaning in the *Yijing* emerges from it. In terms of its structure, the *Taiji* is not as grand or detailed as *Primordial Heaven*; in terms of its principles, *Primordial Heaven* is not as refined or concise as the *Taiji*. Yet the *Taiji* ultimately lies within the bounds of *Primordial Heaven*, for the principles of things are the same, and images and numbers are without duality.

From the first undivided line to the completion of six lines is what is called the learning of Primordial Heaven. After the hexagrams are formed, each is expounded according to its particular meaning—this is the learning of Manifested Traces. What Master Shao called *Primordial Heaven* is the *Yijing* drawn by Fuxi; *Manifested Traces* is the *Yijing* expounded by King Wen.

Fuxi’s *Yijing* originally had no text—only a single diagram to embody its images and numbers, within which the principles of Heaven and Earth and the myriad things, the transformations of yin and yang from beginning to end, were all contained. The sequence of King Wen’s eight trigrams—*Li* to the south, *Kan* to the north, *Zhen* to the east, *Dui* to the west, *Qian* to the southwest, *Kun* to the northwest, *Gen* to the southeast, and *Xun* to the northeast—represents their positions in application, the learning of Manifested Traces.

Though Confucius composed the *Ten Wings* based on King Wen’s *Yijing*, within them—such as the arrangement of the eight trigrams, their doubling to form hexagrams; the *Taiji*, the Two Modes, the Four Images, the Eight Trigrams; and the categories of Heaven, Earth, Mountain, Marsh, Thunder, Wind, Water, and Fire—all derive from Fuxi’s original trigrams, not taking King Wen’s expounded *Yijing* as identical to Fuxi’s initial drawings. Some say this diagram was transmitted from Chen Tuan to Mu Bochang, from Mu to Li Tingzhi, and from Li to Shao Yong, thus sharing its origins with the alchemical Dao.

From *Fu* to *Qian* is yang; from *Gou* to *Kun* is yin. *Qian* and *Kun* establish the positions of above and below, while *Li* and *Kan* arrange the gates of left and right—where Heaven and Earth open and close, where the sun and moon enter and exit. After the winter solstice comes exhalation; after the summer solstice, inhalation—this is the yearly breath of Heaven and Earth. The month of the winter solstice follows the path of the summer solstice’s sun; the month of the summer solstice follows the path of the winter solstice’s sun. From spring to summer, from *Zi* to *Chen-Si*; from autumn to winter, from *Wu* to *Xu-Hai*. The four cardinal trigrams are *Qian*, *Kun*, *Kan*, and *Li*. As the *Cantong Qi* states: "The four trigrams of the female and male serve as the bolt and key." The numbers of *Kan* and *Li* are one and two, forming the warp of the north-south axis. *Kan* and *Li* are the true Water and Fire, the masters of the six trigrams, while the six trigrams serve *Kan* and *Li*.

The two breaths of yin and yang both originate and conclude at *Zi* and *Wu*. *Kan* conceals the six *Wu* stems as the essence of the moon, residing in the north, symbolizing the White Tiger born of water within metal. *Li* conceals the six *Ji* stems as the radiance of the sun, residing in the south, symbolizing the Vermilion Dragon born of fire within mercury. Beginning with *Zhen* and rising to *Qian*, it reaches fullness; passing through *Xun*, it wanes, and with *Kun*, it is exhausted. The middle line of *Qian* resides within *Kun*, where metal and water share the same origin. The middle line of *Kun* resides within *Qian*, where wood and fire share the same position.

*Qian* gives birth to three daughters, dwelling in the east: above *Xun*, middle *Li*, below *Dui*—two yang producing one yin. *Kun* gives birth to three sons, dwelling in the west: above *Zhen*, middle *Kan*, below *Gen*—two yin producing one yang. The *Kan* embryo resides at *Wu*; the *Li* embryo resides at *Zi*. The west borrows the origin of metal; the east borrows the ancestor of wood. *Qian* retreats to dwell in *Kan*; *Kun* retreats to dwell in *Li*.

In the method of *Na Jia*: *Qian* corresponds to the full moon, *Kun* to the new moon, while *Kan* and *Li* ascend and descend at the center. Thus, *Qian* embraces *Jia* and *Ren*; *Kun* embraces *Yi* and *Gui*; *Li* embraces *Ji*; *Kan* embraces *Wu*; *Xun* embraces *Xin*; *Zhen* embraces *Geng*; *Dui* embraces *Ding*; and *Gen* embraces *Bing*—all in perfect accord.

When *Kun* first transforms into *Zhen*, it is the birth of light, the moon emerging at *Geng*. When it transforms again into *Dui*, it is the first quarter, the moon emerging at *Ding*. When *Qian* first transforms into *Xun*, it is the birth of the waning phase, the moon appearing at *Xin*. When it diminishes further into *Gen*, it is the last quarter, its day reaching darkness. At the new moon, *Zhen* receives the tally, and the *Fu* hexagram begins its cycle. *Zhen* receives *Geng* from the west, its image like the *Zhen* trigram embracing *Geng*. On the eighth day, the *Dui* trigram embraces *Ding*. On the fifteenth, the *Qian* body is pure yang, and the *Qian* trigram embraces *Jia*. On the sixteenth, the *Xun* trigram embraces *Xin*. On the twenty-third, the *Gen* trigram embraces *Bing*. On the thirtieth, the *Kun* trigram embraces *Yi*. Thus, *Ren* and *Gui* pair with *Jia* and *Yi*, while *Qian* and *Kun* encompass the beginning and end—this aligns with the firing times of internal alchemy.

Zhu Xi, however, treated the *Primordial Heaven* eight trigrams as a single sequence, disregarding the order of lunar phases, yet still aligning them with *Na Jia*. He considered the positioning of Heaven and Earth, the mutual resonance of *Pi* and *Tai*, as correct. Thus, he suggested that Wei Boyang’s *Cantong Qi* might trace its lineage to Chen Tuan’s teachings. Indeed, the arrangement of trigrams in both texts silently accords. Yang begins with *Fu* and culminates in *Qian*; yin begins with *Gou* and culminates in *Kun*. Within a year’s twelve months, a month’s thirty days, or a day’s twelve hours, each completes a cycle of the heavens. The sixty-four hexagrams and three hundred eighty-four lines form the numbers of one celestial revolution, with each line governing a single day—all in precise accord with the *Yijing*.

The world assumes the *Cantong Qi* imitates the *Yijing*, yet who knows that the wonders of yin and yang, without seeking alignment, naturally align? Some claim the *Primordial Heaven* and *Taiji* diagrams emerged simultaneously, yet Zhou Dunyi and Shao Yong never communicated—thus, the two diagrams do not interconnect. Yet whether vast as the beginning and end of Heaven and Earth, or minute as the life and death of beings, or distant as the changes of ages past and present, all lie within the principles of waxing and waning, fullness and emptiness. Is this Shao Yong’s unique insight? From the nebulous and swirling origins of transformation and cyclical return—what Zhu Xi called the "root source beyond"—the earlier Confucians had lost its transmission, while those outside the orthodox lineage secretly passed it down as the art of the elixir. Only with Chen Tuan and Shao Yong did it return to the *Yijing*, and its teachings became clear and undeniably true. How could their reverence for this diagram be a small contribution?

Yet this trust is not misplaced. If Yang Xiong’s *Tai Xuan* modeled itself on the *Yijing*, dividing into regions, states, and households, all derived from the number three; Guan Ziming’s *Dong Ji* arranged twenty-seven images; and Sima Guang’s *Qian Xu* employed the number five with fifty-five lines—each sought to emulate the *Yijing* in their own way.

All these works merely patch together and complete books, yet none grasp the fullness of the Dao. Those who skillfully probe the traces of yin and yang and the mechanisms of creation and transformation—when they observe Heaven and Earth from the perspective of the Supreme Ultimate, then Heaven and Earth, too, are but things. Thus we know that Heaven and Earth arise from the *Yijing*, and the *Yijing* does not originate from Heaven and Earth. If one can fully realize the subtleties of the Supreme Ultimate and the functions of the Prior Heaven, then one may encompass Heaven and Earth, mold and complete the myriad things, and hold creation within oneself. In that case, even the Prior Heaven’s One Qi would cease to exist—how much less could mere words convey it?

The *River Diagram* originally stems from the *Yijing*, which states: "The River brings forth the Diagram, the Luo brings forth the Writing; the sages took them as their model." The *Xici* likewise says: "Heaven One, Earth Two; Heaven Three, Earth Four; Heaven Five, Earth Six; Heaven Seven, Earth Eight; Heaven Nine, Earth Ten. The heavenly numbers are five, the earthly numbers are five; the five positions interact and each has its pairing. The heavenly numbers total twenty-five, the earthly numbers thirty—altogether, the numbers of Heaven and Earth are fifty-five." This is how Confucius, using the numbers of the *River Diagram*, explained the workings of transformation and the movements of ghosts and spirits. Specifically, it refers to the numbers by which Heaven One generates Water and Earth Six completes it; Earth Two generates Fire and Heaven Seven completes it; Heaven Three generates Wood and Earth Eight completes it; Earth Four generates Metal and Heaven Nine completes it; Heaven Five generates Earth and Earth Ten completes it.

The *Gu Ming* states: "The *River Diagram* is kept in the eastern chamber." The *River Diagram* and the Eight Trigrams: when Fuxi ruled the world, a dragon-horse emerged from the River, and he took its markings as his model to draw the Eight Trigrams. The *Record of Rites* says: "The River brought forth the Horse Diagram; the dragon-horse bore the Diagram and emerged." The *Analects* says: "The River does not bring forth the Diagram." Confucius explained: "The *River Diagram* is the Eight Trigrams." Thus it is clear that Fuxi took the Diagram as his model to draw the *Yijing*. How could this be dismissed as false?

This Diagram emerged before the formation of written records, embodying the natural principles of Heaven and Earth. It encompasses the boundless subtleties of creation and transformation and is the source of the images and numbers of all things and affairs. Zheng Kangcheng said: "The River flows to connect with Qian, issuing the Heavenly Embrace; the Luo flows to connect with Kun, spouting the Earthly Talisman. The *River Diagram* has nine sections, the *Luo Writing* six." Liu Xin said: "When Fuxi succeeded Heaven to rule, he received the *River Diagram* and took it as his model to draw the Eight Trigrams." The *River Diagram* and *Luo Writing* serve as warp and weft; the Eight Trigrams and Nine Palaces serve as outer and inner. Wang Su said: "The *River Diagram* is the Eight Trigrams." Wang Chong also said: "The *River Diagram* emerged from the River’s waters—it is the trigrams of the *Yijing*." Guan Ziming said: "In the *River Diagram*, seven is in front, six behind, eight to the left, nine to the right." Liu Mu said: "The *River Diagram* totals forty-five, arranging the Four Images without mentioning the Five Phases." The *Da Dai Li Ji* says: "Two, nine, four; seven, five, three; six, one, eight." Tradition holds: "Nine treasures, modeled on the markings of a tortoise." It also says: "These are writings from before the Qin." Ouyang Xiu said: "The Diagram is the markings of the Eight Trigrams. A divine horse bore it from the River to present to Fuxi. The Eight Trigrams were not made by man but were bestowed by Heaven." Thus, the Eight Trigrams were created by man, while the *River Diagram* was not involved. As for the *River Diagram*, it is indeed said to have been born from a divine horse bearing the Eight Trigrams, emerging from the waters—these are the natural markings of Heaven and Earth. Why must it be attributed to Fuxi’s creation?

Wang Anshi said: "The Diagram reveals the Dao of Heaven; the Writing reveals the Dao of Man." That which connects with Heaven is expressed through images; that which centers on Earth is expressed through methods. Cai Shen said: "From Kong Anguo, Liu Xiang and his son, to Ban Gu, all held that the *River Diagram* was bestowed upon Fuxi and the *Luo Writing* upon Yu. Guan Ziming and Shao Kangjie both regarded ten as the *River Diagram* and nine as the *Luo Writing*. Only Liu Mu’s opinion differed, taking nine as the *River Diagram* and ten as the *Luo Writing*, claiming it originated with Chen Tuan—contradicting the views of other scholars." He also said: "Fuxi merely relied on the *River Diagram* to create the *Yijing*; he did not need to foresee the *Luo Writing* to have it harmonize with it. Yu merely relied on the *Luo Writing* to create the *Hong Fan*; he did not need to trace back to the *River Diagram* to have it correspond with it."

The numbers of the *River Diagram* are even; evenness is quiescent, and quiescence employs movement as its function. Thus, the movements of the *River Diagram* all pair with odd numbers: one pairs with six, two with seven, three with eight, four with nine, five with ten. The *River Diagram* is round in form but square in function; the sages used it to draw the trigrams. Master Cheng said: "The sages saw the *River Diagram* and *Luo Writing* and drew the Eight Trigrams. The ancient sages merely selected the most evident of divine things. Even if they had not seen the *River Diagram* and *Luo Writing*, they still would have created the Eight Trigrams." Within the *River Diagram* and *Luo Writing*, the central number is five. Extending it to exhaust its numbers, reaching ten, then combines to fifty. The *River Diagram* accumulates to fifty-five; its fifty are all derived from five, yet five alone is the source of the fifty and has no source itself. Thus, if five is left empty, it becomes merely fifty. Furthermore, in fifty-five, five is the center, and the ten are divided into the numbers of old and young yin and yang. The fifty, having no function, are then multiplied—five by ten and ten by five—both yielding fifty.

Master Shao said: "The round is the stars; the numbers of calendrical reckoning find their omen here. The square is the earth; the methods of dividing lands and arranging wells imitate this." The round represents the numbers of the *River Diagram*; the square, the markings of the *Luo Writing*. Thus, Fuxi and King Wen used them together to create the *Yijing*, while Yu and Ji arranged them to compose the *Hong Fan*. Master Zhu said: "The *River Diagram* aligns with the *Yijing*’s 'Heaven One to Earth Ten' and contains the fifty-five numbers of Heaven and Earth—all are the source from which the *Yijing* emerges."

Between Heaven and Earth, the qi of yin and yang, though each has its form, originally bore no numbers. Only with the advent of the *River Diagram* and *Luo Writing* did the fifty-five numbers emerge—odd and even, generation and completion—suddenly manifest and visible.

In the positions of the *River Diagram*, One and Six share a lineage and dwell in the north; Two and Seven form a fellowship and reside in the south; Three and Eight follow the same path and occupy the east; Four and Nine are companions and stand in the west; Five and Ten keep watch together in the center. This is nothing more than one yin and one yang, one odd and one even, doubling the Five Phases. The *River Diagram* takes the five generative numbers as its foundation, governing the five completion numbers, and places them together in their respective directions—revealing its entirety to humanity and demonstrating the constant essence of its numbers. The *River Diagram* prioritizes the generative numbers, while the ten numbers derive their value from the five, attaching themselves to the generative numbers. The sum of five odd numbers is twenty-five; the sum of five even numbers is thirty. Together, these two make fifty-five.

The *River Diagram* embodies wholeness, thus culminating in ten, with the positions of odd and even balanced. The void of Five and Ten in the *River Diagram* is the Supreme Ultimate; the twenty odd numbers and twenty even numbers are the Two Modes. That One, Two, Three, Four correspond to Six, Seven, Eight, Nine represents the Four Images.

Wu Cheng said: "The *River Diagram* appeared before Fuxi drew the trigrams, when a dragon-horse emerged from the River, its back bearing these numbers. Its numbers: behind, One and Six; ahead, Two and Seven; left, Three and Eight; right, Four and Nine; center, Five and Ten—five odd and five even numbers paired together. Fuxi observed Heaven and Earth, humans and all things, finding nothing that was not the pairing of yang (odd) and yin (even). Seeing the numbers of the *River Diagram*, he was struck with insight. Thus, he drew one unbroken line to represent yang and one broken line to represent yin. Adding and doubling these, he formed the eight trigrams, then doubled and added again to create the sixty-four hexagrams. This is what is meant by Fuxi drawing the eight trigrams based on the *River Diagram*."

The numbers of the *River Diagram* also occupy five positions, aligning with the circular arrangement of the five celestial palaces of the stars: One and Six in the north, Two and Seven in the south, Three and Eight in the east, Four and Nine in the west, Five and Ten in the center. The *River Diagram* has five positions, each with one odd and one even number—though the numbers total ten, the positions are only five.

During the Zhou dynasty, the *River Diagram* was stored alongside the celestial sphere and precious jades in the royal treasury.

Chen Tuan said: "The two diagrams, Five as the constant and the other as the variant—if speaking of the *River Diagram*, then it is the constant and the *Luo Writing* the variant; if speaking of the *Luo Writing*, then it is the constant and the *River Diagram* the variant. Though the two diagrams differ in vertical and horizontal arrangement, they mutually present and reveal one another—this is what is meant by their interweaving as warp and weft."

Weng Yong said: "In the *River Diagram*, the positions of yin and yang take the generative numbers as primary, with the completion numbers paired to them. In the yang direction of the northeast, the odd numbers govern, paired with even; in the yin direction of the southwest, the even numbers govern, paired with odd."

Hu Yuan said: "The *River Diagram* divides yin and yang by generation and completion. The five generative numbers of yang govern the five completion numbers of yin, placed together in their directions. Yang within and yin without, generation and completion unite—this is the meaning of harmonious interaction. The *River Diagram*’s number seventeen establishes its form through opposition, thus it is constant."

Hu Yigui said: "In the *Luo Writing*, compared to the *River Diagram*, there is only Five but no Ten. Yet the combinations of One and Nine, Two and Eight, Three and Seven, Four and Six encircle and face one another—there is never an absence of Ten. The earlier Confucians deeply investigated the principles of yin-yang creation, exploring the subtle and obscure, and already plumbed the wonders of the *River Diagram*. Yet distinctions between similarity and difference cannot be avoided."

Liu Mu said: "...One and Six in the north, Two and Seven in the south, Three and Eight on the left, Four and Nine on the right, Five and Ten in the center—this is the *Luo Writing*. Wearing Nine and treading One, Three on the left and Seven on the right, Two and Four as shoulders, Six and Eight as feet—this is the *River Diagram*. Thus, Nine is the *River Diagram*, and Ten is the *Luo Writing*."

Zhang Wenrao, well-versed in Shao Yong’s teachings, also took Nine as the *Diagram* and Ten as the *Writing*. Yet Shao Yong only spoke of round and square, not of the numbers Nine and Ten. He also took Ten as the *River Diagram* and Nine as the *Luo Writing*, aligning with the words of Confucius and Jizi.

Some say that Shao Yong transmitted his teachings through Mu Xiu, and Liu Mu through Zhong Fang—both received them from Chen Tuan. Zhu Xi said: "Chen Tuan transmitted the *Xiantian Diagram* to Zhong Fang, who passed it to Mu Xiu, who gave it to Li Zhicai, who taught Shao Yong. Zhong Fang also transmitted the *River Diagram* and *Luo Writing* to Li Gai, who gave them to Xu Jian, who passed them to Fan Kuachang, who taught Liu Mu. Mu Xiu transmitted the *Taiji Diagram* to Zhou Dunyi, who taught the Cheng brothers. The Chengs, following Liu’s teachings and Zhu Xi’s critiques, vehemently refuted them—this is an unchanging judgment for all ages."

Wei Shi again refuted Zhu Xi, claiming he initially attributed the Nine-*Diagram* and Ten-*Writing* theory to Liu Changmin, falsely ascribing it to Chen Tuan. Jing Shi Jiang Shan later took the *Xiantian* as the *River Diagram*, the Five Phases’ generative and completion numbers as the *Luo Writing*, and the "Wearing Nine" arrangement as the Supreme Unity’s descending numbers.

Luo Duanliang once said: "I received the *River Diagram* from Cai Jitong, who obtained it from a recluse in Shu. Its form resembled a chariot wheel, black and white intermingled, divided into eight parts to form the eight trigrams. The white represented pure yang, symbolizing *Qian*; the black, pure yin, symbolizing *Kun*. The remaining trigrams were formed by gradually diminishing the black and white."

Xie Fang said: "I once received the *River Diagram* from an extraordinary person. Its form resembled the eight trigrams, with *Kan* and *Li* inscribed in the center and intersecting, akin to the alchemical arts of *Kan* and *Li*."

Zhang Heng’s *Qian Zaodu* presents the Nine Palaces’ numbers as the Supreme Unity Diagram, which Liu Mu took to be the *River Diagram*."

Some identify the nine- and twelve-number diagrams as the *River Diagram*. Yet how could such diversity and complexity arise? Master Zhu argued that the *River Diagram* and *Luo Writing* were not to be trusted, a view held since Ouyang Xiu. However, the *Gu Ming*, *Appended Judgments*, and *Analects* all attest to them. Though the numbers transmitted by various scholars for these two diagrams may intersect, they never contradict; whether counting forward or tracing backward, whether arranged vertically, horizontally, or in curves, all follow clear principles—such is the profundity of their explanations.

Ouyang Xiu, having never seen the original diagrams, merely relied on commentarial interpretations, dismissing the diagrams as nonexistent and even doubting that the *Appended Judgments* were composed by Confucius. How could he have erred so gravely in his reasoning? The *River Diagram* and *Luo Writing* encompass the mechanisms of the myriad things’ transformations—what could possibly contravene them? Moreover, the sages indeed used the *River Diagram* to draw the trigrams; can one then equate the *River Diagram* with the eight trigrams? Yet it harmonizes with the *Luo Writing*. The *Luo Writing* and the initial "one" to subsequent "nine" of the *Great Plan* together comprise the nine categories’ numbers. While rooted in the *Great Plan*, the *Luo Writing* uses the five odd numbers to govern the four even numbers, making odd numbers primary. The one does not pair with four, six, or eight; instead, each adheres to its kind, attaching to the sides of one, three, seven, and nine. The *Luo Writing*’s vertical and horizontal sums total fifteen, while seven, eight, and nine alternately wax and wane. Excluding five and dividing ten, one contains nine, two contains eight, three contains seven, and four contains six—interwoven and interlocked, all align tacitly. Such is the marvel of boundless transformation.

Thus, the numbers of the *River Diagram* and *Luo Writing* both derive from five, extending to ten, and together form fifty. The *Luo Writing*’s accumulated numbers total forty-five, dispersed externally, dividing yin and yang into old and young. Only five resides at the center, inherently containing the five numbers, thus also totaling fifty. The *Luo Writing* governs change, culminating in nine, with its positions and actualities favoring odd numbers while even numbers are deficient. Yin and yang’s numbers each total twenty. Though their quantities differ, five at the center allows: great yang, gaining five, becomes six; young yin, gaining five, becomes seven; young yang, gaining five, becomes eight; great yin, gaining five, becomes nine. This mirrors the *River Diagram*’s interwoven yin and yang, producing identical generative and completive numbers. The *Luo Writing*’s odd and even numbers oppose each other, just as the *River Diagram*’s dispersed numbers remain ununified; the *River Diagram*’s generative and completive numbers pair, just as the *Luo Writing*’s numbers unite and belong. Hence, earlier scholars regarded the *River Diagram* and *Luo Writing* as warp and weft, the eight trigrams and nine categories as outer and inner—this is the principle.

The *Great Expansion*’s number fifty likewise arises from the extension of five, each reaching its ten, combining into fifty—no different in essence. Moreover, both the *River Diagram* and *Luo Writing* have void centers, embodying the Supreme Ultimate; their odd and even numbers each total twenty, embodying the Two Modes. Their vertical and horizontal sums of fifteen, alternating between seven, eight, nine, and six, embody the Four Images. *Qian*, *Kun*, *Kan*, and *Li* form the four cardinal directions, while *Zhen*, *Dui*, *Xun*, and *Gen* form the four corners—these are the eight trigrams. In the *Primordial Heaven Diagram*’s circular arrangement, *Qian* culminates at noon, *Kun* at midnight, *Li* at dawn, and *Kan* at dusk. Yang arises at midnight, reaching its zenith at noon; mid-yin arises at noon, culminating at midnight. Yang resides in the south, yin in the north. In its square arrangement, *Qian* begins in the northwest, *Kun* ends in the southeast; yang lies in the north, yin in the south—yet it remains no different from the *River Diagram*’s interwoven odd and even numbers. This is the *Primordial Heaven* as the origin of the alchemical Dao.

The *River Diagram*’s yang generative numbers are five, the root of the five phases; its yin completive numbers are ten, the foundation of the myriad transformations. One and nine, four and six each sum to ten—Metal and Water share the same origin; two and eight, three and seven each sum to ten—Wood and Fire share the same body. Together they total fifty-five, divided among the five directions. One and six reside in the north; two and seven in the south; four and nine in the west; three and eight in the east; five and ten at the center. Subtracting the generative numbers, each direction retains its original number—this is the source of the five phases. Further subtracting the completive numbers from each direction’s original number yields the five phases’ essence. Excluding these, each direction retains five numbers, totaling twenty-five—the root of the five phases, symbolizing the earth of *wu*. Repeatedly subtracting the five phases’ essence, the north retains four numbers, symbolizing Metal. Metal inherently generates Water, yet Metal itself arises from Water, like lead emerging from silver—this is the Metal Tiger. Metal born in Water is the Tiger’s generation from Water. The south retains three numbers, symbolizing Wood. Wood inherently generates Fire, yet Wood itself arises from Fire, like mercury congealing upon encountering lead—this is the Wood Dragon. Wood born in Fire is the Dragon’s emergence from Fire. The east retains two numbers, symbolizing Fire. Fire arises from Wood; extracting mercury from cinnabar is Wood Mercury concealed within *Li*, the mother hidden in the child’s womb—this is the seventh return. The west retains one number, symbolizing Water. Water arises from Metal; the qi returns to its origin, like Metal Lead concealed within *Kan*, the child returning to the mother’s womb—this is the ninth return. The void center symbolizes the Supreme Ultimate; the Four Images unite to form ten—this is the wondrous principle of true yin and earth. All are the mechanisms of heaven and earth’s inverted yin-yang generation, harboring unfathomable marvels. How could the calendrical calculations of the Supreme Unity or the astral numbers of directional arrays compare to these?

Yet some claim these transmissions originated with *Xiyi*, asserting that the *Hetu* and *Luoshu* were originally rendered in circles—thus, after the Three Dynasties, the diagrams and writings lay concealed for over a thousand years, preserved only by the *fangji* practitioners, until their resurgence in the Song. However, the *fangji* merely appropriated their numerical configurations, employing odd and even counts to balance growth and decline, aligning them with the dynamics of punishment and virtue, life and death—nothing more than the foundational techniques of calendrical prognostication and astral divination. That earlier scholars dismissed the *Cantong Qi* as unworthy of esteem may have been an overstatement.

Alas, tradition holds: "The Yellow River brought forth the *Hetu*, and the sage took it as his model." He imitated it to draft the Eight Trigrams—yet the Eight Trigrams are not the *Hetu* itself. Moreover, the *Hetu* emerged in the age of Fuxi, though none among the sages of that era spoke of it. Confucius, too, alluded to it but never explicitly described the diagram. After Confucius, from Guan and Liu onward, interpretations proliferated, each clinging to their own views, yet none reached a definitive conclusion. Nevertheless, Master Zhu’s distinction—ten as the *Hetu*, nine as the *Luoshu*—suffices to resolve the matter. Some identify the *Xiantian* diagram as the *Hetu*; others liken it to a chariot wheel’s black-and-white interplay; still others model it after the Eight Trigrams or claim both the nine- and twelve-point diagrams as the *Hetu*. Such disparate views leave later generations at a loss as to which to follow.

Yet the original text of the *Xici* states: "From Heavenly One to Earthly Ten," wherein five serves as the generative mother and ten as the generative child. From one to ten, five occupies its natural, fixed position. The fifteen points resemble celestial constellations—hence the name "diagram"—what connection does this bear to fantastical absurdities? What room remains for doubt?

Ah, Master Cheng remarked: "Principle precedes image; image precedes number. Grasp the meaning, and image and number reside within." From the moment the *Hetu* and *Luoshu* were first inscribed, their significance unfolded through their patterns—reversals and returns, rises and falls, encompassing the hidden and manifest of heaven and earth, the flight of birds and the stillness of plants, the subtlest details without exception. Thus, the *Yijing*’s alignment with the principles of nature and destiny, its embodiment of the Dao of transformation, all rely on images to illuminate principle.

Those who drown in image and number, reducing it to mere divination, fail to seek principle through meaning and stray far from the Dao—such are the scholars of Guo Pu and Guan Lu. Their claim to "exhaust numbers to determine the world’s images" and "manifest images to fix the world’s fortunes" derives from the movement of spirit and the stirrings of the heart-mind—merely one facet of the whole. If one seeks the *Yijing*’s text through this alone, how can it fully encompass the unity of substance and function, the seamless interplay of manifest and subtle, the doctrines of life and death, the mysteries of the hidden and revealed, and the vast coherence of heaven and earth? To trace the sages’ lineage through such means is something I cannot accept.

The Dao of nature and destiny is one; scholars need only seek the Dao. If one pursues it at the source of nature and destiny, what remains unseen? Yet if the pursuit lacks diligence, few will perceive it. Han Yu, in his *Inquiry into Nature*, sought to extend the thread of the sages. Though his principles were not fully illuminated, he aimed to broaden them. Through the ages, many have discoursed on nature, but how few have grasped its essence!

Yao and Shun embodied it in their nature; Tang and Wu embodied it in their persons—this is the inherent truth of nature. Thus, Yao’s charge to Shun—"The mind of the Dao is most subtle"—sufficed to reveal the root of kingly governance. When the Zhou declined, Confucius arose to continue the tradition. His words—"Nature and the Dao of Heaven," "What completes it is nature," "Each rectifies its nature and destiny," "Know nature, and you know Heaven"—most keenly express the source of the Dao.

Zisi called it "what Heaven commands is called nature"; what Heaven bestows upon humanity is nature. To follow this nature is called the Dao. Mencius’ assertion of the goodness of nature aligns with this. The human heart-mind governs nature and emotion, inherently without flaw—this is the nature endowed by Heaven’s mandate. Its possession of benevolence, righteousness, propriety, and wisdom requires no artifice; this is the goodness that follows. For Heaven’s mandate to things is the goodness of nature, inherently present. Evil arises from the nature of *qi* and material form—this is the nature proximate to one’s constitution. Thus, when moved by external things, stirred by desire, or obscured by habit, distinctions emerge between the wise and the foolish.

Yet goodness, like a mirror’s tarnish or water’s turbidity, is but a fleeting cloud over the vast sky. When the tarnish is cleansed, clarity remains; when turbidity settles, purity endures. The inherent goodness cannot be altered. Thus, it is neither augmented in sages nor diminished in fools. Only by fully realizing one’s nature can one remain unmoved by external things, undisturbed by desire, and unobscured by habit. Then, in its utmost emptiness and numinosity, utmost purity and clarity, it shines like the unclouded sky—how could good and evil be conflated?

Thus, stillness brings focus, and movement brings uprightness; sincerity establishes and clarity permeates, giving rise to perspicacity. This is the governor of the five senses, the commander of the hundred limbs, the repository of the four beginnings, and the regulator of the seven emotions. In its vastness, nothing lies beyond—here reside the rectitude of nature and destiny, the principles of life and death, the mysteries of the hidden and revealed. In its minuteness, nothing lies within—here are bound the forms of the great and small, the emotions of all beings, the qualities of shape and color.

When fully realized, it becomes a Zhou or a Confucius; when perverted, a Jie or a Zhou. In action, it becomes an Yi or a Fu; in purity, a Boyi or a Shuqi; in tranquility, a Yan or a Min—all stand as exemplars of illuminating goodness. Their influence spans generations, enduring without end. In harmony, it becomes ritual and music; in accord, it becomes laws and institutions; in guidance, it becomes the cardinal principles; in regulation, it becomes canonical norms.

All that accords with Heaven’s order and sequence—is this not the public function of Heavenly principle?

Moreover, Yangzi’s claim that good and evil are mixed refers only to the biases of emotions, habits, and qi-quality, not to Heaven’s true mandate. Gaozi’s assertion that "life is what is meant by nature" makes all that emotions desire and enact part of nature. Xunzi’s claim that "nature is evil" treats goodness as artificial. These views, which fixate on emotions, habits, and qi-quality as the essence of true nature, diverge widely from it. Hanzi’s division into three grades posits that nature comprises three grades of the five emotions, while the seven emotions constitute what nature commands—how vast and multifarious are Heaven’s endowments! Yet how can one establish a foundation from such confusion? All that issues from nature is emotion. How can it be confined to three grades, or further burdened with five natures? These are merely the biases of qi-quality.

Later scholars, noting that such discussions had become rare since the Qin and Han, revived them with particular emphasis. Ouyangzi claimed that "unity of nature is not an urgent concern for scholars, nor a topic the sages seldom addressed"—how could the great root remain so obscure? Dongzi said: "Mandate is Heaven’s decree; nature is life’s substance; emotion is human desire; the Dao is the path to governance; benevolence, righteousness, ritual, and music are its instruments." Wangzi said: "Nature is the Supreme Ultimate of the Five Constants, yet the Five Constants cannot be called nature—this comes close to the Dao." Yet some depart from Mengzi’s teachings and align with Xun and Han, how misguided! They simply fail to distinguish clearly.

Only Master Zhou said: "Nature at rest is what is called sageliness." Master Cheng said: "What Heaven bestows is mandate; what things receive is nature; nature is principle." This is truly luminous. Only Zhou and Cheng can be considered Mengzi’s true successors. Their surpassing of the other masters is evident.

Yet what of the divergences in the teachings of Laozi and the Buddhists? Laozi said: "Nature is spirit, the primordial, indestructible numen." The Buddha said: "Nature is awareness, the fullness of original emptiness and numinosity." Only by severing worldly affairs and eliminating desires can one avoid defilement, achieve purity, and establish firmness—thus accumulating numinous clarity into manifest spiritual transformation. Yet this treats all things in heaven and earth with form and qi as illusory and empty. Is this not to lose the reality of principle and qi in emptiness and nothingness? As for their exclusive focus on life and death or personal cultivation, how do they differ from the paths of Shen Buhai, Han Fei, or Yang Zhu?

Yet the Way of Heaven and humanity is one. The Dao in its utmost purity and essence, principle in its utmost subtlety and profundity—if humans cannot walk alongside heaven and earth without transgression, it is only because they fail to distinguish between Heavenly principle and human desire. Thus they cannot fully realize the hearts of sages and worthies. One who fully realizes the heart fully realizes nature and attains the Dao of mandate.

**On Spirits**
Someone asked: "Daoist lineages take spirits as their concern—do they truly exist?"
I replied: "Who says they do not? The confusion arises only from imprecise distinctions. Heaven is accumulated qi; earth is the denser aspect of qi—these are the formless. Within the movement of yin and yang lies the flowing operation of Heaven’s mandate. Since this flow is ceaseless, there must be something that governs it." Master Cheng said: "That which governs is called the Emperor; its wondrous function is called spirits." He also said: "Spirits are the traces of creation, the inherent capacity of the two qi." The workings of yin and yang are invisible, yet their principles can be inferred. Where principle manifests or withdraws beyond perception, the numinous resides. Thus even the sages never denied their existence—they simply did not dwell on them exclusively.

Confucius said: "Sacrifice to the spirits above and below as if they were present"; "The Way of spirits is to be revered and kept at a distance"; "The virtue of spirits is most perfect." He did not deny their existence—he merely refrained from speaking of anomalies, force, disorder, or spirits, lest people become mired in absurdity. The *Book of Changes* says: "The unfathomable workings of yin and yang are called the numinous," and "Spirits unite with good and ill fortune"—this is the meaning.

When the heart is preserved, the Dao becomes clear and principle manifest. The mechanism of yin and yang, its issuing and entering, coming and going, is none other than our own heart. Its good and ill fortune are one with spirits—without spirits, they cannot endure. What then can be said of good and ill fortune? Only those who manifest the Dao and embody numinous virtue can unite with it to penetrate the power of spiritual clarity. Thus "transforming through greatness is called sageliness; sageliness beyond comprehension is called the numinous"—this is what is meant by "subtly pervading all things as the numinous." The Dao of exhausting the numinous and knowing transformation is fully realized here.

The sage embodies the wondrous function of heaven and earth, uniting the ultimate achievement of heaven and humanity. Only then does one become numinous without knowing how—this is the numinous. Thus it is said: "Serving Heaven is illumination; serving Earth is discernment." Only by sincerely aligning the heart to move Heaven can Heaven respond through its mechanism. That which is invisible and unknowable is called the numinous presence. Though "the workings of Heaven above are soundless and scentless," resonance here elicits response there—never interrupted.

Thus even the wicked, if they fast, bathe, and purify themselves, can serve the Supreme Emperor and spirits. Offerings are accepted through sincerity—knowing sincerity is sufficient to serve Heaven. In sacrificial rites, the burning of reeds and fat reports to the luminous; millet, rice, liver, and lungs, combined with aromatic herbs, report to the corporeal. All arise from the resonance of qi.

As for the numinous in the manifest world: thunder and drums, wind and rain, frost and dew—its obscurity and brightness, transformations and fleeting myriad forms—if not the work of the supremely numinous, what else could achieve this?

The hidden as ghosts cannot be traced or sought. Yet when the mournful cries of fish and the desolate rustling of reeds gather—whether in sound or form, whether through resonance of qi or the arrival of thought—what is lofty has no shape, what is lowly has substance: these are the stagnant and untransformed, the bent and unextended. This is what is meant by "essence-qi becomes things, wandering souls become transformations," constrained by the contraction and expansion of yin and yang. Thus the yang-essence and yin-corporeality reveal the true nature of ghosts and spirits.

Observe the traces of creation, and the origins of being and non-being become clear.

Someone asks: The principle of existence is certain, yet when people in the world suffer illness, they say it is the harm of some ghost or spirit, and only by offering blood sacrifices and prayers can they be spared. If the prayers are answered, they clamor to declare the spirit’s efficacy. If unanswered, and a sudden change in life or death occurs, they resentfully claim some ghost has invaded, and the spirit has aided it. Yet when they seek the object of their prayers, it is often not a spirit that Heaven, Earth, or the altars of state should properly worship—these are all excessive cults, unworthy even of the name "spirit," let alone capable of aiding ghosts to violate the Mandate of Heaven. Is this not the height of folly?

This has not arisen overnight. It is the custom of streets and alleys, where common men and foolish women are deluded by vulgar talk, startling the world and shocking the public, until it becomes a prevailing wind. If one but understands that the living are the numinous presence of qi, the dead are the contraction of qi; the extended become spirits, the contracted become ghosts; the ethereal soul belongs to Heaven, the bodily form to Earth—how could perverse and deviant beliefs still deceive?

Those who preserve the purity and sincerity of their hearts, uniting their power with Heaven and Earth, may achieve feats like Lu Yang, who reversed his spear to halt the sun, or the chaste widow whose weeping brought down frost, or Master Han, who parted the clouds of Mount Heng and drove away the ravenous crocodiles. As the early Confucians said: "Where there is sincerity, there is spirit; without sincerity, where does spirit reside?"

Those who daily invoke ghosts and spirits merely awaken the numinous clarity and undivided essence within themselves, aligning with the numinous transformations of Heaven and Earth. This is what Master Dong meant by "releasing the yang and restraining the yin." The *Shenyi* says: "The qi of prior Heaven is the true imperial law; the spirit of my heart is the thunder official and mountain count." This is likely what it means.

Those who dismiss this as esoteric doctrine without examining it—do they truly understand the workings of numinous transformation or the changes of ghosts and spirits?

When Mangmiaozi of Zhixing wandered through mountains and marshes, he saw trees: the tall ones twisted and gnarled, the low ones lush and thriving; the crooked ones coiled, the straight ones towering and dense. Some were several feet in girth, others no thicker than a finger. Flourishing and verdant, they filled the cliffs and ravines, valleys and gorges, while clouds and mist rose and fell with them, and birds nested in their branches, singing in harmony.

Turning with a smile, he said: "I have always loved the lush vitality of things, and none embody it more than plants. Those that soar on wings, glide with scales, or run on legs—are they not all endowed with their own nature? Yet the principle of plants is most visible and delightful. Consider the succession of the four seasons: every flower and blade of grass, whether red or purple, white or yellow, does not miss its time or lose its color. Cold and heat respond to the seasons, and buds and shoots emerge in due course. Their dazzling hues and competing beauty cannot be captured by brocade or cosmetics. Spring cannot make them bloom in winter, nor summer make them flourish in autumn. The swift cannot be hastened, nor the slow forced to delay. Even when planted in halls or cultivated in pots, their branches bent to fit their containers, their natures harmonized to suit their care—though it may seem they cannot follow their Heaven-given nature, and their essence is harmed by constraint and narrowness, yet they do not know their own injury. In the end, they die, but if they await their time and follow the seasons, they will bloom and flourish again. Who, then, makes this so? It is the work of the Master of Creation."

Here the subtlest and most wondrous is thus revealed, for each thing is endowed with its own nature, each possessing its own singularity—what is meant by "each thing containing one Supreme Ultimate." Yet their nurture depends on the timely arrival of conditions. Thus, if their essence is not depleted nor their growth suppressed, there is nothing strange in this principle.

Consider the mountains and marshes: beyond the reach of manure and soil, beyond the touch of irrigation. Whether moistened by rain and dew, assailed by frost and snow, or subjected to untimely dryness or dampness, uneven cold or heat—without distinction of fragrance or foulness, all rely solely on the nourishment of wind and rain, the steadfastness of earth and stone. Yet the tall and the low, the crooked and the straight, the great and the small—each follows its inherent nature, flourishing and thriving without limit. Those that fully realize the completeness of their endowed nature, though mere mountain blossoms or wild herbs, vie in fragrance and beauty, yielding nothing to those in pots and urns, and far surpassing any single flower or blade of grass.

Thus, pines, cedars, and camphor suffice for beams and pillars; catalpa, camphor, and nanmu suffice for corners and edges. Their resin can become oil or lacquer; their withered parts, vessels or firewood. Their sweet fruits are edible; their fine roots, medicinal. Their auspicious blackness can be recorded. Each adapts to its material, yet none are born in deep mountains or hollow forests merely because they are deemed useless—thus preserving their original nature. Each fulfills its singular nature, and those that fully receive Heaven remain beyond constraint. How vast and wondrous is this!

The Dao in the world is like water flowing through the earth, reaching everywhere without exception. How can plants and trees, devoid of sentiment or knowledge, achieve such harmony? Now I comprehend the principle that the myriad things share one root—a truth constant through ancient and modern times, filling the cosmos and unchanging. I know not the extremity of my joy.

One who laughed afterward asked: "Why do you delight in this observation?"
I replied: "It is not for you to know. In observing the nature of plants, I exhaust my own nature—how could I not rejoice?"

The one who laughed said: "What you observe are merely the places where you wander and rest, the sights your eyes have beheld. How can you know the lush verdure of trees, their cycles of prosperity and decline, their waxing and waning with the seasons? These do not occur in a single day. Were they not secluded in the perilous wilds of mountains, they would daily contend with bandits, suffer the calamity of axes, with no regard for age or weakness, crookedness or straightness, hardness or brittleness, size or smallness. Buffeted by wind and frost, felled by ice and snow, they are hewn day and night—carved into boats, bent into mats, chiseled into dwellings, severed for carts, broken for firewood—countless fates. All are harmed by their clarity, destroyed by their substance. Thus, the nature of trees is ruined—what vitality remains to observe? Only what Zhuangzi called the 'gnarled and deformed' mountain trees, which by chance preserve their natural lifespan, may survive. As for those long and great, like lattice and pillars, I have never seen them. You do not choose to depart but instead gaze upon them daily, as Mencius said of Ox Mountain’s trees, once beautiful. Yet how, when felled day after day, can their nature not be harmed, just as one’s own nature is severed without awareness? This applies not only to trees."

Master Shao said: "Even in wood, stone, tiles, and pebbles, each has its fixed principle." What sorrow remains? For the nature endowed in things cannot be harmed. Their suitability for use is a matter of fortune or misfortune—this is their destined lot. Thus, the finest timber, fit for zithers and flutes, is offered in the ancestral temple; the sturdiest, fit for trays and mats, graces halls and chambers. The inferior is placed in lowly quarters; the rotten, cast into dust. Each follows its fate—yet who can destroy or sever that which fully realizes its Heaven-given nature? This is what it means to exhaust one’s nature.

The one who laughed said: "Indeed."

*Reading the "Observing Things" Chapter*
When the books of Confucius and Mencius appeared, their Dao was illuminated. As their Dao faded, royal governance declined. It was not that the books were lost, but that those who spoke of the Dao failed to clarify it. From the Warring States onward, even in the prosperous ages of Han and Tang, those who knew and practiced it were few. With the rise of the Song, the Dao became manifest. Master Zhou elucidated the uncharted depths of the Supreme Ultimate, while Master Shao revealed the infinite principles of the Primordial Heaven. Through them, the teachings of Fuxi, King Wen, the Duke of Zhou, and Confucius—long severed—were revived. No revival was greater than this.

The Dao of the sages originates in the heart—*The Book of Changes* is the study of the heart. Master Shao said: "The heart is the Supreme Ultimate. To study is to cultivate the heart." The study of Primordial Heaven is the study of the heart. His words on the heart reached their zenith; his discourse on principle was luminous. His circular and square diagrams exhaust the wonders of *The Book of Changes*. Though vast as Heaven and Earth, subtle as yin and yang, mysterious as ghosts and spirits, profound as images and numbers, the transformations of being and non-being, the boundlessness of things, the divinity of principle—all emerge from the Supreme Ultimate and return to it, gathered within the single heart. Expanding, it fills the cosmos; contracting, it dwindles to the finest hair. Its purity, refinement, depth, and subtlety are truly detailed and precise.

This is what he meant by "observing with the heart" and "observing with principle." He also said: "Do not observe things with the heart, nor with the self, nor with things themselves." Consider the successions of Yao, Shun, Yu, and Tang—differing in virtue, merit, exile, and execution; the durations of Zhou, Qin, Han, and Chu—varying in length. The choice lies solely in good and evil.

Thus we know that order and chaos arise from what is esteemed as righteousness or profit, while deviance and rectitude follow from words and deeds. All the affairs of the Three Sovereigns, Five Emperors, Three Kings, and Five Hegemons—like a mirror’s reflection in water—reveal even the finest detail without concealment. In turning inward to observe, this is what is meant by the sage’s ability to unify the feelings of the myriad things. This is the Way of exhausting principle, fulfilling nature, and reaching destiny.

Expanding to its utmost, the numbers of heaven and earth, yin and yang, arise naturally from the formless One, and the non-acting One serves as the model for the Dao. There are three applications of this, corresponding to heaven, earth, and humanity. Thus it is said: before the Ultimate of Non-being, yin contains yang; after the appearance of images, yang divides yin. The cycles of the Celestial Root and the Lunar Grotto reside within the inquiry into the Ultimate of Non-being. How could this not align with Master Zhou’s teachings?

Since the *Yijing* appeared, later commentators have numbered in the thousands, yet how many have truly grasped the sage’s intent prior to the first hexagram line? Yet those who admire such teachings merely seek the leisurely pleasures of seclusion, remaining ignorant of the Way itself. When Han Yu read Xunzi, he took what was closest to Confucius and deemed Yang Xiong a disciple of the sages. Comparing *Tai Xuan* to the *Yijing*, Shao Yong called pre-cosmic learning the study of the heart, and post-cosmic learning the study of traces—movement between being and non-being, life and death, is the Dao. To seek it through cunning calculations and shortcuts is to bend heavenly principle to serve human desire. How vastly distant is such a view!

This is sufficient to show that his vision was most expansive, his knowledge most far-reaching, his discourse most lofty, his joy most great. He could engage in matters of utmost breadth, depth, height, and magnitude while remaining empty within. Could this not be the work of one most divine and sagely? Truly, it is no falsehood.

Who among those who nurture the heart well could speak of the learning of the heart with him? Alas! Some say that beyond the six directions, the sage preserves but does not discuss. Yet Shao Yong may be called one who exhausts the divine and knows transformation. Could this be what the sage did not discuss?

Reading the *Biography of Dong Zhongshu*, I observe that after the Three Dynasties, the Zhou declined and the Kingly Way ceased. Qin inherited the practices of the Warring States, its qi dissipated and its ambitions perverse. It burned the *Book of Poetry* and *Book of Documents* to ashes, and the transmission of the Dao was nearly extinguished without a trace. When Han arose and the world was greatly ordered, its corrupt scholars and petty officials still clung to old habits, unable to revive the teachings of the former kings from their remnants. Emperor Wen was called a worthy ruler, yet even he failed to employ Jia Yi. A thousand years later, reading Jia’s words still evokes sorrow and indignation—how much more so for those unlike Jia Yi!

Dong Zhongshu, lowering his curtain to lecture and recite, did not glance at his garden for three years. In his comings and goings, he moved only in accord with ritual. Scholars all revered him as a teacher. When Emperor Wu sought worthy and learned men, Zhongshu responded with his memorial. At that time, the meeting of ruler and minister was something Qin could never have hoped for. His words were sufficient to expose the flaws of the Zhou decline and restore the correctness of the Kingly Way, with benevolence, righteousness, music, and ritual as its foundation. His distinctions between nature and destiny, feelings and substance, and his discourses on yin and yang, punishment and virtue were incisive.

From this we see that in the interaction between heaven and humanity, when ritual and propriety are cultivated in the person, good and evil move heaven. The unceasing transmission from Yao, Shun, Yu, and Tang was due to upholding the single Way without the flaws of exhausted governance. How could the mediocre men of Qin and Han ever attain this? Though Emperor Wu was resolute and decisive, after three memorials he still could not implement Zhongshu’s teachings in the slightest, instead sending him to serve as Chancellor of Jiangdu. Was this not a cause for lament in that era?

Take Gongsun Hong, who curried favor to gain power, obtaining a marquisate through classical studies, yet he envied upright discourse and banished Zhongshu to Jiaoxi. Though Zhongshu could not promote his Way, he rectified himself to lead his subordinates, serving two arrogant kings while maintaining their respect. He ultimately retired to complete his studies and writings at home—was this not the perfect embodiment of the Way of advancement and withdrawal?

His words declared: "Rectify your judgment without scheming for profit; clarify the Way without calculating its effects." Only with the emergence of the Cheng brothers in the Song was this teaching fully elucidated, becoming a model for later generations. At that time, even Ban Gu, Jia Yi, Liu Xiang, and Yang Xiong could not compare—how much less those who came after!

Sima Qian wrote that Liu Xiang praised Zhongshu’s talent, saying even Yi Yin and Lü Shang could not surpass him, while Guan Zhong and Yan Ying barely matched him. This was excessive. Liu Xiang’s words were mistaken. Had Zhongshu met a ruler who practiced his Way, would he not have achieved the accomplishments of Yi Yin and Lü Shang? How could Guan and Yan even compare? For Guan and Yan were merely aides to hegemons, capable only of hegemonic affairs—how could they match the greatness of the Kingly Way?

Moreover, considering the depth of his teacher-student lineage, he still fell short of Ziyou and Zixia. Yet when Master Zhou Dunyi revived the lost learning and promoted the Way in the Song, scholars to this day all follow him—the Dao has never perished. How could his lineage be considered flawed? Had Sima Qian seen this, would later generations not have failed to understand Zhongshu? Yet some who recognized his worth sought to enshrine him in the temple and offer sacrifices alongside Confucius, to perpetuate the illumination of Confucius’ teachings and the suppression of the Hundred Schools. But this was never carried out—was this not regrettable?

Yet the Dao and words of Zhongshu do not depend on sacrificial offerings. How difficult it is to meet with true understanding! Across a hundred generations, the same holds. Even Emperor Wu of Han, with all his wisdom, cast him aside and did not employ him—how much less those beneath him? There is no cause for surprise.

*Postscript to the Orthodox Lineage of Literary Works*
The qi of the Three Luminaries and Five Peaks issues forth as literature, and literature is the vessel for conveying the Dao.

When literature flourishes, the Dao is thereby illuminated—yet it must be rooted in qi. The primordial qi courses through Heaven and Earth, and thus the Dao is established. The ancients of virtue invariably left behind words, for their harmony and compliance accumulated within, and their brilliance manifested without. Without the Dao being full and virtue being luminous, how could this be expressed in words? Thus, the *Orthodox Lineage of Literary Works* compiled by the True and Loyal Duke was precisely to preserve those of correct source and flow—hence the name *Orthodox Lineage*. Its arrangement comprises four categories: first, diplomatic correspondence; second, discourses and treatises; third, narrative accounts; and fourth, poetry and rhapsodies. Only the writings of the Yu and Xia dynasties, the Warring States, the Western Han, and the early Tang—rooted in the *Zuozhuan*—were included. Thereafter, those deemed worthy of emulation—Ban [Gu], Sima [Qian], Han [Yu], and Liu [Zongyuan]—were followed by Ouyang [Xiu], Su [Shi], Zeng [Gong], and Wang [Anshi], while the rest were excluded. Its standards and distinctions were stringent, for it took morality as its foundation, principle and righteousness as its essence, and required that what was expressed in literary form must first attain the correctness of human nature and feelings before it could be deemed fitting. Viewed thus, even among the words of the ancients, those truly in accord are exceedingly rare. How much less could those who fail to grasp this, yet recklessly call their work "literature," hope to align with the masters? Such a goal is unattainable. Yet this is only because their Dao remains unmanifest and their qi unfulfilled.

Learning serves to exhaust principle and achieve practical use, and it must be rooted in the Dao and cultivated through qi. When one knows what to cultivate, qi becomes full and the Dao is established—how could one’s writing then fail to be masterful? I have devoted myself to literature for over twenty years, seeking its essentials. Below the Six Classics, I have savored the works of the Han and Tang masters, aspiring to trace the words of the Three Dynasties and explore how they correspond to the Classics as their inner and outer expressions. Yet I am ashamed that my learning remains weak and sluggish, lacking breadth, depth, and integration—this is my constant fear. Only upon studying this compilation have I gained clarity and insight, deepening my conviction and expanding my understanding. Even if one’s path is not yet correct or one’s cultivation not yet perfected, and thus unworthy of inclusion, this too becomes distinctly discernible.

Alas, how few in the world comprehend this! They prize mere ornamentation, embellishment, triviality, and decadence, regarding this work as distant as a thousand *li* or as perilous as a ten-thousand-*ren* cliff. Who among them realizes that without first following the proper path, one cannot reach the destination? Even those who seek the threads of its source and flow amid the essence and refinement—if they pursue it without guidance—how could they fail to grasp the residual intent of the Loyal Duke? And for those who have the honor of continuing his legacy, how could they not count themselves fortunate?

---

**On Distinguishing the *Xunzi***

The *Xunzi*, in thirty-three chapters, begins with *Encouraging Learning* and *Cultivating the Self*, grounding itself in the words of Confucius. *The Efficacy of the Ru*, *The Way of the Ruler*, *The Way of the Minister*, *Kingship and Hegemony*, and *Rites and Music* follow, seemingly grasping the beginnings of the kingly way—yet interspersed with debates on enriching the state and strengthening the military. It explicitly declares the inherent evil of human nature as untreatable, all derived from its fundamental premise. By falsely invoking Yao and Shun and rejecting Zisi and Mencius, it paved the way for Li Si’s destruction of Qin and the pernicious habits of the Warring States—how could these be escaped? Li Si studied under Xunzi, and Li Si’s downfall reveals the nature of Xunzi’s teachings.

At that time, Mencius engaged with Xunzi in debate, yet we never hear from Wan Zhang or his disciples what Mencius’ words on this matter truly were. Had Mencius spoken of the goodness of human nature and first addressed King Hui of Liang with the words, "There is nothing but benevolence and righteousness," then even if his teachings were insufficient for his own era or for ten thousand generations, how far would Xunzi’s words have been from the harm wrought by Yang Zhu and Mo Di? Han Yu claimed that Xunzi "promoted the Dao in an era when it was no longer transmitted," yet he spoke of it only in passing without elaboration. Was this truly because he had no time to refute the resentment of Xunzi’s doctrine of inherent evil, or was there something he did not fully understand? Perhaps he did not necessarily regard Xunzi’s words as inferior to Mencius’—or did he simply lack sufficient conviction?

Moreover, during the reign of King Xiang of Qi, Xunzi was revered as a venerable Confucian master—was there truly no one capable of debating the distinction between kingship and hegemony with him, yet all deferred to him as their teacher? Was this not because they were swayed by his rhetoric, such as his discourse on Duke Ai’s question to Yao, using it to dazzle and entice? Or was it because they were drawn to his techniques of divination, shamans, and belief in omens? Thus, later generations knew only the elegance of his words but rarely scrutinized them.

---

**On the *Classic of the Hidden Correspondence***

Historians speak of the "Yellow and Lao" as sharing the same Dao, yet the words of the Yellow Emperor himself are nowhere to be seen. As for the *Liezi*’s attribution of books to the Yellow Emperor, they largely align with Laozi’s teachings. Among transmitted texts, only the *Classic of the Hidden Correspondence* is claimed as a work of the Yellow Emperor. Its language is plain yet refined, profound yet essential—could such a text have been produced by anyone other than one who embodied the Dao?

In the Tang dynasty, Li Quan obtained it from Mount Song, stating that it had been hidden by Kou Qianzhi during the reign of Emperor Taiwu of the Northern Wei. Li Quan said: "A hundred words expound the Dao, a hundred words expound the law, and a hundred words expound the technique. The Dao pertains to immortals embracing the One; the law pertains to enriching the state and pacifying the people; and the technique pertains to strengthening the military and achieving victory." Yet in its phrasing and meaning, all three are invariably present.

Shao Yong considered it a Warring States text, while the Cheng brothers believed it could not have been written later than the end of the Shang or the end of the Zhou—certainly predating the Qin. For in the era of the former kings, when the sagely Dao was luminous, none dared to propagate heterodox teachings. But as the Zhou declined and the Dao became obscured, men of talent and intelligence rarely knew where to turn, so each relied on their own insights to speculate and establish their doctrines. Some orthodox teachings may have been impure or inconsistent, yet this text alone employs paradoxical language that aligns with orthodoxy—it must have been deliberately chosen.

Its great essence takes "ultimate non-being" as its宗, and the patterns of Heaven and Earth as its reckoning. It declares that all phenomena in the world arise from non-being, and that if one can return from being to non-being, all is well.

And Master Zhu [Xi] once remarked that this book might well have been written by Guan Yin—yet since Guan Yin was not profoundly versed in the Dao, it is certain that he could not have attained such depth. Cheng [Yi] and Shao [Yong] held it to be a text from the Shang or Zhou dynasties, and if it predates the Qin, then even the Tang masters could not have matched it. Is this not because Confucians avoid speaking of immortals and thus insist it was not written by the Yellow Emperor? Yet Master Zhu’s commentary, though it notes the text’s obscurity and convoluted phrasing, risks leading readers to see only its complexity and miss its unity.

Moreover, to observe the Dao of Heaven and grasp the workings of Heaven—one who can do so becomes one body with Heaven and Earth, and the Dao resides within. This is the root of the entire scripture. The principle of human nature and the human mind is one, yet their manifestations in the realm of mechanism are two. Establishing the Dao of Heaven to stabilize its mechanism, one finds that excess movement gives rise to transformation and reversal, and thus Heaven and humanity act in unison. Only by establishing the Dao to stabilize it can the foundations of the myriad transformations be secured, and movement and stillness naturally find their constancy. Even the path of cultivation and refinement may be understood thereby.

As for the Five Thieves, the Nine Orifices, the Three Essentials, generation and destruction, and mutual restraint—all arise from those who steal the mechanism, and once such theft is rectified, the Three Powers [Heaven, Earth, and Humanity] are at peace. The noble person remains steadfast in adversity and dares not act recklessly, while the petty person treats life lightly and invites calamity. For what cannot be seen or known—is this not the very essence of the ineffable and numinous? Thus sagely achievement arises from this, and divine illumination emerges therefrom.

Therefore, the threefold return through day and night restores one’s original state. Severing profit to guard the source, one transcends the distinction between day and night, life and death. With ears that hear well though blind and eyes that see well though deaf, the mind becomes single-minded, and its strategic power increases tenfold. Thus Heaven’s great benevolence arises from non-benevolence, and perfect impartiality resides in the utmost partiality. Life and death, benefit and harm—the Dao is no different in any case, for all are the natural principles of the cosmos.

Only those who attain the utmost joy and stillness can unite with the Dao of the threefold return, thereby moving and resting to rediscover it. What they perceive are the patterns of Heaven and Earth. Others see them as sages, yet my own grasp of the patterns of phenomena and seasons has never differed from that of Heaven and Earth—this is why it is called wisdom. Thus the foolish seek to deceive the sage with cunning, while I alone do not deceive the sage with foolishness, nor do I seek to make the sage appear extraordinary.

Therefore, the Dao of spontaneity is still, and from it Heaven, Earth, and the myriad things are born. The Dao of Heaven and Earth is gradual, so when yin and yang prevail, they push one another, and transformation proceeds in order. Moreover, Heaven and Earth generate the myriad things through perfect stillness, and their Dao grows gradually, so hardness and softness prevail, yin and yang push one another, and transformation is inexhaustible. Within this perfect stillness lies the Dao of spontaneity. The ceaseless generation of the myriad things—this is the marvelous vessel of Heaven and Earth. Even the calculations of musical temperament, calendrics, and hexagrams cannot fathom it, for it is the numinous mechanism hidden like a ghost.

The Eight Trigrams and the sexagenary cycle, as in Shao Yong’s theory of "inserting the heavenly stems," are all techniques of yin and yang prevailing over one another—these can be used to construct hexagram images. Their beginning and end, their patterns, are never disconnected. Yet the versions of the text transmitted by Master Chu [Yunü] and Master Cai [Yong] conclude with "the patterns of phenomena and seasons, wisdom," while the twenty-one sentences that follow are what Master Zhu [Xi] deeply valued. Thus the versions of Master Chu and Master Zhang [Zai] are the correct ones. Extending this reasoning, though the division into three chapters may be said to address distinct matters, it is only by illuminating the essence and extending its application—by establishing the essence so that its function may operate—that one can grasp the mechanism of movement and stillness, the discourse on life and death. Those who cannot do so cannot attain this.

It is certain that this is not the Yellow Emperor’s book, but must have been written by a reclusive gentleman of the Shang or Zhou dynasties. How could one be partial to the Dao, partial to techniques and doctrines? Thus concludes the first volume of *Xianquan Ji*.

[Textual notes: In the phrase *yì pào yān*, the character *pào* is suspected to be a misprint for *yì*. The character *mǎ* appears to be an error and should be corrected to *yān*. In the phrase *dúlì chí xíng ér bù gǎi zhě yě*, *chí* is likely a misprint for *tè*. The character *gǔ* should be corrected to *sú*. According to the *Records of the Grand Historian: The Grand Scribe’s Autobiography*, *xiǎo* should be corrected to *shǎo*.]

*Xianquan Ji*, Volume Two
Written by the Non-Action Heavenly Master of Mount Qi, Zhang Yuchu

**Preface to *The Veritable Record of the Primordial Supreme***

The establishment of the Dao as a teaching begins before Heaven and Earth, and its end remains unknown even after their formation. Its origins trace to the Most High, though the world transmits only that during the Yellow Emperor’s time, it was known as Master Guangcheng. The Emperor once sought the Dao at Mount Kongtong, later casting a tripod to refine the elixir before ascending on high.

Yet when examining the records and other texts, they all state that he was born in the Yin dynasty and served as the Keeper of the Royal Archives in Zhou, later becoming the Archivist of the Treasury. After eighty-odd years—though Sima Qian claims over two hundred—he was known as a reclusive gentleman, posthumously styled *Chi*. Confucius once traveled to Zhou to inquire of him about ritual. When Zhou declined, he departed for Qin, passing through Hangu Pass, where the magistrate Yin Xi, having observed the qi, welcomed him and urged him to write. Thus, he composed the *Dao and Virtue* in two scrolls to transmit to the world.

According to genealogical records, some say he was Li Qian of the Zhou, whose wife, a daughter of the Yishou clan named Yingfu, bore a son named Er, styled Boyang. Others claim the Li clan descended from Gaoyang’s son Tingjian, who served as Grand Judge under Yao and took the surname *Li* from his office. Later, Li Zheng’s wife, Qie of the He clan, fled punishment under Zhou and survived by eating the fruit of the *li* tree, hence changing their name to *Li*. Still others say Li Lingfei attained the Dao, and his wife, Lady Yin, gave birth to Laozi, whose personal name was Hongyuan, styled Yaoling, also called Boyang, Lao Laizi, or Grand Scribe Dan. The accounts vary.

As for the Yellow Emperor’s predecessors, they existed from the very beginning of the Three Sovereigns’ era, and the transmission of their divine transformations has continued unceasingly across hundreds of millennia. Thus, the compilation of this *Veritable Record* must have its basis.

The spirit knows no bounds—it may precede or follow, exist or not exist—viewing a thousand years as but a single breath. It cannot be measured by form or sought through traces. Therefore, that which cannot be spoken is called the Dao; by namelessness, one observes the beginning of Heaven and Earth; by desirelessness, one perceives its mystery. Thus, one engages in the affairs of non-action and practices the teaching of no-words, causing the people to be without knowledge or desire through non-contention and non-theft. This was how, as Zhou declined, Laozi sought to restore customs and save the age by returning to simplicity.

Had he, at that time of moral decay, instead instructed the people in the ways of names, desires, action, and words, how could they have known that the crooked is made whole, the bent is made straight, the hollow is filled, and the worn is renewed? Thus, he stabilized the age with the nameless uncarved block, so that none could distinguish Dao from benevolence and righteousness—thereby restoring antiquity.

His words emerged in the aftermath of Qin’s tyranny, and when Han arose, its governance was aided by purity and stillness, like water quenching fire. Was this not the precedence of Huang-Lao over the Six Classics? Speaking of the trends of the time, Laozi valued the Dao’s emptiness and non-being, adapting to change through non-action. Hence, his writings, subtle and profound, are indeed difficult to fathom.

Later rulers of the world adopted his teachings as their model. What remained of his legacy was enshrined as the *Celestial Canon* and *Cavern Records*, revealed in *Numinous Writings* and *Hidden Instructions*. Cultivated in the self, it enables transcendence and ascent to the heavens; applied to others, it saves the world and benefits all things—thus encompassing the Dao of both inner sageliness and outer kingship.

Yet if one clings to the theories of great scholars and masters, reducing it to the likes of Zhuangzi and Liezi, dispersing the *Dao and Virtue* into mere discourse, or worse, conflating it with the Legalist and Nominalist schools of Shen Buhai and Han Fei, this is entirely misguided. Even more extreme is the error of dismissing it as mere emptiness and delusion.

Nevertheless, the *Veritable Record*’s meticulous and comprehensive account could not have arisen from private veneration alone, deceiving the world and enduring so long. Now, our August Emperor, endowed with Heaven’s mandate and benevolent sagacity, has first annotated the *Dao and Virtue* in two scrolls, employing it to govern with purity and tranquility. What greater fortune could there be for our Dao?

In the fifteenth year of the Hongwu reign, the Bureau of Daoist Registrars was established, and our fellow mountain dweller, Master Cao Xiwu, assumed office. His virtuous conduct and sincerity have earned him imperial favor surpassing all previous ages—could this not be due to his support of the transformative power of purity and non-action?

In his leisure, he examined and corrected this record, commissioning its printing to broaden its transmission, and asked me to compose this preface. Though I am unworthy and tremble at the task, how could I refuse to set forth its beginnings? Yet the Dao of divine transformation and mysterious penetration—what the sage leaves unspoken beyond the Six Directions—is glimpsed herein. With reverence, I write this preface.

---

**Preface to *The Gazetteer of Mount Longhu***

The Dao lies hidden in the most subtle yet manifests in the most evident. Heaven and Earth are vast, their yin and yang revolving in cycles, yet principle is made manifest therein. The myriad things flourish and decline in endless succession, yet pattern is made manifest therein. This subtlest of mechanisms lies concealed within the most evident, unseen by mortals. Only after persisting for a thousand years without extinction does its truth become known.

That which is called the Dao—empty, formless, profound, and silent—originates in Heaven. Within the boundless and indistinct, its essence and signs reside. Though unseen, it may yet be known.

Our Most High’s teaching, from the time of the Yellow Emperor and the Wen and Jing emperors onward, has consistently employed its principles of purity and non-action, proven effective in the world.
