Some compare it to the Legalist and Nominalist schools of Shen Buhai and Han Fei, while others dismiss it as the strange and absurd doctrines of fangshi. Thus, there are those who condemn and reject it as heterodoxy.

Yet Sima Qian observed that its movements align with the formless, sustaining the myriad things, its guidance simple and easily grasped. The Daoist school has indeed existed since antiquity. Thus, the words of Guan Yin and Zhuangzi and Liezi have further elucidated what was incomplete. Their teachings of softness, humility, and withdrawal were sufficient to remedy Zhou’s decline, guiding the world and shaping customs. How could one regard them as mere profundities?

Moreover, the establishment of spiritual teachings as guidance—how could it deceive later generations to lead them astray? From the Warring States onward, Mao Jun of Qin, our ancestral Celestial Master of Han, Xu and Ge of Wu—all stand as its most illustrious exemplars. The Celestial Master, endowed with a once-in-an-era disposition, received the transmission directly from the Most High. Thus, the esoteric transmissions of the Three Caverns’ scriptures, talismanic methods—once concealed within the nine heavens and ten poles—descended in full to the world. He then journeyed to Wu in Shu, where he refined the elixir upon Dragon-Tiger Mountain. This mountain, renowned throughout the realm, ranks among the blessed lands, and is celebrated alongside the Three Maos and Mount Jian. Since the Marquis of Liu of our Zhang clan, forty-eight generations have passed—surpassing even these two mountains in distinction.

Through the Wei, Jin, Tang, and Song dynasties, each era bestowed honors and ranks in full measure. Whether in the splendor of its mountains and rivers, the magnificence of its temples, the flourishing of its people; the marvels of its immortal traces, the numinous efficacy of its Daoist practices, or the eminence of its titles and prestige—each generation has admired, extolled, and immortalized them in poetry and prose, engraving them in metal and stone. The transmission has endured for over two thousand years, growing ever more prosperous with time, its influence expanding ever further. Was this not due to the boundless virtue of past generations—relieving the suffering of beings, averting disasters, and shielding from calamities—whose hidden blessings sustained the people, subtly aiding royal transformation, and thus ensuring an unbroken lineage of such enduring renown? Or was it also the profound favor of the mountain and river spirits, nurturing and protecting them to achieve this?

Thus, the *Mountain Records* cannot be left unrecorded. In the spring of the second year of Huangqing of the Yuan, Master Wu, the hereditary preceptor of the Mysterious Teaching, compiled it into three scrolls and presented it to the court. The imperial decree ordered Minister Yuan, Wenqing, and Minister Cheng, Wenxian, to compose its preface. My great-grandfather, Master Liu, and my grandfather, Master Taixuan, basked in imperial favor, their glory resplendent in their time, honored in the annals of records—a distinction of the highest order. My late father, Master Chongxu, in the early reign of the previous emperor, was repeatedly summoned to the imperial presence, where profound grace was bestowed upon him. As for myself, though unworthy, I early inherited the teachings, received exceptional favor, and oversaw the renovation of the temple halls. Later, upon the enthronement of the present emperor, I was first to receive the imperial summons, enjoying the boundless benevolence and vast blessings of two reigns—gifts beyond full recounting.

Upon reviewing the old records, I found them crude, shallow, and commonplace, and felt a deep sense of regret. Some said: "In this flourishing age, if we do not delve into antiquity and seek out hidden truths to compile a grand record of the mountain’s glory, would we not allow its rare fragrance and noble footsteps to fade into obscurity, like a pearl buried in the depths, its precious essence neglected and uncherished—would this not be self-abandonment?" Though my resolve is earnest, my learning falls short. I thus commanded Instructor Li Tangzhen, whose cultivation is pure and sincere, to search for missing materials. My second brother, Yuqing, with keen determination and sharp talent, vigorously assisted in the endeavor. The work was then divided into ten scrolls. As it neared completion, a virtuous gentleman offered to sponsor its printing—how could I not contribute a preface to commemorate this?

The Dao in the world moves in harmony with heaven and earth without conflict; as it resides in humanity, it is nothing less than flourishing and great. To record this without neglect is to worthily inherit its established legacy and extend its abundance to future generations. It should endure as long as the mountains and rivers—how could it ever end? Yet as eras change and ages pass, records may not capture everything in full. Still, those who admire its lingering influence and distant traces may yet find value in this work.

Respectfully, I presume to place this preface at the beginning.

---

**Preface to the Genealogy of the Han Celestial Master’s Lineage**

The *Book of Changes* states: "Manifest the Dao, and the numinous power of virtue is enacted—thus may one partake in divine communion and receive spiritual protection." That which fills heaven and earth, enduring through past and present without cease, is the Dao. To make the Dao manifest is to reveal its wondrous functions in full, aligning with virtue—thus may one respond to all changes and assist the divine. This is what is meant by "assisting heaven and earth in their transformative nurturing."

Sima Qian said: "The Daoist school practices non-action, concentrates the spirit, moves in accord with the formless, sustains the myriad things, adapts to the times, responds to transformations, establishes customs, and applies its methods—nothing is unsuitable." How true this is! The Most High was born in the Yin dynasty and served as the Keeper of Archives for Zhou, later becoming the Historian Beneath the Pillar. Through unfathomable and divine transformations, he descended upon the world in successive ages as the founder of the Mysterious Teaching. Our ancestor, the Han Celestial Master, inheriting the legacy of the Marquis of Liu, personally received the Dao from the Most High. Thus, the hidden and untransmitted scriptures of the immortal canons and cavern records descended fully into the world. Thereafter, he subdued demons and spirits, refined the divine elixir, achieved transcendence, and transmitted the sword and seal to his descendants—a lineage now spanning fifteen hundred years. Though dynasties have changed and things have transformed, the transmission has never faltered. Is this not because the teachings of purity and non-action, with their numinous efficacy, align with the celestial virtue, and thus suffice to assist in transformation and nurturing?

To this day, in every renowned mountain and secluded region, immortal traces remain. The far-reaching fragrance of his descendants and the enduring glory of their lineage clearly attest to their hidden support of royal governance and their boundless beneficence toward the people. From the late Han onward, those dwelling on Dragon-Tiger Mountain lived in seclusion among cliffs and valleys, cultivating and refining to prolong their years. By the early Song, their Daoist practices gradually gained renown. During the Daoguan and Chongning eras, the Perfected Lord of Emptiness and Stillness emerged. His divine works and wondrous responses, once manifested in the practice of refining qi and cultivating form, thereafter flourished even more, rivaling the achievements of the past.

Among his descendants, none shone brighter than my great-grandfather, Master Weishan, and my grandfather, Master Taixuan. Their illustrious deeds in rituals and prayers, their encounters with imperial favor at its zenith—during that time, their marvelous signs and abundant traces were revered even by scholar-officials, surpassing what came before.

In our present dynasty, my late father, Master Chongxu, flourished under the sagely court, which had unified the realm and all beneath heaven. His lofty standing and towering prestige shone brilliantly in his time, his honors enduring from beginning to end—how could those of former eras compare? Such must be the legacy of a lineage blessed by divine luminaries.

I, unworthy and of mediocre talent, have inherited this illustrious legacy, yet my undeserved favor from the throne fills me with trembling awe. As the history of our lineage remains obscure to the world, I fear its omissions and gaps. In earlier days, while attending my father, I received from him an old manuscript, which I entrusted to the eminent Daoist Tongxu. I sought a preface from the Grand Historian Song Lian, yet had no leisure to compile and edit it for circulation. The original text, however, was verbose and cumbersome; thus, I have presumptuously revised, abridged, and rearranged it for publication, hoping to fulfill my father’s wishes.

Alas! The principle of all things is that they wax and wane. To bear the past forward and open the way for the future is no easy task. Since the latter ages, many have abandoned substance for superficiality, vying for power and profit. If one cannot attain mastery in learning and radiate the brilliance of virtue to exalt one’s lineage, how can they be said to carry on the work of their forebears? How could they even begin to comprehend it?

What was transmitted by the Most High—its exalted virtue and conduct, its monumental achievements—ensures its perpetuation without end. Yet how is this so? It is because the divine and luminous reside in the person. Those who come after must strive with diligence, and if they can expound and continue this tradition, only then will its teachings not fall into oblivion.

This work is titled *Genealogical Records*, following the example of historical texts.

**Postscript to the Zhang Lineage Genealogy**

In ancient times, those who received surnames and clan names were all descendants of sages and worthies. Some took their names from states, and though the states perished, the names endured. Over time, lineages faded and clans were lost—this was not uncommon. The system of genealogical records was overseen by a bureau established by historians. When the bureau was abolished, scholar-official families compiled their own genealogies to order generational ranks, distinguish degrees of kinship, and ensure that all descendants preserved the reverence for ancestors and respect for lineage. This is among the greatest duties of the noble-minded.

The Zhang clan traces its lineage to the Ji surname, descending from Qingyang, son of the Yellow Emperor. His fifth son, Hui, served as Minister of Bows, inventing the bow and arrow. He used nets to hunt birds and beasts, presided over the worship of the Fox Star, and his descendants perpetuated this office, thus being granted the surname Zhang. In the Yin dynasty, Zhang Muzi, and in the time of Emperor Yao, Zhang Guo, both gained fame as immortals. By the reign of King Xuan of Zhou, Zhang Zhong served as a minister, and his descendant Zhang Hou became a grandee of Jin. When the Three Families partitioned Jin, the Zhang clan served as ministers to Han for five generations. Sima Qian recorded that the Zhangs were originally of the Han royal clan. After Han’s fall, Zhang Liang, the Marquis of Liu, avenged the First Emperor of Qin and changed his surname. The old genealogy begins with the Marquis of Liu as the founding ancestor, with no records preceding him. Having examined its outline, I have sought to supplement its omissions.

The Marquis of Liu was originally from Fengyi in Pei. He aided Emperor Gaozu in establishing the Han dynasty, was enfeoffed as a marquis, and his lineage prospered for seven generations, many of whose meritorious deeds were recorded in history. By the Tang dynasty, the Zhangs were listed among forty-three eminent clans—including Anding, Fanyang, Taiyuan, Nanyang, Dunhuang, Xiuwu, Shanggu, Peiguo, Liangguo, Rongyang, Pingyuan, and Jingzhao—producing seventeen chancellors and eight marquises. In the tenth generation, the Celestial Master of Han, the True Man of Tongbai, was born. He moved from Pei to Hangzhou, passing through Dantu County in Zhenjiang, where there stood a temple to the Marquis of Liu. In Changzhou, the immortal site known as Zhanggong Cave also bore his name, likely a shrine to his beneficence. Thereafter, the family settled in Zhejiang.

The Celestial Master of Han was born on Tianmu Mountain in Hangzhou, becoming the founder of the Orthodox Unity tradition. Through his refinement of divine elixirs and exorcism of demonic forces, he journeyed to Shu and eventually settled on Longhushan in Xin[zhou]. His lineage flourished, surpassing all that came before. Historians, unaware of these developments, assumed that the Marquis of Liu’s line had ended when his state was abolished in the second generation. Little did they know of the thriving branches that spread across the north and south.

From the tenth generation of the Celestial Master onward, the tombs remained intact, and the generational records preserved. Those residing on Longhushan numbered twenty-eight generations. Including the distant ancestors of the Marquis of Liu, whose origins are unknown, the lineage spans over fifty generations. As for the divergences among branches, the distinctions between prominence and obscurity, and their various fates—these cannot be fully traced.

Some traditions hold that the Zhang clans of Shixiao in Min and Shangrao in Xin[zhou] share common ancestry. When questioned, their descendants and elders could still recount old tales, yet when pressed for generational records, no evidence could be found. As for the inquiries across the regions east and west of the Yangtze—where lineages of lofty rank and esteemed repute have flourished, and where the imperial court’s reverence has never waned—few can rival them. Could this enduring prosperity and longevity arise without the divine efficacy and transformative power of their ancestral line, whose virtue has spread across the four directions?

Yet I have often lamented how those in the world who crave fame and praise fabric genealogies to dazzle others. In their eagerness to claim kinship with the noble and illustrious, they resort to forced connections and convoluted proofs. Where, then, lies their true filial piety? Our Zhang lineage, descended from divine luminaries, has endured for over a thousand years. Yet commentators dismiss it merely as a succession of empty titles within an esoteric tradition—unaware that the transmission of jade tablets and sacrificial vessels has continued unbroken across generations. Perhaps the fault lies in the insufficiency of records. Still, that its full glory remains obscured stems from earlier generations’ lack of resolve in seeking the root. Thus, even the most learned scholars of the time left no written account of it—a profound regret.

Today, our lineage’s descendants are but a fraction of what they once were. The ebb and flow of fortune evoke both sorrow and joy, yet how can we neglect the task of compilation? I, however, am of mediocre talent and unworthy of this burden, ever fearful that the thread may snap, leaving future generations ignorant of their origins. How then will they honor their lineage and safeguard its foundation?

Some say that in compiling genealogies, the close should be detailed and the distant abbreviated. Each descendant should elaborate on their immediate kin, so that even the abbreviated entries may be inferred through mutual reference. Later generations must grasp the weight of their ancestors’ virtue and legacy, dedicating themselves to its preservation, ensuring that its influence and grace do not fade. Only then can they truly inherit its goodness.

The words of Han Xiang, who urged reverence for family records and remembrance of ancestral glory, reflect the essence of learning—how can we not take heed? Thus, the ancient system of genealogies, designed to strengthen the root, was not established in vain. Here, I append these reflections to the end of this compilation, tracing its origins, while leaving the task of full illumination to the great hands of our age.

**Postscript to the Recorded Sayings of the Thirtieth Celestial Master, the Vacuous Tranquility Perfected Lord**

Within the cosmos, the purest essence of light and mountain-spirit coalesces in humanity alone. When one cultivates the self, what lies within inevitably manifests without. How, then, can one who harbors such inner abundance remain silent? Even the teachings of Laozi and Zhuangzi—who cast aside the body, subdued the senses, and rejected all external attachments, turning wholly inward—required words to convey and establish their doctrines for posterity. From this, we see that even those who wander beyond the mundane world do not deem words useless.

Their words, like clouds drifting across the vast sky or ripples on a still pond, form patterns with their movement, yet defy pursuit by mere traces. This differs from the words of Confucians, whose expressions range from bold and unrestrained when successful to melancholic and lamenting when in adversity. Nevertheless, the Dao’s profound spiritual transformations, which endure as long as heaven, are not defined by the presence or absence of words—how then could one judge immortality by them?

The Thirtieth Ancestor, the Vacuous Tranquility Perfected Lord, possessed innate wisdom and karmic roots, flourishing in an era of harmony. During the Chongning and Jingkang reigns, Emperor Huizong’s devotion to the Dao reached its peak, and true immortals emerged in abundance. Among them were Xu Shenfang, Wang Wenqing, and Lin Lingsu, who stood alongside the Perfected Lord in that age. Whether subduing malevolent spirits, calming turbulent tides, or receiving imperial honors, their deeds bore extraordinary signs.

As the nation faced hardship, the Perfected Lord foresaw omens of disaster and, with a mere gesture, summoned wind, thunder, and timely rains. His divine power was so manifest that he became a paragon of the celestial court, his blessings extending to all beings. Though centuries have passed, people still speak of him with admiration—not like those who sought fleeting fame, only to vanish without a trace.

The *Record of Responses and Transformations* meticulously documents his meritorious deeds. His teachings, which guided the world, and his verses, which refined the spirit, continue to awaken the deluded and dispel ignorance. Had he not roamed beyond the primordial chaos of the cosmic egg, shed the dust of the mundane world, and attained a state utterly free of even the slightest impurity, could his attainments have reached such heights?

Those across the land who recite and long to see his works regret their incompleteness. Though they were once published, much has since been lost. I have sought them in sacred mountains and re-engraved them to broaden their transmission. May scholars and seekers alike delve into the meaning beyond his words, awakening to the secrets of the "fire talisman" and plumbing the mysteries of lead and mercury. In this, they will find an enduring teacher, advancing toward the realm of Dao and virtue.

In the vast wilderness, by the shores of the formless, amidst towering peaks and ancient trees, on evenings when the wind is clear and the moon bright—who can fathom the descent of rainbow banners and cloud-borne sashes as one chants his ethereal verses, where forests echo and springs respond? Should his words not endure alongside those of Laozi and Zhuangzi?

I, Yu Chu, unworthy heir to this lineage, am ill-equipped to fully extol his virtues. Fearing omissions, I offer this brief preface, awaiting the brush of a master.

**Preface to the *You Ran Pavilion Collection***

All things, from their first sprouting and budding, embody the intent of ceaseless generation. Yet as they grow in abundance and grandeur, few recognize this truth. What we perceive are merely the shifting hues of flourishing and withering through the seasons—we have not grasped their origins.

If all things are thus, how much more so the human heart, which even fewer examine? The goodness inherent in human nature—once consciousness takes shape—exists in all, quietly abiding within, no different from the Way of things. Yet private desires and worldly concerns obscure this truth, just as the flourishing and withering of seasons cannot be escaped. Only by perceiving these obscurations and dissolving desires entirely does the original goodness of the serene heart remain. Thus, the path of learning lies in embracing benevolence and righteousness, upholding ritual and discipline, to preserve its center and restore its origin. Then the serene heart within—like the kernel within a fruit—exists in us without question.

For a scholar to establish himself and honor his kin is not to seek success through such means; otherwise, it cannot be called true learning. In Poyang and the western prefectures of Jiangxi, wealthy merchants expanded their fortunes through trade. During the Five Dynasties, eminent scholars and great families flourished, with the Zhou clan being particularly prominent. By the Yuan dynasty, Nan Weng, Director of the Bureau of Scholarly Worthies, was renowned for his learning. Dwelling among the lakes and fields, where mountains gleamed and waters shimmered, he delighted in the scenery and built the You Ran Pavilion there, perhaps inspired by the poetry of Tao Yuanming of Jin. Soon after, he entered court, where officials and scholars all composed tributes in his honor.

Initially, both Nan Weng and his elder, Left Vice Director Bo Wen, held illustrious positions, their clan prosperous and numerous. Their kinsman, Langzhong, upheld the family legacy without fail. When war erupted at the end of the Yuan, the pavilion was destroyed, but upon Langzhong’s return from official service, he recovered and preserved the relics of past generations as if they were his ancestors’ own handiwork. I, humbled by my deep bond with Langzhong, was shown his collection and entrusted with these words.

This compilation shines with literary brilliance, reflecting Nan Weng’s prosperous era, though the name *You Ran* was chosen in his days of obscurity. Its various compositions—flavored with the praises of renowned officials and scholars—celebrate his later success. Though his affection for his homeland’s lakes and mountains may differ from Tao Yuanming’s seclusion, the enduring legacy of his family and the serene heart he cultivated, expressed through landscape and inspiration, set him apart. Nevertheless, the vicissitudes of scenery, like the cycles of flourishing and withering, are bound by the principles of qi and reason. Those with deep roots have profound origins, just as the longevity of a family depends on the depth of its accumulated virtue.

During the height of the Yuan, scholars from all directions gathered like clouds, yet how many of the Zhou clan’s contemporaries remain today? Langzhong not only continued their legacy but also reassembled this collection to inspire future generations, revealing the serene heart that lies within. My ancestor, Tai Xuan, shared a generational bond with Nan Weng and Bo Wen, and I have been honored with Langzhong’s friendship across three generations—how could I refuse this request? If his descendants cherish this work, the legacy of *You Ran* will transcend the constraints of prosperity and decline, for it springs from the boundless manifestation of perfect virtue.

Thus, I offer this preface in respectful response to his request.

---

**Preface to the *Essentials of the Elixir Manual***

When the writings of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi emerged, our Dao flourished. Historical records tell of the Yellow Emperor seeking the Way from Guangchengzi on Mount Kongtong, whose teachings—though neither mystical nor abstruse—were concise, essential, and readily understood and practiced. Later, King Mu of Zhou constructed the Grass Pavilion to welcome recluses, elevating their esteem. Masters like Guan Yin and Zhuangzi, active during the Warring States, differed in their approaches according to the times, yet their transmission shared a single source.

At its root lie the Three Caverns, Nine Tiers, and the various scriptures of the Daoist Canon. Its application in the world is the Way of inner sageliness and outer kingship. For instance, the governance of the Duke of Zhou and Cao Shen through tranquility exemplifies this. Its essence lies in returning from existence to non-existence, from fullness to emptiness, following the flow of primordial qi, deepening the root and firming the stem, returning to the origin and reverting to the source. Thus, nature and life merge, one guards the singular truth, and transcends into the formless.

By the time of the First Emperor of Qin and Emperor Wu of Han, they were deluded by alchemical arts, and the teachings became confused. Later, the so-called *Golden Elixir* and *Great Cinnabar* originated with the *Golden Green Dragon-Tiger Scripture* and the *Supreme Stone Wall Song*. During the Han, Wei Boyang, drawing from the *Golden Green Scripture*, composed the *Triplex Unity*, using the *Book of Changes* to elucidate the elixir. He expounded on the five phases, eight trigrams, yin-yang, and the warp and weft of cosmic principles, detailing the mechanisms of fire, medicine, growth, and transformation. Some cited minerals, plants, and herbs to illustrate substance and function; others employed metaphors of infants and transcendent beasts to conceal profound mysteries.

Those who followed, however, drowned in metaphorical language about wood and stone, losing sight of the essential meaning and misleading the world. Not only did the transmissions of inner and outer alchemy become obscured, but those unversed in their exploration dismissed them as bizarre and deceitful, dismissing the true principles of body, mind, nature, and life. Such distortions not only misled their own time but also left lasting harm for future generations.

The transmission of outer alchemy involves refining the essence of the five metals and eight minerals according to the fire phases and the *Yin Convergence*, no different in principle from the ascent and descent of inner alchemy. Thus, the workings of inner and outer alchemy are one. Before birth, the human form belongs to yin, formed by the congealment of essence and qi. It must draw upon the essence of metals and minerals, the radiance of clouds and sunlight, to refine and transform, achieving the marvel of lightness and flight. Hence, among famous mountains and rivers, remnants of elixir furnaces and medicinal cauldrons remain. The legacies of our ancestors—the Celestial Master of Han, Historian Xu, and Immortal Ge—are particularly renowned. To abandon these two transmissions is to embrace falsehood.

In the late Yuan, unscrupulous individuals promoted heretical teachings on "sexual arts," compiling spurious texts under the guise of venerable traditions to deceive the world.

Wealthy merchants and powerful magnates often pursue these practices in hopes of prolonging life, yet their indulgence in desire inevitably leads to the ruin of body and fate. How dire must be the karmic retribution for those who first propagated such teachings! I have long wished to eradicate this pernicious influence, yet my reach has not been wide enough.

Others rely on texts concerning herbs, clouds, mists, massage, or Daoist breathing exercises, treating them as shortcuts to mastery. Though these may nurture longevity, to seek true accomplishment through them is as futile as climbing a tree to catch a fish or carrying firewood to extinguish a blaze—nothing more than the proliferation of heretical doctrines and reckless conduct.

There are the Northern and Southern Lineages: the Quanzhen School venerates Wang Chongyang of the Golden Perfection, while the Southern Lineage follows Zhang Ziyang. Zhang’s writings are abundant yet veiled, while Wang’s are direct and concise. Zhang’s transmission requires the integration of inner and outer alchemy to achieve fruition, whereas Wang’s focuses solely on inner cultivation. Yet the profound secrets of their teachings must be pursued with diligent inquiry, for they cannot be fully conveyed through texts alone.

I inherited this tradition under the gracious and munificent favor of the August Emperor, who urged me to advance in the study of the Daoist lineage. For over a decade now, I have traveled far and wide, engaging in discussion and seeking counsel from masters of the four directions, never daring to rest. Yet my humble talents and limited understanding have left me unable to fully repay the imperial benevolence or illuminate the radiance of our teachings. This compilation, titled *Essentials of Elixir Refinement*, gathers the scriptures, treatises, poems, and formulas I have studied over the years—from the divine masters and patriarchs of antiquity down to the present—examining their differences and selecting their essentials to serve as a guiding standard for learners. It is not merely for ease of study but aims to elucidate the principles of nature and destiny, the vital essence of spirit and qi, uniting all in one comprehensive whole. Through recitation and contemplation, one may avoid the pitfalls of erroneous teachings and need not heed the derision of those who seek worldly utility or universal benefit. In this way, the transmission of the Yellow Emperor, Laozi, Zhuangzi, and Liezi may yet remain unbroken. If this work is disseminated widely and enduringly, the blessings of our Dao may reach all. Moreover, for those who retreat to the mountains and forests, accumulating practice day by day without cease, how can the attainment of transcendence and soaring through the heavens remain unattainable? Yet this depends on those of superior capacity and deep karmic roots, who must realize it through their own cultivation.

Herein lies the preface to the *Commentary on the Scripture of the Generation of the Divine Chapters*.

The *Book of Changes* states: "The divine is that which wondrously brings forth the myriad things." It accomplishes transformation and governs the spirits; its divinity is boundless, so profound that even the sage cannot fully comprehend it. The *Changes* speaks of the divine in the most absolute terms. All things arise from the singular qi, which divides to form the Supreme Ultimate, the Two Modes, the Four Images, and the Five Phases, each occupying its place within qi. From the distribution of the Five Phases’ qi, the ceaseless generation of the ten thousand things unfolds. In the movement of the Five Phases, yang culminates in nine, yin in six; the principles of two, five, nine, and one are all contained within the Five Phases. One is the root of all numbers, and qi is the mother of all existence. The flow of qi is governed by the divine, from which all transformations emerge. Heaven is formed by the accumulation of qi, and the Emperor is named for his governance of the divine.

From the singular qi arise the three qi, from the three arise the nine qi, and from the nine qi comes boundless generation, filling the six directions—all transformations of qi, which we call the various heavens. These heavens return to emptiness and nothingness, for they cannot be sought through form. Thus, the establishment of our teachings is rooted in emptiness and spontaneity, which are none other than spirit and qi. To know how to nurture spirit and qi is to perfect the Way of nature and destiny. Though the methods of cultivation may vary, their origin in the Three Caverns and Nine Heavens is one.

The three realms are vast and obscure, their talismans forming scriptures unlike the mundane discourses of the human world. The *Scripture of the Generation of the Divine Chapters in the Nine Heavens* is profound in its meaning, its divisions and phrases revealing the deepest mysteries. Each chapter governs an original destiny and corresponds to a heaven, exhausting the path of refinement. Internally, it cultivates the self; externally, it delivers souls. Its recitation—nine cycles and seven repetitions—follows numerical subtleties that can lead to spontaneous attainment, ascending to the celestial court in broad daylight. Yet this is not merely the result of recitation.

Ten completes the cycle of numbers, upon which the creation of heaven and earth depends. The human body embodies the creation of heaven and earth—how could the Way of transcendence and response remain unattainable? The text adheres to the principles of golden elixir and return to the origin, for nine is the number of yang. The wondrous teachings of the Primordial Beginning and the petitions of the Divine Kings are most solemn in their warnings of calamities and karmic seeds. Since the eras of Red Clarity and Kaihuang, the celestial bureaus have conducted their selections, ascending step by step. Those who neither cultivate virtuous conduct nor seek the supreme Way—how could they not feel profound awe and trepidation? Those who do cultivate may not only transcend the kalpas and achieve perfection but also ascend to the ranks of the divine, for this arises from nurturing qi, refining spirit, returning the nine qi to the myriad spirits, and merging the three realms into one qi.

This is what is meant by knowing the divinity of the divine, without which one cannot grasp the source of its divinity. Regarding the discourse on life and death, refining the self’s spirit can deliver the departed. Though this manifests the boundless compassion of the Most High, it also stems from the immeasurable merit of self-cultivation. Thus, this scripture harmonizes with the *Scripture of Salvation*, the *Great Cavern*, and the *Scripture of Deliverance from Suffering*, their inner and outer meanings fused as one. Those who diligently recite and study it, interpreting its words to fathom its depths, will daily deepen their understanding of the Dao.

Dong Guishan of Qingyuan has provided clear and insightful annotations, serving as a bridge between the seen and unseen worlds. In my leisure, I have commissioned its carving and printing to broaden its transmission, a deed of profound kindness and beneficence. When asked to write a preface, how could I presume to expound its principles and guide all toward reverence, whether in this world or beyond? Yet by adhering to its teachings and cultivating without cease, one may eventually attain spiritual transformation, soaring through the void, returning one’s celestial nature to the realm of emptiness and nothingness, and roaming the heights of purity and clarity. This depends on those of superior capacity and deep karmic roots, who must realize it through their own evidence.

Thus, this preface is placed at the beginning.

**Preface to the Record of Mount Wuyi**

Those who compile geographical records and local gazettes often overlook the renowned mountains and great rivers, their numinous traces and distant wanderings left insufficiently detailed. This is not due to gaps in the historical records, but rather because the learned scholars of later ages, wary of empty exaggerations, have shunned such accounts. Yet the immortals and perfected beings of antiquity—wherever their divine presence dwells—manifest everywhere. Their extraordinary powers to avert disasters and ward off calamities, their unfailing responses to prayers, surely contribute in hidden ways to the imperial order. How could they lack merit in aiding the world? Yet if historians fail to record them or chroniclers neglect their details, then after a thousand years, their traces may fade into oblivion. Should not those who study the teachings devote themselves to preserving such knowledge?

Wuyi in Jianning is the Grotto-Heaven of Ascending Perfection and Mystic Transformation. According to the *Records of Hongjian* from the Jin dynasty, a divine being once descended upon the mountain’s summit, claiming the title "Lord of Wuyi" after receiving the Emperor’s mandate to govern the earthbound immortals. The mountain thus took its name. Yet the *Biographies of the Immortals* in the *Transmissions of Bai Ziqing* states that a man of Qin named Qian Keng once secluded himself there with his two sons, the elder named Wu and the younger Yi. The truth of these accounts remains unresolved.

The *Han Treatise on Sacrifices* records that Emperor Wu of Han once worshipped the Lord of Wuyi in Jianning, entrusting the rites to his sacrificial officials. Remnants of the Han-era altar still stand on the mountain. Some say that on the fifteenth day of the eighth month in the second year of the First Emperor of Qin, the Supreme Emperor, along with the Primordial Lady of Mount Taimu and the Perfected Wei Zijian, convened a gathering atop the Peak of the Canopy Pavilion. A rainbow bridge spanned the heavens, and hundreds of figures ascended in single file. They addressed the locals as "descendants" and feasted them with wine and fruit, their chambers adorned with brocade, their furnishings resplendent, celestial music resounding. Then, as wind and rain arose, they vanished without a trace.

Zijian had first studied the Dao on this mountain, learning alongside Zhang Zhan, Sun Chuo, and eleven others. They encountered an immortal riding a crane and received the teachings, all ascending to immortality. From the Tang and Song through the Yuan dynasties, those who attained transcendence here emerged in every generation.

The Chongyou Abbey was first established during the Tianbao era of the Tang. By the eighth year of Baoda in the Southern Tang, Li Xuanfu, having attained realization in the Dao, relocated it to its present site and was granted the name "Assembly of Immortals." In the early Song, its halls and corridors were renovated. Emperor Taizong honored it with an imperial edict, and in the second year of Xiangfu, it was endowed with fields.

In the early Xining era, an official of the second rank was appointed to oversee it. During the Shaosheng, Yuanfu, Duanping, Jiaxi, and Shaoding reigns, prayers for rain were invariably answered, prompting repeated bestowals of honorific titles and grants of land and tax exemptions. In the Tianli era of the Yuan, imperial favor was again conferred as before, and those of pure conduct and steadfast virtue never ceased to emerge. Thus, its renown spread throughout the realm, rivaling even the Three Mountains in fame.

The region is known as "Cinnabar Mountain and Azure Waters," where extraordinary peaks and splendid ridges emerge in succession along the winding river, their cliffs and hues embracing one another in a grand encircling embrace. Caves, grottoes, and sacred altars abound beyond enumeration, each a site of unparalleled beauty, and which among them has not been the dwelling of transcendent beings and extraordinary figures? Thus, luminaries such as Master Zhu Wen, Minister Cai Wenjie, and Academician Liu Wenjian—eminent scholars and great Confucians of their time—also studied and lectured here. That the splendor of the Nine Bends is renowned across the four directions is no mere coincidence.

I have often marveled at the gatherings of the two Directors Yang and Xu on Mount Sanmao within the Huayang Grotto-Heaven, or at the assembly of Ma Gu with Cai Jing and Fangping upon the immortal’s altar. Consulting the records, even Liu Xiang of the Han, famed for his exhaustive mastery of texts, did not speak falsely. From this, we know that the gatherings at Manting were not baseless, and how could they go unrecorded? The mountain’s annals are meticulously researched and detailed, truly worthy of transmission to posterity.

I have long wished to visit but have yet to fulfill this desire. The abbey’s superintendent, so-and-so, recently requested a preface, and though I am unworthy due to my shallowness, I have sought out the accounts of elders and fragmentary texts to supplement what was lacking.

---

**Preface to the *Records of the White Crane Abbey***

Since the Yellow Emperor obtained the tripod and ascended after refining the elixir of immortality, and King Mu of Zhou built the Grass Pavilion to summon immortals, tales of transcendence have flourished. By the Qin and Han dynasties, the fervor for seeking immortality reached its peak, and masters of esoteric arts emerged in succession, often resorting to fantastical claims and exaggerations to deceive and captivate rulers enamored of such pursuits. The transmission of their arcane and abstruse teachings, mingled with falsehoods, naturally drew ridicule from their contemporaries. Yet were there not true immortals who concealed themselves in remote and secluded places, abandoning fame and renown to cultivate the Dao? Their alchemical furnaces, medicinal mortars, sacred scriptures, and profound secrets were either hidden within mountain caves or left in the world in concealment or revelation. Though their traces differed, their legacy has been transmitted from antiquity to the present, generation after generation.

The White Crane Abbey in Fengcheng, Jiangxi, traces its origins to the era of Emperor Wen of Chen’s *Dajian* reign, where the divine traces of the immortal Gan Jun remain. In his youth, Gan Jun was renowned for his filial piety, his conduct celebrated in his hometown. After years of Daoist study, he heard of the virtuous Xu Jun of Jingyang and sought him as his master. Xu Jun accompanied him to study under the female immortal Chen Mu of Danyang, where Gan Jun received esoteric teachings. His mastery of the Dao grew daily, and he followed Xu Jun in accumulating merit along the rivers and lakes. Whether subduing monstrous pines, slaying flood dragons, or exorcising malevolent spirits, he wielded his three-foot sword to achieve feats that would benefit myriad generations. His name and deeds endure eternally—how could it be otherwise? Moreover, his meritorious deeds are too numerous to record in full.

Xu Jun, too, disseminated teachings on clarity, loyalty, and filial piety, all rooted in the principle of the Great Mean and utmost rectitude—unlike the mere talismans, incantations, and pacing the stars practiced by others. Gan Jun, renowned for his filial conduct, ascended to immortality and soared through the heavens. Was not his attainment of the Dao and his merit largely due to his loyalty and filial piety? Thus, the path of immortals to transcend the mundane does not abandon humaneness but rather perfects it. That Gan Jun, having achieved the Dao, served his mother until her passing before departing—this is the epitome of harmonizing both.

Moreover, the abodes of immortals are invariably situated in landscapes of majestic beauty, and the elegance of Fengcheng’s scenery must have nurtured his innate qualities. This spring, on my return from paying respects at the Jade Dragon Abbey on Mount Xishan, I passed through the White Crane Abbey. Though it was a stormy evening, I sought out its relics and remnants, and my admiration was deeply stirred. Though the abbey lies in a secluded corner, its scenery is serene and exquisite. Upon inquiry, I learned that its two main halls once housed the abbot’s quarters of my ancestor, the Perfected Lord Xu Jingyang, and that the Dragon Pool before them was where the Perfected Lord Feihuan once soared. The Daoist assembly leader, Zou, and the elder, Xiong, who oversee the abbey, presented me with Gan Jun’s *Elixir Canon*, the abbey’s records, and a letter from the Perfected Lord to Abbot Ren. Their words were profoundly illuminating, and though the events are extraordinary and the names endure, how could one not feel a deep sense of regret and longing?

Later that summer, someone arrived from the mountain and requested a preface for the abbey’s records. From my youth, I have been enamored of renowned mountains and rivers, seeking to explore their hidden wonders, yet I have always regretted not being able to visit all the famous sites of antiquity and the present. Now, having read the words of the immortal Gan and walked upon his sacred ground, how could I refuse to write this preface? Moreover, this pertains to the lingering echoes and lost traces of my ancestor—how could I not strive to detail their records? That the abbey’s superintendent has diligently fulfilled his duty and compiled these texts, intending to preserve them in print, is truly a commendable pursuit of their origins. If in the future the numinous transformations of true immortals endure alongside these records, will this not be a great boon to our Dao? Those who follow, take heed and strive onward.

---

**Preface to the *Collected Poems of Master Danmo* by Zhang**

The *Collected Poems of Master Danmo*, comprising several volumes, has been compiled by my friend Dazhuang, who has asked me to write a preface. I cannot refuse.

In my youth, I often attended Master Zhang in the presence of my late father and benefited greatly from his teachings. At that time, the esteemed local scholars Lu Bogong and Xia Bocheng were his fellow students and friends, and their scholarly pursuits advanced in tandem, making it impossible to discern who was foremost. Master Zhang hailed from a prominent family in our district, which had produced generations of officials and scholars since the Song and Yuan dynasties, many of whom gained renown through their literary achievements.

From childhood, Master Zhang displayed exceptional talent and a keen intellect. As he matured, his learning became vast and his memory prodigious, though he excelled particularly in poetry. During the late Yuan dynasty, poets of neighboring prefectures who gained fame—such as Zhang Zhongju, Huang Junrui, and Wei Dapu—all engaged with him as peers.

Master Li Zhonggong and Master Zhu Fanyuan were still his teachers. He traveled to Yan and Zhao with his accumulated learning, yet ultimately found no kindred spirits, and so journeyed through Wu and Chu before returning to settle. Before long, the realm was thrown into turmoil, and heroes arose on all sides. Those who usurped power and seized titles, hearing of his reputation, sought to recruit him, but he withdrew in seclusion and refused to bend. His hardships and loyal indignation were all expressed in his poetry, while his spirit remained as bold and unyielding as ever.

At the founding of our dynasty, when the realm was first pacified, he was among the first recluses summoned to the capital. He was appointed magistrate of Jiading Prefecture, and upon completing his term, was permitted to retire due to age. He then returned home, journeying along the Yi, Wu, Yue, Huai, and Xiang rivers. In his later years, he built a retreat among the fields and forests, secluding himself to teach at home, and his poetry grew ever more refined. Once, when dozens of the prefecture’s people were falsely implicated in a fabricated disaster, Master Zhang exonerated them and secured their safety—a deed for which the people still hold him in gratitude.

His poetry, in form and spirit, drew from the *Airs* and *Elegantiae*, while deeply immersing itself in the styles of the Han, Wei, and Six Dynasties. As for the poets of the High Tang and the early Yuan, he did not deign to mention them. His bearing was lofty and imposing, his appearance striking, and thus his words and thoughts flowed with divine swiftness, as if preordained. He did not rely on exhaustive search or labored thought, yet achieved a grandeur both profound and enduring—like the boundless clouds of the vaulted heavens or the ceaseless waves of the Yangtze—immeasurable and beyond tracing. This is why those of shallow talent and narrow vision could never attain his heights. His renown among princes, scholars, and officials was thus well-earned.

Alas, much of his lifetime’s writings was lost amid the ravages of war, with only fragments remaining. Master Lu once gathered what he could—perhaps one or two hundred pieces—intending to preserve them in print. Before the task was complete, he passed away, and the collection was again scattered, with little surviving. Those who cherished his work could not bear to discard it, and so I have transcribed a tenth of it, most of which aligns with the selections in *Dazhuang’s* compilation.

Though I once studied under Master Zhang and failed to live up to his teachings, how dare I presume to compose this account? Yet his learning was vast, his words eloquent, and his literary brilliance unmatched in his time. His great virtue and towering reputation were already evident in his deeds, and his legacy, though dimmed, shall not fade into oblivion.

My elder brother *Dazhuang*, diligent in study and devoted to righteousness, will surely carry on Master Lu’s aspirations with ease—how could he not delight in upholding the Way of literature?

The Master’s personal name was *Shuai*, his courtesy name *Mengxun*, and his sobriquet *Danmo*. His disciples referred to him as *Master Danmo*.

**Zong Lian’s Preface**

My friend, Master Ni Zizheng, in his youth studied under our late teacher, Master Xia, and later received the teachings of Master Lu’s *benxin* doctrine from Master Peng Mengyue. His conduct was steadfast and sincere, earning the trust of his community—even the humble and unlearned recognized him as a gentleman. Though he lived in poverty in a humble lane, he remained self-disciplined, cautious in his associations, and sparing in speech. He never ceased seeking instruction from teachers and friends, and his demeanor was always marked by humility. Afflicted by chronic illness, he seldom wrote, but when compelled to compose, he grounded his work in the classics, ensuring its purity and correctness.

I once heard the teachings of *xingli* from Master Peng, and in our discussions and refinements, Master Ni was my greatest aid. For over a decade, our bond remained as close as a single day. In a certain year, he was recommended for office and appointed instructor of Xinjian, returning home twice during his tenure. There, we delved into the words and deeds of ancient sages and worthies, finding solace in the quiet of overgrown groves, crumbling pavilions, lamplight, and moonlit ponds. His spirit was rich and sincere, rivaling that of the ancients.

In the spring of the seventeenth year of *Hongwu*, he traveled to the capital after mourning his parents, but succumbed to a minor illness and passed away. Overcome with grief, I urged his son, Heng, to preserve his father’s writings. Four years later...

Heng presented several writings, titled *Zonglian Ye*, and entreated, saying: "When my late father served in Xinjian, the county academy was once the study hall of Prime Minister Zonglian of the Yuan. Hence, he named his work thus. None understood my father’s depth of learning as Master Mo did—would you honor us with a preface?" Unable to refuse, I read the texts twice and sighed in reflection: "Of scholars who pursue learning, many seek fulfillment through others, while few seek it within themselves. My friend was nurtured by the harmony of heaven and earth, the refinement of mountains and rivers, and the blessings of his ancestors. His nature was pure, his qi clear, his conduct upright and unsullied—qualities beyond the reach of mediocre scholars or vulgar men. Having fulfilled himself inwardly, he had no need of external validation. Thus, his discourse and manner arose from the simplicity of principle and the subtlety of attaining the Dao, self-evident without exhaustive inquiry, self-manifest without deliberate assertion. Was this not the fruit of his innate understanding?

Naturally, in his actions and teachings, he eschewed superficial recitation or rhetorical flourish, grounding all in the rectification of the self and sincerity of purpose. With the self rectified and sincerity established, the Way of the sages and the methods of governing the world were already complete—what need was there for superfluous elaboration? His policy proposals, lectures, and expositions all emerged from the essentials of uprightness and the methods of self-cultivation. Even the Yang and Qian of the Lu school would scarcely surpass him. His poems and verses, rooted in the truth of human nature, embodied the refined elegance of Wei and Meng, the serene simplicity of Tao and Liu.

Alas, he was never granted a grand office to fully express his harmonious and pure cultivation—how rare such men are in this sagely age! Even now, among Xinjian’s students, those who once heard his words stand firm in their resolve. Was this not the fruit of his profound influence in nurturing integrity and discipline? Yet his learning was rooted in the Lu school, and those who venerate Zhu Xi may not embrace him. Thus, his steadfast independence and unwavering clarity remain little known. Nevertheless, when one examines the essence of his thought and the substance of his words, can they truly be dismissed?

If his teachings endure, they will surely serve as wings to the words of Zhou, Cheng, Zhu, and Lu. My friend Wu Bozong has composed his epitaph, but I lack the eloquence to amplify its praise. Thus, I offer this preface in lieu of refusal, recording the origins of his work."

---

**Preface to *Yunxi’s Poetry Collection***

Mencius said: "When the traces of the true kings faded, poetry perished; with poetry gone, the *Spring and Autumn Annals* arose." How difficult it is to speak of poetry! After the *Three Hundred Poems* and the ancient *fu*, the Han poets Su and Li, and the Wei masters Cao, Wang, Liu, and Ying, still drew near to the *Airs* and *Elegances*, beginning to transform them. By the early Jin, the likes of Ruan, Lu, Pan, and Zuo had not yet let the tradition decline. Later, in the Six Dynasties, Bao, Xie, Yan, and Zhang emerged, but their tones grew delicate and languid, their forms ornate and florid—the purity of the *Airs* and *Elegances* gradually dissipated.

By the early Tang, Song, Du, Chen, and Liu arose, followed in the High Tang by Wei, Liu, Wang, and Meng, whose spirit and cadence soared with unmatched vigor. Yet the one who synthesized it all was Du of Shaoling. Even contemporaries like Gao, Li, Cen, and Jia could not rival him. In his poetry, whether in adversity or prosperity, Du’s songs aligned with the *Airs* and *Elegances*, offering a model for all. By the late Tang, customs grew extravagant, and in the Song, only Su Shi, Mei Yaochen, Shi Yannian, Wang Anshi, Ouyang Xiu, the Su brothers, Zhu Xi, and Yang Wanli were deemed exemplary. In the Yuan, Fan, Yang, Yu, and Jie revived fading echoes, directly reviving the High Tang. For a time, scholars of the imperial academy and literati from all quarters took them as masters. In the regions between Jiangdong and Jiangxi, those who excelled in poetry flourished.

Those who attained mastery within themselves found the vastness of the cosmos and mountains, the minutiae of flora and fauna—regardless of scale or beauty—stirring their hearts when perceived. Their diction and thought surged like rivers and seas, unstoppable. Thus, the literary men and ink-slingers of the age vied in splendor, arrayed like scales on a fish. Yet how many among them achieved grand and complete works, worthy of ascending to the ancients and serving as models for posterity?

The *Three Hundred Poems* were selected from the words of commoners and simple women, yet their satire, praise, and lament were fit for the ancestral temple, aiding governance and preserving the royal grace. If poetry perished and the *Spring and Autumn Annals* succeeded it, then poets must strive to revive what was lost. Yet later scholars, if they do not trace the source and follow the stream, if they do not arise from the correctness of a tranquil nature and the substance of self-cultivation, but instead chase after superficial imitation and embellishment, gleaning only the remnants of the ancients, merely dabbling in ink and plagiarizing fragments, racing to outdo one another in ephemeral verses of orchids and moonlight—how can they be deemed worthy of poetry?

My friend Yang Mengyu’s family is renowned for poetry. In my youth, I read his uncle Xianmin’s poems, though I regret not seeing them all. This spring, Mengyu was invited to tutor my nephew, and I lamented our late acquaintance as I studied his discourse and manner. He also presented several of his poems, which I read in full. Upon inquiring about his family, I learned that his ancestors, beginning with his great-uncle, were esteemed scholars, known as the "Master of Guanzhong." By the Sui and Tang, many held prominent offices. One ancestor, whose name is recorded, served in Jiangxi and later settled in Shuibei Village, Jinxian County, during the first year of Gaozong’s *Linde* reign. In the Song, a certain Zhongbo passed the imperial examinations, served as magistrate of Changsha, and was later promoted to transport commissioner of Jiangxi before his death.

There was also one named Longwei, who likewise passed the imperial examinations and served as registrar of Poyang. His father’s younger brother and younger son both held office in the Yuan dynasty: the former as examiner in the Secretariat, later promoted to vice minister of the Ministry of Rites, and posthumously honored as minister of the same ministry. Thus the clan flourished with each generation, and those now dwelling in Shuibei number twenty-nine in descent.

Their learning originated with Shuneng, who studied under Wei Jianxiu, a student of the Song dynasty Imperial Academy, and Master Jianxin Weixian. Shuneng’s younger son Xianmin and his own younger son both received instruction from Shuneng, perpetuating the family’s scholarly tradition. Jianxin was refined in temperament and unassuming in demeanor; in his later years, he also admired Daoist teachings on seclusion and reclusion, inviting renowned masters to rigorously educate his sons. Mengyu is virtuous and erudite, his bearing and literary style marked by clarity, elegance, and breadth—modeled after Du Fu yet encompassing all poetic forms. Indeed, as a family of official rank for generations, their noble origins and long-cultivated scholarly refinement run deep.

I am certain that neither worldly success nor obscurity disturbs his inner peace. Had he been placed among the ranks of the eminent, who is to say he could not have continued the sacred music of the ancestral temple, enriching the literary legacy of a flourishing age? Such is his aspiration toward the grand tradition of *Guofeng*, *Ya*, and *Song*. As for myself, unworthy as I am, exiled to remote mountains and dense forests, my mind wanders the desolate shores of wind and moonlight. My own shallowness has surely been refined by his brilliance, yet how could I fully comprehend Mengyu?

Yet I am struck by the rarity of those in this world who truly understand poetry—even Mengyu is seldom recognized. How few can soar beyond the ancients and contend with the Creator! When I asked about his uncle, my affection for him left me no choice but to await another day to do justice to his merits. Is this not so?

---

**Preface to the Xu Clan Genealogy**

I have read the *Rites of Zhou*, which states that the duty of the *xiaoshi* (minor historian) is to establish lineage, distinguish senior and junior branches, and thereby determine the order of the state. Though ancient times lacked genealogical records, the strict distinction between major and minor clans was maintained. Only after the decline of the clan system did emphasis shift to family status, giving rise to genealogical records. These serve to clarify lineage and distinguish degrees of kinship.

Yet those who compile genealogies often seek to glorify their ancestors and elevate their descendants. Whenever they encounter a prominent figure of the same surname, they invariably claim affiliation, fabricating connections to complete their records. Though such self-deception may bring temporary pride, it undermines the principles of honoring ancestors and clarifying lineage. How, then, can later generations trace their roots? This is why scholars and officials reject such practices.

Tracing further back, details grow scarce, and inaccuracies may arise. Some disregard a family’s historical standing or the purity of its customs, valuing only wealth and fame. They revere great households and prestigious lineages, yet how can they verify distinctions of antiquity, the origins of surnames, or the bestowal of clan names?

The Gaoyang Xu clan descends from the Marquis of Xu and the Lord of Qi in the *Spring and Autumn Annals*. After the *Spring and Autumn* period, the state ceased to exist, and its descendants took the former fief as their surname. According to the genealogy, seven members of the Xu clan held marquisates in the Han dynasty, losing their titles only after Wang Mang’s downfall. In the Eastern Han, the upright official Xu Pan’s descendants settled in Runan, each branch gaining renown. Xu Jing served as Grand Tutor of Shu, while Xu Chu, in service to Wei, was enfeoffed as a marquis. In the Jin dynasty, Xu Zi was nominated as *xiaolian* but declined office; his descendants flourished in Gaoyang. Xu Shao was enfeoffed as Duke of Anlu in the Tang, and his grandson Jingzong became prime minister of Longxiang. Xu Riyuan died for righteousness during the *Tianbao* rebellion. In the Song *Xiangfu* era, Xu Sui served as director of the Directorate for Palace Buildings. Xu Yi, in the Yuan dynasty, was appointed pacification commissioner of Jianghuai, Jinghu, and the Two Zhe provinces. His descendants grew numerous and held successive court positions. One ancestor, through official promotion, was transferred to Yihuang in Fuzhou, where the family settled, becoming a prominent clan of Jiangyou.

My connection to the Xu family is one of kinship by marriage. My cousin Biyuan once entrusted me with their genealogy to compose a preface. I have long reflected on the inexorable cycles of prosperity and decline, glory and hardship—how profoundly they unfold without end! In my youth, I heard of the customs of old families, none more illustrious than those of the Yuan dynasty. Due to its long reign, its benevolence and generosity, and the flourishing of commerce, all who bore its legacy sought to revive and restore it. Thus, not only did extravagance and indulgence prevail, but the influence of poetry and books, along with the virtues of filial piety and brotherly love, still occasionally thrived.

Yet over thirty years have passed, and with the shift of fortune, all has changed. What once stood proud now lies in ruins. Is this not the inevitable ebb and flow of destiny? For those who uphold their family’s legacy through learning and conduct, without genealogical records to trace their lineage’s reach or the origins of their branches, how can the noble deeds and illustrious achievements of their ancestors avoid fading into obscurity? Few indeed are those who preserve their genealogies. That the Xu clan has so carefully safeguarded theirs, tracing their roots to officials of high rank and esteemed reputation, may be called a return to fundamentals and self-awareness.

Alas, I have yet to meet the standard, but Biyuan’s words compel me. Thus, I offer this preface without hesitation.

---

**Preface to *Zeng Yu Fengzi***

All beings endowed with form and *qi* arise from non-being and ultimately return to it. Thus, form is borne by *qi*, and *qi* governs form. The primordial *qi* animates heaven and earth, while yin and yang operate within it. Heaven’s canopy, earth’s support, the radiance of the sun and moon, the sequence of the four seasons, the alternation of day and night, the transformations of ghosts and spirits, the multitude of the ten thousand things—all their ceaseless movements arise from the mutual generation and transformation of being and non-being.

The ancients who excelled in instructing others guided them solely toward the Dao. To understand the Dao is to grasp the mechanism of being and non-being, generation and transformation. Yet today’s so-called scholars pursue empty fame and superficial adornment, neglecting the inner for the outer. How can they silently seek and inwardly comprehend the interplay of being and non-being, generation and transformation?

While dwelling in the mountains of Qi, I wandered leisurely beneath the pines where cranes roosted. There I encountered a Daoist, his hair wild and unkempt, who bowed and spoke: "I have roamed rivers and seas for many years, once questioning extraordinary persons about the Way of the Golden Elixir and Returning Cinnabar. I believe in Liezi’s teachings on the Corpse-Chariot and Spirit-Steed, and that there are those who attain divine transformation through riding the wind. Though I do not yet know if I am capable, is it so—or is it not? I wish to have this clarified."

I replied: "Wind, as a thing, has sound but no form. Master Zhuang called it the great sigh of the cosmos. Does riding it truly rely on something? If what it relies upon is emptiness, how can one repeatedly attain fortune? If what it relies upon is not emptiness, how can fortune be attained at all—let alone repeatedly? Thus, it is difficult to escape dependence. How much better to move without relying on anything, to be without dependence on externals. Therefore, those who ride the correctness of heaven and earth and harness the transformations of the six qi to wander the boundless—what do they await? Liezi’s riding of the Corpse-Chariot and Spirit-Steed is but the endless coming and going of a single qi, a reliance on riding the wind—yet what does it truly await? Thus, their wandering knows no rest, and its limits cannot be measured, let alone returning after fifteen days. To rest is to return, and to return is never without rest. This is how being rides upon non-being, and non-being governs being. When it reaches the state of neither being nor non-being, the mechanism of ceaseless generation and transformation is fully realized.

In your studies, you must seek the correctness of heaven and earth and the transformations of the six qi, cultivating them within yourself until, over months and years, form and spirit undergo dark transformation. Then, riding the boundless Corpse-Chariot and Spirit-Steed, the wind moves without needing to be ridden. As for attaining fortune—it becomes something I have no use for. To abandon this and pursue other paths, even if one commands the winds of Feilian and the lightning of Lieque, rides the thunderous chariot of the storm, or traverses the starry roads of the clouds, how can one truly ride anything?

Yet all who approach the Dao must cast aside illusion, sever perceptual defilements, make their form like withered wood and their heart like dead ashes. They must regard the world’s treasures as dust and their own weighty bodies as clods of earth. Then, their self-reliance becomes light, and not even the entire world can burden their minds with so much as a hair’s weight. They move as if relying on emptiness, needing no vehicle yet knowing no bounds. Even beyond heaven and earth, their traces cannot be confined.

You, with your resolute nature and rigorous inquiry, must penetrate beyond being and non-being to wander the carefree wastes and boundless wilds. Like the roaring of ten thousand hollows, the emptiness of all openings—where nothing is heard, nothing is seen—what then are the earthly pipes of the hollows, the human pipes of bamboo flutes, or the heavenly pipes of the myriad winds? Only by listening to the soundless and seeing the formless can one realize that between being and non-being, all is but the myriad winds of a single breath. This is what those who seek the Creator rely upon. Yet I alone have not attained it—strive onward."

The Daoist then asked to adopt this as his name and recorded these words before parting.

**Preface to the Zither Master Zhu Zongming**

Music’s power to bring harmony extends to the heavens and coils through the earth, moving through yin and yang and penetrating ghosts and spirits. Thus, grand music harmonizes with heaven and earth. Its resonance with things manifests in sound, and sound shaped into patterns is called tone. In tones arise joy and anger, sorrow and delight, exultation and grief, perversity and uprightness. This is why the noble person never parts with the zither and se for even a moment. As it is said: the zither is the essence of music, what the noble person ought to master.

All that enters the ears and eyes, all that fills the four limbs, must be regulated by ritual, and the zither suffices to dispel perversity, cleanse error, gladden the heart, and nurture the nature—thus attaining the correctness of human feelings. It is no different from the harmonious chimes of jade pendants or the refined sounds of the *Ya* and *Song* odes, which should not be silenced without cause.

From the times of Fuxi, Shennong, Yu, Shun, King Wen, and Confucius down to the works of Boya, Qu Yuan, and Song Yu, some compositions arose from sorrowful thoughts, taking shape in mournful sighs or expressions of joy and choice. They begin with faint and distant notes, suddenly surging like anger; they end with a swift, stirring intensity, mournful as grief. In their urgency, they shake the wind and rain, splitting cliffs; in their slowness, they stir waves, rousing dragons. In their grandeur, they spur the gallop of unicorns and the brandishing of shields and axes; in their subtlety, they mimic the drip of springs and the whispers of insects. Their thousand forms and ten thousand postures cannot be fully described. Truly, they can rub against yin and yang and resonate with heaven and earth.

Yet to grasp this in the heart and express it through the hands requires long practice—how could it be otherwise? Only by attaining the correctness of human feelings can one preserve the lingering echoes of the sages and worthies. How could it be merely to please the vulgar ears and eyes of the masses?

The masters of old—Shi Xiang, Shi Kuang, and Shi Wen—are long gone. Those who came after, modifying their teachings—Yongmen Zhou, Sima Xiangru, Cai Dang, Sun Deng, Ji Kang, Huan Tan, and Yang Shou—each excelled in their own way. Among those renowned in recent times for this art are Xu Zifang of Xiaoshan, Yuan Minzhong, and Mao Bozhen. Yang, who studied under Xu and Yuan, rose to prominence in his time as Qi Ling. His *Two Compositions of Jiang* became models, and later generations took Xu and Yuan as their standard.

From my youth, I have been devoted to this study. In great cities, whenever I encountered someone skilled in it, I would inquire. Yet most who claim mastery today merely boast of their abundance and compete in extravagance. Those whose tones are bold and transcendent, whose spirit soars to distant realms, are exceedingly rare."

The Fujian scholar Zhu Zongming studied under Yuan Ling, a friend of mine. This spring, he came to travel through our mountains, and together we swept the stone drums by Xian Spring. His melodies and tunes were all pure, profound, elegant, and refined—bold and transcendent, soaring and unrestrained—truly beyond comparison with those who merely boast of their abundance and compete in extravagance. In his discussions, he invariably took the attainment of the true nature of feeling as essential. In his youth, he traveled widely, drawing from many sources. At the beginning of our dynasty, he was first recommended to the capital but firmly declined and returned. All the great families and prominent clans of Fujian vied to invite him. Yet only now, in his advanced years, has he attained the subtleties of sound—surely not the work of a single day.

As the ancients said of "lofty mountains and flowing waters," he found in him one with whom to cultivate the spirit and express the heart amid wind-swept forests and moonlit cliffs. When he departed, how could I not dream of the celestial sounds and surging waves of his music? And what indeed were those tones of *Huangzhong* and *Baixue*? I feared only that my ears could never forget them. As he left, I took up my zither and played *The Debate by the Stream*, strumming twice without ever sensing the sound of parting.

Thus, I write this preface as a gift for his journey.

**Preface to "Roaming the Immortal Crags"**

I have often observed how people differ in their stillness and restlessness, their toil and ease. Their conduct and speech, their silence and expression, inevitably reveal the inclinations of their nature—how could these be forced? If forced, how could they ever reach the truth of such inclinations?

Our mountain’s Immortal Crags, numbering twenty-four in all, have long been renowned throughout the land. Since the Han-era Celestial Master refined his elixir here, dragons and tigers have appeared in the rocks, lending the place its name. From then on, scholars near and far—regardless of rank, age, or strength—who passed by or made a special pilgrimage never failed to wish for a visit. Thus, boats and oars have come and gone without cease, month after month, year after year.

On the sixth day of the ninth lunar month in the *bingzi* year of the Hongwu reign, I set forth with my academy guests—Master Yang Mengxu, my second brother Zhan Bi, my nephew Wu Ruxu—and our attendants Wang Jingshan, Wu Chuyuan, and Yuan Zhi’an. We ordered a boat to be readied at the creek’s cleansing waters, loaded wine and provisions, then cast off, riding the waves past islets until we arrived in no time at all.

That evening, we rested to the left of the Crags at the *Yanfa Abbey*, the shrine of the Han-era Celestial Master. Towering terraces and soaring pavilions pierced the heavens, while water curtains draped the altar platforms, cinnabar turtles and mushroom crags arrayed to either side—all offered respite for our repose. The next dawn, we embarked again, the boatmen’s poles and gestures guiding us up and down in ceaseless succession, leaving no leisure to admire the winding paths. Yet among the isles and shores, strange rocks and rare trees, secluded blossoms and exotic blooms flourished in lush, fragrant profusion, each vying in solitary splendor.

Soon we turned toward the shallows, skirting coves where the boat’s hull scraped hidden hollows and jagged crevices—places where only birds could perch or cling, and no human trace could reach. Some coves nestled deep in forests and narrow valleys, sheltering fishermen’s huts; others lay concealed by wild thickets and overgrown islets, where woodcutters’ trails wound in meandering seclusion. This was scarcely different from the Peach Blossom Spring of Wuling. Presently, we paused to brew tea by the stream, drifting downstream as sheer cliffs and precipitous crags reared up in sudden fury—some like leaping dragons and bounding leopards, others like crouching tigers and coiled serpents. Those that bowed low formed natural screens and barriers, while clouds surged and mists billowed in endless transformations. Where hollows gaped in the towering rocks, kitchens, granaries, windows, and weaving looms seemed fully furnished within, inspiring awe and longing as one gazed upward. The mind dissolved, the spirit released, one truly forgot the mortal world existed.

Below, the limpid pool shimmered in emerald clarity, while ethereal harmonies arose on all sides. Yet above, soaring into the azure skies and brilliant sunlight, only jagged peaks and towering ridges stood—verdant ravines and intricate ridges woven together. In the distance, wild waves and shallow shoals, solemn streams and muddy shallows stretched on without end. After a long while, we disembarked at the base of the Crags, securing our robes to ascend. Suspended boulders hung like canopies over the void, spacious enough to seat dozens. There we laid out wine cups and dishes, drinking our fill. Some recited poetry, others plucked the zither, still others sketched the scene—thus we passed the day in delight, lingering until evening.

Just then, a western wind arose, as if urging us to tread the void and ride the floating clouds, our spirits irrepressible. We ordered the boat to seek the Crags’ right flank, where the *Mingcheng Abbey*—the *Hetu* Immortal Altar of Grand Master Wu—stood. Night had fallen by the time we lit torches to hasten to our lodging. Our host, a descendant of the Wu clan named Congshan, was well-versed in divination and astrology. We conversed through the night, departing the next morning. The poems we composed filled our sleeves, forming a scroll before we knew it.

Mengxu then arranged them in order and declared: “Life is for joy alone. These famed mountains and rivers are the dwelling places of immortals and perfected beings—I have heard of them for years. They are no less wondrous than Penglai, Weifeng, Fanghu, or Yuanjiao. Many long to visit but never can. Some hear of them and dismiss the tales as fantastical exaggerations, refusing to believe. Yet today, by good fortune, we have beheld them with our own eyes. The realms of Anqi and Xianmen, the flights of immortals and feasts of leviathans, all lie within a blink’s reach—how could we not count ourselves fortunate? I beg you to preface these works, to record this gathering’s splendor and leave it for posterity.”

I could not refuse and sighed in assent: “Since the dawn of creation, heaven and earth have stood in their places, and mountains and rivers have flourished. These Crags have towered through endless eons, never crumbling. For thousands upon thousands of years before us, who can say how many cycles of rise and fall, growth and decay they have witnessed? How many have shared in this joy? For thousands upon thousands of years to come, who can say how many will know this same delight? Yet those entangled in worldly glory, drowned in mundane filth, buffeted by stormy gloom or the vicissitudes of human affairs—how vast is the gulf between them and those who wander here! This journey, with its pleasures of poetry, music, and song, need not yield to the famed excursions of Red Cliff or the Ke River. How could I remain silent?”

Moreover, the restless yearn for stillness, the weary long for ease—yet how many truly grasp the essence of nature’s truth? Thus, I gladly acceded to his request and composed this preface.

---

**Preface to the *Huanzhen Collection***

The Dao of immortality has been revered since antiquity. When the Yellow Emperor sought the Way from Guangchengzi, the school of Huang-Lao arose, for Guangcheng was none other than Laozi. Thus began the teachings of immortality. In the Tang, there was Qian Keng; in the Xia, Xiaofu; in the Shang, Wuanqiu; and in the Zhou, Wang Qiao of the Zhou clan—such figures existed even in the Three Dynasties. Yet it was King Mu of Zhou who built a thatched pavilion to receive scholars, bringing their doctrines into prominence.

Later, the First Emperor of Qin and Emperor Wu of Han were deluded by the arts of alchemists and elixirs. Even Wang Cizhong and Dongfang Shuo, though of their time, failed to find true masters. Their heterodox teachings spread and intermingled, ultimately leading to their downfall. Thus, the great scholars and eminent minds of the age rose together to condemn them—rightly so.

Yet in the Han, Wei Boyang modeled his *Cantong Qi* after the *Book of Changes*, expanding upon the *Ancient Text of the Dragon and Tiger Classic*. Through this, the Dao of the Elixir was illuminated, free from the pitfalls of metallurgy, herbalism, cloud-gazing, or breathwork.
