It clarified the natural principles of body, mind, spirit, and vitality, using the trigrams, lines, and timekeeping to establish its rules—all harmonizing with the patterns of odd and even, images and numbers.

Later, Zhongli Quan transmitted his teachings to Lü Yan of the Tang, who upheld and expounded them in songs, discourses, and debates, nearing the true essence of the tradition. Such figures have never been lacking through the ages. In the Song, Zhang Ziyang, Shi Xinglin, Chen Niuwan, Bai Ziqing, Li Yuxi, and Li Qing’an—all were outstanding masters of their time. Their words and ideas were no less profound than Boyang’s, each achieving deep, silent understanding in their own way.

The span of a thousand or ten thousand years—how is it that their words align as if tallying broken seals? This is no coincidence, but the natural outcome. For the principles of a thousand or ten thousand generations remain the same, as do the hearts of men. What causes divergence is invariably the perverse and deceptive doctrines, either contrived to impress the present age or to delude later generations—how could these ever truly accord with the natural Dao of body, mind, spirit, and qi?

Thus, the ancients invariably transmitted their teachings only to the worthy, for the Dao does not spread in vain. Moreover, if a master does not guard the profound and abstruse mysteries, refusing to reveal them indiscriminately, while the learner lacks the solid foundation of accumulated merit and cultivated conduct—merely adorning themselves with empty formalities and pretense, no different from those who chase fame and self-aggrandizement—how could they ever speak of the Dao? How could they give voice to its truths?

The Master of Hunran of Linjiang in Nanchang, once of another surname, was broadly learned. Having encountered an extraordinary person and received secret transmissions, he remained diligent in discourse and writing. I have long read his words, and though we met briefly at an inn, haste prevented a thorough inquiry. This spring, my disciple Yuan Wenyi returned from Wu, bringing his work *The Collection of Returning to the True*, and asked me to inscribe a preface. After savoring it twice, I find it truly conveys the essence of the Golden Elixir and the returning cinnabar, elucidating both the subtle and the manifest, the principle and its application. If one cultivates accordingly, even those who ascend to meet Ziyang or Qing’an may find themselves neither ahead nor behind.

I have long lamented the enduring corruption of superficial, reckless, and heterodox habits in the world, which seem impossible to eradicate entirely. Yet I rejoice that these words may serve to rouse and rectify the ills of this declining age. If steadfast scholars and upright individuals possess the discernment to distinguish fine jade from common stone, then wandering in the primordial radiance of the undifferentiated chaos, refining qi beyond the merging of primordial mists, will be no difficult feat. This is how one fully grasps the return to the origin and the consummation of the end, exhausting the divine and comprehending transformation.

Perhaps one day, I may summon the yellow crane to descend from the skies, and together we shall discuss these matters by the rivers and isles of the Xiang—there is still time. This preface stands at the beginning of the compilation.

*Jianquan Collection, Scroll Two* concludes.
*Jianquan Collection, Scroll Three*?
?
*Jianquan Collection, Scroll Four*

Master Qishan, the Celestial Master of Non-Action, Zhang Yuchu, presents:

**On the *Tai Su* and the Sayings of Liezi**

Liezi spoke of the sequence: There is *Tai Yi* (Supreme Change), *Tai Chu* (Supreme Beginning), *Tai Shi* (Supreme Commencement), and *Tai Su* (Supreme Simplicity).

*Tai Yi* is the state before qi emerges. *Tai Chu* is the beginning of qi. *Tai Shi* is the beginning of form. *Tai Su* is the beginning of substance. When qi, form, and substance are complete yet undivided, this is called *hunlun* (primordial chaos). *Hunlun* describes the state where the myriad things are intermingled and inseparable.

Qi is the origin of form and substance. Before yin and yang separate, its body is *hunlun*. Once divided, it is carved and chiseled, and the *hundun* (chaos) dies—*hunlun* is lost. Yet these three—qi, form, and substance—constantly encompass beginning and end, cycling and interweaving. Outwardly, they appear separate, but in the fleeting interplay of growth and decay, they never depart from *hunlun*. For qi courses through heaven and earth as wind, rain, frost, and dew, as mountains, rivers, and valleys. Within humans and all things, it manifests as the four limbs and the hundred bones. Though creatures fly or burrow, move or grow, each unique in its myriad forms, all are confined by form and substance.

The beginning of qi, unseen, remains unfathomable. Yet its fullness in substance can be exhaustively explored. The azure of the sky is the true color of the Great Void, pristine and clear. But when darkness and transformation arise in an instant, the azure hue becomes obscured. Without these shifts of darkness and change, the fullness of heaven’s nature cannot be realized.

Humans receive the purity of qi and substance. When passions flare and desires overflow, hardness and softness, good and evil become manifest—this dilutes their purity, mixes their essence, and stains their clarity. Yet if substance were utterly undifferentiated, how could the fullness of human nature be expressed? All things follow this principle. In motion and stillness, advance and retreat, none withhold their nature.

Thus, the substance of things, without adornment, lacks splendor. The true flavor of taste, without salt or bitterness, lacks harmony. The subtlety of sound, without pitch and rhythm, lacks concord. This is the inevitable nature of things. Humans must curb desire to return to their origin, so that purity is not diluted, essence not mixed, and clarity not stained. Then the luminous body of pure brightness becomes fully evident. Here we see that our hardness and softness, good and evil, are like heaven’s fleeting darkness and change—yet the original purity of our substance remains unmoved in stillness or motion.

Nevertheless, those in the world who uphold this teaching for self-cultivation may nurture purity but fail to wander in *Tai Su*. Without wandering in *Tai Su*, how can they perceive the origin of substance? Substance endures, while adornment decays. Substance nourishes, while adornment pleases. Those who aspire to the Dao must shed adornment to return to substance. By returning to substance, the mind becomes clear, the spirit bright, and inner and outer align in integrity. The myriad things merge in *hunlun* yet remain undivided—is this not perceiving their origin?

The scholar Lian Taisu of Wudang Mountain’s Five Dragons Palace has studied broadly and conducted himself with rectitude. He has dwelled on our mountain for over twenty years, steadfast in practice, residing in quietude within a single chamber, untouched by the world. My late father once honored him, and since I inherited this teaching, I have drawn much from his inquiries into our Daoist traditions. Could he not wander in *Tai Su* and yet remain thus? This autumn, he wished to return home, and though I urged him to stay, he declined. I said to him: "From ancient times, famous mountains and rivers have been the abodes of immortals and perfected beings. Why limit yourself to returning here? Yet I have heard this mountain’s wonders surpass all under heaven. Are there not recluses like Anqi or Xianmen hidden within its peaks? Taisu, you should seek their company to expand your *hunlun* and return to the primordial state before the merging of mists, transcending the confines of form and qi. What more would you seek of the origin of substance?"

As he prepared to depart, I combined these teachings for him. Lian gladly requested that I record them as a keepsake.

**The Pure One’s Discourse**

Ah Jiang’s Zhang Yanhong comes from a family of renowned virtue. Last spring, I visited Xishan and Hua Gai, passing through Ah Jiang on my return, where I first met him. I admired his sincere and earnest demeanor, and in conversation, learned of his fervent pursuit of our Daoist teachings. He has styled himself "The Pure One." Upon his departure, I had no time to discuss these matters. This spring, he came to visit my mountain. My younger brother, Yankui, and I accompanied him to the peaks of Longjing, Guancheng Lake, Pipa, Yuntai, and Miaogu, where we bathed in raging currents and sat upon boulders, roaming as if with heaven itself, forgetting the world of men.

Yanhong then asked: "You once spoke of my name, 'The Pure One.' Now that I have had the honor of this journey, may I inquire into its meaning?" I pointed to the water and said to him: "Water is a tranquil thing. From deep sources and hidden ravines, it gathers in a single, limpid pool. Yet when it surges from its lair, leaping and tumbling through a hundred turns, it flows downward for thousands of miles—vast as rivers and seas, narrow as streams and valleys. It rages against rocks, howls in the wind. Though obstructed by thorns, it may be blocked; though fouled by filth, it may be muddied. Fish and dragons dwell in its depths, forests and trees shade its seclusion, wind and rain darken its moisture—nothing can impede its flow. Day and night, it never ceases, its momentum unstoppable, for this is the nature of its encounter.

Yet in its essence, it is inherently clear. Thorns cannot block it, filth cannot muddy it. It embraces all yet illuminates all—this is the work of primordial qi. The Dao’s principle of method originates in qi. Water flows between heaven and earth as qi flows within the body. In the space of a single breath, movement and stillness, fullness and emptiness, growth and decay are all present.

The human heart governs qi. Fundamentally pure and bright, its emptiness and luminosity are like a limpid pool. Yet when it pursues desire and is obscured, moved by external things, if one does not apply the effort to restrain and refine it, it becomes enslaved by the outer world, stumbling and entangled—like water surging from its lair, its momentum unstoppable. Its qi scatters into thoughts, emotions, and desires, becoming blocked and muddied as it should."

Thus, the sages and worthies instruct us to guard and restrain the heart, precisely to awaken it from error, restore its flow, and make its seeking akin to water’s response to what it encounters. Its purity and luminosity are never truly lost; the radiance remains, and this is called the Dao in motion, the method in operation. Then, circulating through the six directions of emptiness, moving in harmony with heaven and earth without conflict, it manifests as wind, rain, thunder, and lightning—ever-present as if inherent. Why is this so? For the numerical patterns of the Yellow River and Luo River, the Way of thunder and lightning, and the ceaseless generation of the myriad beings all share a single origin. Thus, to cultivate it is to attain supreme harmony; to apply it is to achieve supreme divinity. Through sincerity, it polishes and refines; through earnest devotion, it penetrates and communicates. Then, the wondrous efficacy of its resonance is as effortless as turning one’s palm.

Yet there are subtle and mysterious workings of spirits and gods, rarely spoken of by others, which I also employ. This requires absolute purity to achieve unity; once unified, the Way of nature and destiny is complete—what further method could be needed? Therefore, by observing the nature of things, one knows one’s own nature; by fully realizing one’s own nature, one knows Heaven. Knowing Heaven, the Heaven within oneself is the same as that beyond. In the Way of resonance and communion, who can divide them into two?

Thus, those who cultivate inwardly often feign madness or indulge in wine, their laughter and rage alike capable of summoning wind and commanding thunder. Yet how could this be mere happenstance? Yan Hong has thoroughly investigated the profound subtleties of the method and the work of purity and unity, practicing and verifying it through experience—what more need be said? Moreover, I have heard that among his ancestors were those who attained the Dao and transcended the mundane. His deep devotion to this pursuit—could it not be the echo of past cultivation?

Yan Hong, take heed. As one who dwells in the same village as the Perfected Lord of the Celestial King’s Court, you must revive this legacy to enlighten future generations. This is my hope. At his request, I have inscribed these words upon the wall.

**On the Learning of the Great Foundation**
The great foundation of learning lies in nature and destiny, in the Dao and virtue alone. The heart governs nature and emotion, while nature is bestowed by the mandate of Heaven. The four seasons, five phases, and the transformations of the myriad beings all emerge from destiny. The four beginnings, five constants, and the ten thousand things and affairs all reside within nature. Yet though the myriad things differ, their principle is never not one. Thus, to follow it is called the Dao; to cultivate it is called teaching. Yet one must study to know it.

Learning begins with filial piety, brotherly respect, loyalty, and trust, and culminates in the rituals of ascent, descent, toasting, and drinking—there is no moment when one does not learn. The Classics call this learning. It originates in the *Shu Jing*, which states, *"Study the ancient teachings to gain insight,"* and *"Hold fast to the beginning and end, relying on learning."* This is the learning of the sages.

The sages and worthies, fully comprehending the impartiality of Heaven’s principle, possess a body of clarity, purity, and integrity. The foolish and unworthy, drowning in the selfishness of human desire, fall into obscurity, turbidity, and perversity. Thus, those who study seek to remove their obstructions and restore the inherent goodness within. Therefore, one must thoroughly investigate the Way of fully realizing nature and fulfilling destiny, of illuminating goodness and embodying sincerity.

Thus, ritual and righteousness serve as its standards and boundaries; speech and conduct are its means of nurturing and reflection. Yet one must honor teachers and befriend the virtuous to attain it. Abide in reverence, act in accordance with righteousness, and over time, it enters the ears, takes root in the heart, and permeates all without exception.

Consider this: Zaofu excelled in charioteering, Yi in archery, Shi Kuang in tuning, Zhui in bow-making, Xizhong in carriage-crafting, and Du in horsemanship. Even in such minor skills, they focused their intent before achieving mastery. How much more so for learning—the work of sages and worthies!

Confucius, though already a sage, did not consider himself one, saying, *"At fifteen, I set my heart on learning."* From that time to a thousand generations hence, who could surpass his sageliness? Yet he still dedicated himself to learning.

In ancient times, the institutions of learning took different names: in the Tang and Yu dynasties, it was called *Chengjun*; in the Yin, *Taixue* or *Guzong*; in the Zhou, *Taixue* was in the eastern suburbs, and the elementary school was called *Yuxiang*. The Han and Tang dynasties developed these further. Yet even the sage-kings of Tang and Yu never ceased to learn: Yao studied under Jun Chou, Shun under Wu Chengzhao, Yu under Xi Wangguo, Tang under Cheng Zibo, King Wen under Shi Zisi, and King Wu under Guo Shu. Even Confucius once inquired about ritual from Laozi, sought music from Chang Hong, and asked about the *Yi* from Xi Ze. Mencius studied under the disciples of Zisi, and Xunzi under Zou Yan. This is the transmission of the Confucians.

Later, Dong Zhongshu and Han Yu continued this lineage, ultimately seeking the return to the mean, uprightness, benevolence, and righteousness, firmly rooted in self-cultivation. Those who draw from the rich essence of the Six Classics, refined within and flourishing without, are capable of assisting in the generation and nurturing of all things, manifesting the supreme harmony. This is expressed in writing, weaving together the warp and weft to bear the Dao.

Thus, the instruments of *heng*, *huang*, *tang*, and *li*, the vessels of *yi*, *ding*, *jian*, and *guan*—without adornment, one knows they are the sacred implements of the ancestral temple. How could they be compared to those who, like thieves of bronze and iron, boast of empty embellishments, competing in ornate carvings and trivial decorations to curry favor?

If one cannot reach the realm of uprightness, grandeur, and luminosity, but merely vies in the petty and trivial, how can this be called learning? One must grasp its essence and attain completion. Compared to those who gain from teachers and friends, it is as if one had merely memorized formulas or molded clay—achieving nothing without effort.

For what is gained from within—the subtleties of the Dao, the profundity of learning—fills and illuminates, daily expanding one’s vision. Brocade and embroidery cannot be called splendor; mats, tripods, jade tablets, and sashes cannot be called honor. Even the humblest of silk threads or grains of millet, the simplest of cups or plainest of water, do not diminish the joy of one who has attained this.

Yet is this not because even the slightest hindrance could burden one’s heart? Still, the swiftest steed cannot cover ten paces in a single leap, while the slowest horse, through tenfold perseverance, achieves success by never abandoning the effort. Though their destinations may differ in speed, the distinction lies in whether one ceases or persists. Such is the unwearying resolve of the sage.

My young nephew, so-and-so, is of pure and keen disposition, devoted to learning. He once asked his elder brother, so-and-so, to name his study "Daily Progress in Learning" and requested a few words to expound its meaning. As for my own learning, I have merely explored the byways without entering the gate—how could I presume to enlighten him? Yet I urge him to strive on, so that his practice becomes consummate and his conduct illustrious. Then I shall witness his accomplishments, which shall be boundless and immeasurable. This is my explanation, offered in anticipation.

---

**The Transmission of Lady Zixu, the Primordial Sovereign**

The Primordial Sovereign was surnamed Wei, named Huacun, styled Xian’an, and was a native of Rencheng. She was the daughter of Wei Shu, Minister of Works during the Jin dynasty, and was born in the third year of the Jiaping reign of King Qi of Wei (251 CE). Of extraordinary natural endowment, she read the words of Laozi and Zhuangzi in her youth and soon came to revere the Dao, delighting in seclusion and solitude. At the age of twenty-four, her parents compelled her to marry Liu Wen of Nanyang, styled Youyan, by whom she bore two sons, Pu and Xia. Youyan served as Magistrate of Xiuwu in Jijun. Once her two sons were grown, she purified her heart, withdrew to a separate chamber, abstained from food and drink, and resumed her original practice.

On the night of the sixteenth day of the twelfth month of the ninth year of the Taikang reign (288 CE), the Perfected of the Supreme Ultimate, the Lord of the Azure Lads, the Divine King of Fusang’s Yang Valley, the Perfected of Jinglin, and the Perfected of Qingxu descended and addressed the Primordial Sovereign: "Having heard of your secret cultivation of true qi, the Supreme Emperor has commanded us to transmit to you the Way of the Divine Perfected." Thereupon, they presented thirty-one scrolls, including the *Treasure Writ of the Supreme Lord*, the *Hidden Book of the Eight Simplicities*, the *Perfect Scripture of the Great Cavern*, and the *Mysteries of the Lofty Immortals*, saying: "These texts were once bestowed upon the Lord of Total Perfection of the Western City; now we entrust them to you." They also instructed her in the secrets of visualization and the essentials of return before departing. At that time, the Primordial Sovereign was thirty-seven years old.

In the ninth year of the Xianhe reign (334 CE), the Lord of Qingxu and the Azure Lads descended again, presenting her with two doses of elixir and commanding her to ingest them in preparation for a meeting at the Palace of Luoyang at an appointed time. On the night of the seventh day of that month, the Supreme Unity dispatched a terrace chariot to welcome her. Employing the Way of Concealed Radiance, the Primordial Sovereign entrusted her form to the divine sword and transformed, having lived eighty-three years. She then proceeded to Yangluo Mountain.

The following day, the Lord of Qingxu, the Four Perfected of the Supreme Ultimate, and the Divine King of Qingxu descended once more, meeting the Primordial Sovereign at the Cloud Terrace of the Hidden Grotto. Each Perfected imparted their ultimate teachings, and after three days, they departed. The Primordial Sovereign had recited the Jade Scriptures so extensively that her complexion remained that of a young maiden. Thereupon, the Queen Mother of the West and the Primordial Sovereign of the Southern Culmen came to welcome her. Ascending at dawn to the Jade Tower of the Palace of Supreme Clarity, the Great Dao Lord of the Jade Dawn, the Celestial Emperor of the Grand Tenuity, and the Later Sage Lord of the Golden Watchtower each issued decrees, bestowing upon her jade tablets and golden writs. She was invested as the Primordial Sovereign of Zixu, charged with overseeing the life-destinies of the Upper Perfected of the Southern Marchmount, her rank equivalent to that of an Immortal Duke. She was further entrusted with governance of the Central Terrace of Tiantai and the Great Huo Mountain, where she would instruct those who sought immortality. The Perfected of the Life-Destiny Office of the Immortals were placed under her jurisdiction in the Southern Marchmount.

The various Perfected then accompanied the Primordial Sovereign to Tiantai and the Central Terrace of the Great Huo Mountain. Passing through Jinqu’s Golden Altar, they were hosted by the Mao Lords, Shu and Shen, for a two-day feast before proceeding to Huo Mountain.

On the twenty-third day of the sixth month of the second year of the Xingding reign (1219 CE), the Primordial Sovereign and the host of Perfected descended to the Golden Altar of Jinqu at the home of Yang Xi, marking the beginning of the Huayang transmission. Thus, she became the first patriarch of the Supreme Clarity lineage. During the Yuanyou reign of the Song dynasty, she was posthumously enfeoffed as the Primordial Sovereign of Zixu, the Illustrious and Profound Dao of the Supreme Mystery.

---

**The Transmission of Jin Ye’an**

Jin Pengtou, a native of Yongjia, was named Zhiyang and styled Ye’an. His unkempt hair, gathered into a single topknot, earned him the sobriquet "Pengtou" ("Disheveled Head"). Born into a family of virtuous repute in his hometown, he was resolute and daring from childhood, possessing great aspirations and an untamed spirit. As he matured, he came to yearn for the Dao and relinquished worldly concerns, becoming a disciple of Li Yuexi of the Quanzhen tradition. Yuexi, a disciple of Bai Ziqing, recognized his potential upon their first meeting and dispatched him to travel through Yan, Zhao, Qi, and Chu in search of true teachings. Later, upon studying with the eminent master Li Zhenchang, he gained even deeper insight.

Passing through Yuanzhou, he encountered Dian Junzi, a garrison commander of imposing yet unadorned appearance, who shunned worldly company by day and lodged in spirit shrines by night. Ye’an, struck by his singularity, took him as his master. With time, their exchanges yielded ever deeper understanding.

At that time, Zou Tingsuo of Zishan, an admirer of the Dao, built the Changchun Abbey and paid homage to him. Shortly after, he entrusted the abbey’s affairs to his disciple Liu Zhixuan and journeyed to the two sacred mountains of Wuyi and Longhu. There, Fu Shizheng, the master of the Xiantian Abbey on Longhu Mountain, lodged him at Penglai Hermitage, situated at the confluence of the ruins of the Recluse, the Sacred Well, and Miaogu Peaks. Ye’an scaled cliffs and ravines, mingled with deer and boars, and slept amidst clouds and mist as though it were ordinary. By night, he would sit upon a boulder, and when serpents or tigers appeared before him, they would startle and flee. Thus he came upon a level, open expanse, where he commanded his disciples Li Quanzheng and Zhao Zhenchun to construct Tianrui Hermitage atop the peak.

As his reputation for the Dao spread, people from near and far afflicted by illness sought him out. Whatever fruits he offered them proved efficacious without fail, and so those who came to pay their respects grew daily. Once, during a drought, he knocked upon the Dragon Well and summoned the dragon forth. The dragon emerged to listen, its form gradually diminishing until it leapt into his sleeve. He admonished it sternly, whereupon the dragon surged back into the water, and rain fell before long.

In the *guiyou* year of the Yuantong era (1333), he withdrew once more to Wuyi Mountain, residing at the Zhizhi Hermitage of Ziqing. Li Taiping, the Yuan Marshal of Zhedong, heard of him and paid him homage, saying: "When commands are strict, the lord governs; when the mind is clear, cares diminish." Li was all the more filled with reverence and admiration.

When the rebel Li Zhifu invaded Zhangzhou, the imperial court appointed Prefect Wu to oversee the suppression of the bandits and named him vanguard commander. Wu’s son, Zhongliang, wished to become Ye’an’s disciple, but he refused. Soon after, the rebels were indeed vanquished. As the throng of spirits and deities grew weary of the constant petitioners, they plotted to poison him. Foreknowing this, he ordered his disciples to dig a pit in the earth, whereupon the poison was expelled. The site is now called the Elixir-Vomiting Well.

On the first day of the first month of the *zhi-yuan* era (1335), his fellow adept Gui Xinyuan—known in the world as Gui Fengzi—attained liberation while seated at Mount Lu. Ye’an soon learned of this. On the tenth day of the fourth month, he instructed his disciples to record his final admonitions and passed away in meditation. Thirteen days later, his face remained as though flushed, and his limbs retained their warmth and suppleness as in life. His disciples interred him beneath an ancient plum tree beside the hermitage.

He was born on the fourth day of the fifth month of the *bingzi* year of the Zhiyuan era (1276). His foremost disciples—Lao Yansu, Guo Chuchang, Li Xilai, Yin Pona, and Fang Fanghu—all became renowned in the world for their mastery of the Dao.

Mang Miaozi remarked: "The resolute men and righteous knights of old invariably endured hardship and purified themselves to fulfill their aspirations. Thus they regarded the rending of flesh and the breaking of sinew as matters of course, and by their towering singularity, they left examples for a thousand generations. Such was Jin Pengtou. Yet if one seeks to establish a name in the world, one must first attain such resolve—how much more so for those who transcend illusion and transformation? Those capable of such attainment yet failing to achieve the Dao are unheard of. Alas, how few in any age have been capable of this!"

---

**The Transmission of Zhao Yuanyang**

Zhao Yuanyang, whose given name was Yizhen, was a native of Anfu in Ji Prefecture. His ancestors hailed from Junyi, and he was a thirteenth-generation descendant of Zhao Dezhao, the Prince of Yan during the Song Dynasty. His forebear served as magistrate of Anfu under the Yuan and settled there. Yuanyang was precocious in childhood, quick to learn, and adept at recitation. He mastered the classics, histories, and the writings of the Hundred Schools, and in his youth pursued the path of the imperial examinations. Soon after, he sat for the metropolitan examination but withdrew due to illness. His condition persisted, and one night he dreamed of a divine figure who said: "You are a man of my Dao—why do you seek worldly eminence?" His father then directed him toward the Dao, and thereafter he became deeply devoted to tranquility and detachment. His learning advanced ever further.

At first, he studied under a man of the Dao in his commandery, one Zeng Chenwai, and inherited the essential teachings. Where texts were incomplete, he would invariably seek out and verify them in exhaustive detail. Later, he became a disciple of Zhang Tianquan of Taiyu Abbey in Ji, also known as Iron Mystery Zhang, who had himself studied under Jin Yea of Mount Longhu and received the oral instructions for the inner and outer elixirs of golden fluid. Subsequently, he studied under Li Xuanyi of Nanchang, who recommended him to his own master, Master Feng of the Robes of Rushes, who had also been a disciple of Yea. It was said that he once traveled to Yongxing Abbey on White Crane Mountain, the former site of the immortal Kuang of the Western Jin, and took up residence there. When asked about the methods he had been taught for summoning thunder and rain or refining the essence-spirit, he invariably elicited extraordinary responses, and those who heard of him would travel a thousand *li* to seek his instruction.

In the year *renchen*, as war broke out, he journeyed west to Wu and Shu with his disciples, and upon his return, he traveled to Mount Wudang, visited Mount Longhu, and sought out the vestiges of the Celestial Master of Han. At that time, the Celestial Master Chongxu deeply admired and honored him, wishing to detain him, but he declined. Many of the abbey’s scholars then became his disciples. On his return, he stayed at Ziyang Abbey in Yudu of Ganzhou and settled there. He compiled all the profound principles of the Daoist canon into written works. At times, he composed poems and songs to admonish himself, while also using medicine to aid others. In seclusion, he spoke little and laughed seldom, yet those who heard of him longed to pay him homage, though few were granted the opportunity. His lofty conduct and noble character were revered and admired by his contemporaries, and his followers grew ever more numerous.

In the first month of the year *rentu*, on the first day of the new year, he addressed his disciples, saying: “I shall soon depart. From this day forth, I shall seal myself in silent retreat—take care that none disturb me.” On the third day of the fifth month, the summer solstice, he emerged, exhorted his disciples to stand firm in their own practice, rinsed his mouth, bathed, changed his robes, and sat in meditation. Just as the county magistrate arrived with an imperial edict to perform music in celebration, he wrote a final poem, cast aside his brush, and passed away. Thunder and lightning suddenly erupted, and the daylight turned to darkness. The following day, officials and commoners who came to pay their respects gathered in great numbers. His disciples were dispatched to the regions of Yan, Zhao, Qi, and Chu to seek further instruction. When they consulted the former virtuous Li Zhenchang, they gained even deeper insight.

Passing through Yuanzhou, he encountered the garrison commander Junzi of Dian’s army, a man of imposing and unadorned appearance who by day held no commerce with the world and by night lodged in spirit shrines to inquire. Struck by his unkempt head, he took him as master. As time deepened, his words yielded ever greater insight.

At that time, Zou Tingzuo of Zishan, drawn to the Dao, built the Changchun Abbey and paid him homage. Before long, he charged his disciple Liu Zhixuan with overseeing the abbey’s affairs and journeyed to the two mountains of Wuyi and Longhu. At Longhu, the master of the Xiantian Abbey was Fu Shizheng, who lodged at Penglai Hermitage, a ruined sanctuary at the confluence of Zhengjun, Shengjing, and Miaogu peaks. With disheveled hair, he scaled cliffs and ravines, called to deer and boars, and reclined upon clouds and mists as though it were ordinary. By night he sat upon a flat boulder, and when serpents and tigers appeared before him, they would startle and flee. Thus he came upon a level and open tract of land, where he commanded his disciples Li Quanzheng and Zhao Zhenchun to build Tianrui Hermitage atop the peak.

As his Dao became renowned far and wide, those near and far afflicted with illness would seek him out. Whatever fruits he offered them proved efficacious without fail, and so those who came to pay their respects gathered in ever greater numbers. Once, during a drought, he knocked upon the Dragon Well and summoned the dragon forth to speak. The dragon emerged and listened; as it grew smaller by the moment, it leapt into his sleeve. He admonished it sternly, and the dragon surged back into the water. Soon after, rain fell from the sky.

In the third year of the Yuantong reign, he again withdrew to Wuyi Mountain, residing at the Zhizhi Hermitage of Ziqing. Li Taiping, the marshal of eastern Zhejiang, heard of him and paid him homage, saying, “When commands are strict, the lord governs; when the mind is clear, cares diminish.” Li was all the more filled with reverence and admiration.

When the rebel Li Zhifu invaded Zhangzhou, the imperial court appointed Prefect Wu to oversee the campaign against the bandits and named him vanguard to pacify them. His son, Zhongliang, wished to take him as master, but he refused. Soon after, the rebels were indeed vanquished.

As the spirits of the multitude grew weary or sought audience in great numbers, they plotted to poison him. He foresaw this and commanded his disciples to dig a pit in the earth. The poison was indeed regurgitated, and the site is now called the Elixir-Vomiting Well.

On the first day of the first month of the Zhiyuan reign, his fellow adept Gui Xinyuan—known in the world as Gui Fengzi—sat in dissolution upon Mount Lu. Hearing of this, on the tenth day of the fourth month, he commanded his disciples to record his final words, then sat in meditation and passed away. Thirteen days later, his face remained as though in slumber, his limbs warm and supple as in life. His disciples interred him beneath an ancient plum tree beside the hermitage.

He was born on the fourth day of the fifth month in the bingzi year of the Zhiyuan reign. His foremost disciples—Lao Yansu, Guo Chuchang, Li Xilai, Yin Pona, and Fang Fanghu—all became renowned in the world for their mastery of the Dao.

Mang Miaozi remarked: The resolute men and righteous knights of old necessarily endured hardship and kept themselves pure to fulfill their aspirations. Thus they regarded the rending of flesh and the breaking of sinew as matters of course, and so their towering singularity endured, sufficient to serve as models for a thousand generations. Such was Jin Pengtou. Yet if one seeks to establish a name in the world, one must first attain such a state—how much more so for those who transcend illusion and transformation? To seek such ability and yet fail to achieve the Dao is unheard of. Alas, how few in each generation are capable of this!

**Biography of Zhao Yuanyang**

Zhao Yuanyang, styled Yizhen, was a native of Anfu in Ji Prefecture. His ancestors hailed from Junyi; a certain thirteenth-generation descendant of Prince Dezhao of the Song’s Yan Kingdom served as magistrate of Anfu under the Yuan and settled there. Yuanyang was precocious in childhood, fond of reading, and skilled in recitation. He broadly mastered the classics, histories, and the hundred schools of thought, and in his youth pursued the path of the imperial examinations. Before long, he sat for the metropolitan examination but, due to illness, did not proceed. His condition lingered, and one night he dreamed of a divine being who said, “You are a man of my Dao—why do you aspire to worldly honor?” His father then directed him to follow the Dao, and thereafter he became deeply devoted to tranquility and simplicity. His learning advanced ever further.

At first, he studied under a man of the Dao in his prefecture, one Zeng Chenwai, and inherited the essential teachings. Wherever he found gaps in the texts, he would investigate and expound upon them in exhaustive detail. Later, he studied under Zhang Tianquan of Taiyu Abbey in Ji, also known as Iron Mystery Zhang, who had himself learned from Jin Yea of Mount Longhu and received the oral instructions for the inner and outer elixirs of golden fluid. Subsequently, he studied under Li Xuanyi of Nanchang, who recommended him to his own master, Master Feng of the Robe of Feathers. Master Feng had also studied under Yea.

It is said that he once traveled to Yongxing Abbey on White Crane Mountain, the former site of the immortal Kuang of the Western Jin, and took up residence there. When asked about the methods he had been taught for summoning thunder and rain or refining the essence-spirit, he demonstrated extraordinary efficacy, drawing seekers from over a thousand *li* to gather and follow him.

In the year *renchen*, when war broke out, he journeyed west with his disciples through Wu and Shu, and upon his return, traveled to Mount Wudang, paid homage at Mount Longhu, and visited the legacy sites of the Celestial Master of Han. At that time, Celestial Master Chongxu deeply admired and honored him, wishing to retain him, but he declined. Many of the abbey’s scholars then became his disciples.

Returning to Ziyang Abbey in Yudu of Ganzhou, he took up residence there. He compiled all the profound principles of the Daoist canon into written works. At times, he composed poems and songs to admonish himself, and he also used medicine to aid others. In seclusion, he spoke little and laughed seldom, yet those who heard of him longed to pay him homage but could not.

His lofty conduct and noble integrity were admired by his contemporaries, and his followers grew ever more numerous. In the first month of the year *rentu*, on the first day of the new year, he addressed his assembly, saying, “I am about to depart. From this day forth, I shall seal the gate of stillness—take care that none disturb me.” By the third day of the fifth month, the summer solstice, he opened the gate, exhorted his disciples to stand firm in their own practice, rinsed his mouth, bathed, changed his robes, and sat in meditation.

Just then, the county’s herald arrived with an imperial edict, playing music in celebration. Yuanyang immediately wrote a farewell message, cast aside his brush, and passed away. Thunder and lightning suddenly erupted, and daylight turned to darkness. The next day, officials and commoners who had come to pay their respects gathered in great numbers. His disciples, mourning deeply, requested that Master Deyuan and others prepare a coffin for the encoffining. His limbs remained supple as if alive. Once the rites were complete, sweat emerged, bathing his entire body.

Three days later, behind the meditation platform on Phoenix Ridge,...

After a long time, the grasses withered and birds no longer nested there. His disciples, such as Cao Xiwu and Liu Ruoyuan, still entered his chamber to receive instruction. Several of his poems and lyrics have circulated in the world; as for his profound and esoteric discourses, they are recorded in the *Essentials of the Dao*.

**Eulogy:**
The writings of the Mystic Gate number in the thousands, and the Way of inner sageliness and outer kingship is fully expounded therein. As for the teachings on immortality and longevity, they emphasize nothing more than emptiness, stillness, tranquility, and non-action—truly easy to understand and easy to practice. Thus, those who emerge in each generation to transcend the mundane must uphold austere conduct and lofty integrity. Without exceptional resolve and vision, none can attain such heights. How, then, could one dismiss them as mere delusions of the obscure?

As for Yuanyang, his words suffice to guide the world, and his Dao suffices to enlighten later generations. How could he not be commemorated? Yet what shines so brightly cannot be extinguished—what need is there for verification?

---

**Letter to Ni Mengchong on the Fire Times**

Recently, you graced my mountain retreat with a visit, and your lofty discourse filled the day, cleansing my withered and rustic heart—how deeply comforting! I had hoped you might stay a while longer, that we might speak through the night amid mountain rains and savor the true flavor of forest and spring. Yet you departed like the wind, leaving me with a lingering sense of loss.

Later, I received your refined composition, and after washing my mind and savoring its depths, I immersed myself in its meaning. Truly, there are moments when silent understanding and heartfelt resonance arise. Your learning is vast and profound—how could I, in my limited capacity, fully grasp or comprehend it? Yet I am precisely the sort who seeks to explore the subtle and delve into the hidden.

Recently, I have suffered unforeseen calamities. Flattery and deceit have grown rampant, and hypocrisy and falsehood compete to arise. The perilous and treacherous circumstances of the time are like fire that burns and water that drowns—nowhere to escape. I have no choice but to endure in silence, feign ignorance, and take blame upon myself. What more is there to say?

At such times, I have yet to see a single person who remains steadfast and unchanging, upholding propriety and mutual support. Why is it that so many abandon principle and follow the vulgar tide? Even if there are those with talents like Feng Guan or Zhu Bo, they remain mute, watching in silence without uttering a word. For the sake of wetting one’s feet, would one not save a drowning man?

Fortunately, with your wisdom guiding me and the legacy of our ancestors’ blessings, I have survived and returned. Yet my illness lingers, and the ways of the world shift like thunder and surging rivers—how could such drastic changes occur? Truly, it is lamentable.

Thus, in the midst of adversity, I strive to reflect inwardly, restrain myself, and cultivate virtue with reverence, awaiting Heaven’s decree. This is how the ancients handled glory and disgrace, gain and loss—accepting whatever befell them. Though loved ones may grieve and detractors rejoice, none can sway my heart or shake my resolve. Precisely in times of hardship and peril does one test the strength of one’s progress on the Way.

Thus, King Wen was imprisoned and composed the *Book of Changes*; Confucius faced adversity and compiled the *Spring and Autumn Annals*. Even among those who excelled in learning and literature—such as Ban, Ma, Yang, Jia, Han, Liu, Ou, and Su—none escaped exile or demotion. Yet their studies never ceased, and their virtue only deepened. As the saying goes, “Without encountering tangled roots, one cannot distinguish a sharp blade.” This is the truth. Did they seek to embellish their faults to deceive later generations?

As for myself, I have brought misfortune upon myself through caution, and by anticipating disaster, I have invited it. What else could this be if not fate and destiny? As the saying goes: “Cast a man to the jackals and tigers, and they will not eat him; cast him to the northern wastes, and they will not receive him; cast him to the vast heavens, and they will not accept him.” I have witnessed this firsthand—how could I not feel shame in my heart? Though I am humbled now, I may yet rise in the future.

Fortunately, I find temporary peace among streams and rocks, dwelling daily with trees and stones, wandering with woodcutters and herders. Little separates me from a rustic life, yet I am free to indulge my aspirations in discourses on nature, destiny, and the Dao. Compared to those mediocre souls who chase fleeting fame and profit—scheming and deceiving, jostling like flies and ants after carrion—what are their true intentions? Who among them understands the Way of advancing and retreating, living and perishing, following only what is right? They do not consider life and death, success and failure—why then do they scramble for favor and flattery?

Moreover, the mandate one receives from Heaven is fixed. How could the whims of a single person alter it? This is something the world cannot comprehend—it is the common affliction of past and present.

Yet your words have deeply resonated with me, and I have much to learn from them. Though my studies fall short, I have lingering doubts—particularly regarding the transmission of the *fire times*. For decades, I have consulted with masters beyond the world, yet few have truly aligned with my understanding.

Some speak of it in wild and absurd terms, dismissing it all as empty—claiming that the hexagrams, lines, and timekeeping are merely frameworks, to be followed but not rigidly adhered to. Others speak of it in profound and secretive terms, insisting on precise details—claiming that the signs, weights, and measures are its laws, to be upheld without deviation. Which of these is correct?

As for the mysteries of Qian and Kun, Kan and Li; the essence of metal and the sap of wood; the fire dragon and water tiger; the gold within water and the wood within fire; the marvels of reversal and return; the metaphors of the crucible, furnace, and elixir—I have heard them all. Yet I remain uncertain about the hundred days of foundation-laying, the ten-month gestation of the embryo, the mechanism of spiritual transformation after birth, and the precise application of the fire times. Specifically, the true yin and true yang of Kan and Li, their ascent and descent, their ceaseless circulation—this is the unity of the Five Phases and yin-yang, and yin-yang is the Supreme Ultimate.

From the Supreme Ultimate arise the Four Images, and from the Four Images emerge the Eight Trigrams. The Eight Trigrams unfold into sixty-four hexagrams, and the sixty-four hexagrams further unfold into three hundred eighty-four lines—endlessly circulating, ceaselessly flowing. Though heaven and earth are vast and the myriad things are boundless, none can escape this pattern.

This is how a single breath in the human body harmonizes with the Dao of heaven and earth, and how humans may steal the mechanism of heaven and earth. Viewed thus, a year has twelve months, a month thirty days, and a day one hundred ke. A month totals three thousand ke, and ten months total thirty thousand ke—within these thirty thousand ke, one seizes the tally of heaven’s thirty thousand years. Each ke of effort corresponds to a year’s seasonal nodes, so thirty thousand ke may seize thirty thousand years. Thus, a year’s twelve months comprise thirty-six thousand units, and within thirty thousand ke, each ke must be harmonized. Should even a single ke deviate, the medicinal ingredients dissipate, and the fire times falter. If the fire’s measure is excessive, it scorches; if the water’s weight is overabundant, it floods. The scorching of fire and the flooding of water must be balanced without fail.

Therefore, drilling years into months, drilling days into hours, within the twelve hours, only one hour is pivotal. One must not be ignorant of the new moon, full moon, first and last quarters, the bathings of purification and punishment, the waxings and wanings, the advancements and retreats. There is a saying:

*The Southern and Northern Schools invert the hexagrams’ images,
Dawn and dusk fire times align with the celestial pivot.
Follow the day, follow the hour to weigh the measures—
A hair’s deviation, and the elixir fails to form.
From of old, the commands of geng and jia are made clear,
Exchanging days for hours refines the divine work.*

The recorded fire times confirm the sages’ adage: "Transmit the medicine, not the fire." And again:

*If the winter solstice does not fall at zi,
The great medicine is not measured in jin.
True fire has no fixed time—
Comparing it to mao and you is futile.
Within this, grasp the meaning and cease seeking images;
To exhaust the lines is to labor in vain.*

Thus, the signs and measures are not mere empty words. This must begin with effort and culminate in effortlessness.

As heaven and earth complete one cycle in a year, the sun, moon, and stars complete one cycle in a month, and the great medicine of the human body completes one cycle in a day and night. Within a single day, yang is born at zi, reaches its peak at si, while yin is born at wu and peaks at hai. Yang begins with *Fu* (Return), and yin begins with *Tai* (Peace). Within a month, the new moon begins with *Zhun* (Difficulty) and *Meng* (Youth), and the dark moon ends with *Ji Ji* (After Completion). From *Xun* to *Kun*, all are hexagrams of the unborn—thus, what comes is met with reversal. From *Zhen* to *Qian*, all are hexagrams of the already born—thus, what passes is followed in sequence.

The year’s twenty-four qi and seventy-two seasonal markers—this is what is meant by "one return per month as one revolution, nine returns in a year as nine revolutions," which aligns precisely with the immortal masters’ teachings.

Master Zhu of Kao Ting, however, held that the method of fire times takes the three hundred eighty-four lines as the tally of one celestial cycle, with each line governing a day. Since there are more lines than days, the four trigrams *Qian*, *Kun*, *Kan*, and *Li*—totaling twenty-four lines—are removed to correspond to the twenty-four fortnights. As each fortnight arrives, the fire is gradually increased. Yet within a single day, the tally already completes three hundred sixty units, while the fire added per fortnight amounts to only one line—its weight and measure are incommensurate.

According to his method of calculation, the month is divided among twelve hexagrams, each governing two and a half days. Each hexagram’s lines are applied to its own tally. The tally’s quantity, whether greater or lesser, determines the vigor of the yang’s movement or the depth of the mind’s quiescence. Twelve hexagrams complete one month’s work, and twelve months complete one year’s cycle—repeating endlessly without excess or deficiency.

Is this not how the marvels of the *Great Change* perfectly accord with the great elixir? Through this method of withdrawal and addition, advancement and retreat, there must arise the transformations of waxing and waning.

How could it compare to the one-sidedness of sinking into emptiness and stagnating in quiescence—what the Chan school calls the "demon’s den beneath Black Mountain," for it is but silent sitting in the realm of yin? This diverges utterly from the essence of our Life-Dao school.

Only my friend, who has devoted a lifetime to exhaustive study and traversed lakes and seas, must have long grasped these subtle mysteries. I can but hope he will deign to enlighten me, that even in my twilight years, I might gain some small progress and await its fruition—how could I ever forget its source?

This is not a discourse to be shared with those long bound in mountain forests, clad in tattered robes and subsisting on coarse fare, severed from the world. Nor is it for those who flaunt themselves before the age or fawn for fame and profit. Yet one must also discern the unfit, lest words miss their mark—so may this not stray from true understanding.

With utmost reverence, I return the brush and bow, awaiting even a single feeble shot from your bow. How fortunate, how fortunate indeed!

*Jianquan Collection*, Scroll Four concludes.
[*Originally omitted the character "柔," now restored according to context.*]

*Jianquan Collection*, Scroll Five
By Qishan’s Non-Action Heavenly Master, Zhang Yuchu

**Admonitions and Inscriptions**
*Inscription for a Study, with Preface*

In my youth, before reaching adulthood, I developed a passion for learning and set my aspirations high. I memorized and recited all the words of the former Confucian scholars—every poem, book, and text of the Six Arts. As I grew older, I reflected that my grasp of the literary arts remained incomplete. Thus, in great cities and towns, wherever scholars of learning and conduct were renowned in their time as masters and elders, I sought their company. Thereupon, my reading expanded to encompass the classics, histories, and philosophical works, extending even to the writings of Laozi and the Buddha. The volumes piled up daily, yet only when I synthesized their essential teachings and turned inward to embody sincerity did I realize that all was already complete within me.

On the discourse of the Dao, virtue, nature, and destiny, from Confucius and Mencius downward, the teachings of Zhou Dunyi, the Cheng brothers, Zhang Zai, Shao Yong, and Lü Zuqian stand as heirs. In the craft of literary composition—from Zuo Qiuming onward through Ban Gu, Sima Qian, Han Yu, Liu Zongyuan, Ouyang Xiu, and Su Shi—no later school surpasses antiquity. If one’s learning departs from the masters of the Zhou and Cheng traditions, it cannot be called true learning. If one’s writing strays from the models of Ban and Sima, it cannot be called true literature. Thus, I reject even finely wrought texts that fail to convey the Dao. The ancients spoke of learning as self-cultivation—how could it be divided into two paths? Pursuing this principle to its fullest, one draws near to the ancient classics and their commentaries. Those who do not probe the root and seek the source—how can their efforts be deemed learning? It is not merely the adornment of rhetoric or the pursuit of rank and emolument. The essence of self-mastery and sincere cultivation lies in aspiring to sagehood and worthiness.

What is nurtured within must be cultivated without. In harmony, heaven and earth find their places, and the myriad things flourish. The profound suffices to embrace transformation and traverse the unseen, while the luminous suffices to refine ritual and music and assist in nurturing life. Is this not the way to guide rulers and benefit the people? Such is the great enterprise of ordering the fundamental principles of governance. Yet the path of transcending the world relies upon the words of Laozi, Guan Yin, Zhuangzi, and Liezi. How could it share a road with the harsh legalism of Shen Buhai and Han Fei or the reckless critiques of recluses? As for the singular conduct of the Buddha’s followers—illuminating the mind and perceiving nature—it too serves as an aid to transcending the world. But their heterodox doctrines and deviant practices cannot escape censure; how could one dismiss them all as mere emptiness and void?

As for later texts on divination, astrology, and omens, I have no leisure to examine them. I regret only that my dull and inferior nature is unequal to fully inheriting this aspiration. I have set aside a chamber to store these works and inscribed this admonition upon its walls as a warning to myself. May it also be passed to future generations to encourage their resolve—let them not neglect it.

**Inscription:**
Since the dawn of writing and the study of ancient canons,
The eternal principles follow their order—
The gateway of the Dao and virtue.
When the Supreme Emperor bestowed the celestial endowment,
The August One established the ultimate norm.
The Mandate of Heaven flows and operates—
One must follow its pattern.
In purity, sincerity, and clarity,
One returns to the origin.
When form is enslaved by things and altered by change,
Who remains hidden in my emptiness?

Only the sages and worthies
Have illuminated their plans and teachings.
With careful thought and steadfast practice,
One washes away dross and dissolves obstructions.
Establishing the body and enacting its use,
In movement and stillness, one maintains balance.
Cherishing the essence and savoring the bloom,
One manifests it in words.
The heart governs nature and emotion;
A hundred thoughts unite as one.
Expansive order, lofty chapters—
The great tone, the pure flavor.
Dispelling delusion and returning to truth,
Probing the subtle and attaining the mysterious,
Mists and grime dissolve entirely,
Never departing from Heaven.

To gather and dwell in discernment—
What value lies in jade tablets and sashes?
Hold these words dear forever,
And befriend the ancients across the ages.

---

**Inscription for an Ancient Zither, with Preface**

Su Kuai’s family possesses an ancient zither, said to have been crafted by Master Lei. Kuai is especially fond of the zither and once spoke of it to Mangmiaozi.

Kuai said: “Born late, I cannot fathom the realm of sages and worthies, nor do I harbor ambitions for fame and recognition. My refuge in the hills and forests lies solely in playing the music of high antiquity, responding to the wind in the pines and the springs among the rocks. How is this not a conversation with the people of Fuxi and Shennong’s time? Though I do not match Boya in skillful playing, in your keen listening, are you not the very Ziyou—he who recognized the high mountains and flowing waters? You should compose an inscription for me.”

**Inscription:**
Its substance moist and clear—
To express my song.
Its form round and square—
To uphold my dignity.

---

**Inscription for Reverent Silence, with Preface**

Mangzi wore a coarse hempen robe and a tattered fur coat, wandering atop Phoenix Terrace. A guest laughed and said to him: “You study the teachings of Master Zhuang—is your knowledge limited to the way of profound silence? Do you also know what we Confucians call ‘reverent silence’?”

Mangmiaozi stepped aside and addressed the guest: “Why do you fear this? Is it truly unwelcome, or merely superficial? Our king is virtuous and loves scholars. Since ascending the throne, no discourse on the Dao, virtue, benevolence, or righteousness has failed to enter his ears or reside in his heart. Thus, he pursues learning with tireless diligence and conducts himself with unwavering dedication. He has inscribed the words ‘reverent silence’ in the corner of his seat as a reminder to himself. Now that I have met you, Master Hou, how could I not offer a word to advance your understanding?”

Unable to decline, I composed this and spoke:

The Dao in relation to things—
Though heaven and earth are vast,
And the myriad beings numerous,
With no distinction between past and present,
Or day and night—
Its subtlety and wonder,
Its refinement and coarseness,
Its waxing and waning, advance and retreat,
Its transformations and changes—
All accord with the slightest movement of the heart.
It can match the utmost greatness of heaven and earth,
The utmost multitude of the ten thousand things.
Yin and yang, movement and stillness,
Follow what has no beginning or end.
Though the ten thousand differences swirl in confusion,
They are truly contained in a single root.

Grasp its essentials firmly,
And hold fast without lapse—
Then there is no loss in presence or absence,
And one merges with the reality of Heaven’s inherent principle.
If one grows lax in grasping,
Or slack in holding fast,
The obscurations of human desire—
How swiftly they gallop like a runaway steed,
Covering a thousand miles in an instant!

Yet the work of grasping and holding fast—
Is it not precisely what we call ‘reverent silence’?
Reverence approaches ritual.
Sincere reverence perfects virtue;
Tranquil reverence preserves one’s position;
Consummate reverence fulfills its measure.
Thus, ritual is perfected through reverence and only then is there peace.

Silence approaches the Dao.
Guard it with cautious silence,
Reflect on it with reverent silence,
Nurture it with profound silence—
Only then does one reach the ultimate work of the Dao.
Such is silent recognition, silent demeanor, silent resonance,
Attained without effort.

Yet there must be a means to achieve its center,
Lest one err by excess or deficiency.
Thus, conduct does not exceed reverence,
And silence does not fall short of concealment.
In reverence, one fulfills ritual’s unyielding demand;
In silence, the heart cannot but remain still.
Then in movement and stillness, speech and silence,
All is governed by the One—
No external desire can disturb one’s center.

If one should fail in due reverence or lapse in proper silence,
One falls short of the subtlest depths.
Thus, reverence knows no rest,
Silence knows no desire.
Without rest, there is unity;
Without desire, there is stillness.
Unity suffices to govern multiplicity,
Stillness suffices to master motion.
Such is the way—
Even the utmost in cultivating the heart and nurturing nature,
In preserving one’s Heaven-given endowment,
Does not exceed this.

Does one not wander in the primal vastness?
How does this differ from the thunder’s roar—
Or the profound silence of the hidden?

Now, when a worthy prince holds the altars of state,
He examines virtue and inquires into learning,
Devout, reverent, cautious, and tranquil,
Diligently taking the defense of the imperial house as his heart’s aim.
In his inquiries, there is a clear origin and sequence,
Extending upward to the sagely brilliance on the throne,
With brotherly affection deepening ever more.
How splendid! How magnificent!
I dare to inscribe this in verse.

**Inscription:**
Oh, radiant Lord on High,
Bestowing the center here—
All noble qualities
Are endowed with constant principle.
How outstanding the worthy prince,
Firm in his self-mastery!
Unified and preserved,
He dares not grow careless.
In solemnity and reverence,
Daily he strives for clarity.
Reverent and silent,
How could he ever falter or stray?

Lofty his hall’s words,
Majestic his virtuous teachings.
Not drawn to extravagance,
He draws from what is noble and bright.
Hold fast without wavering,
Grasp firmly to illumine—
Ever aiding the imperial screen,
Ever broadening virtue’s foundation.
His era attains its glory,
The imperial favor shines resplendent.
May his flourishing wings endure a thousand ages,
His great merit eternally radiant.

**Admonition on Concealed Cultivation**
The heart’s square inch governs the primordial One,
By which it matches Heaven and Earth as one body,
Stands with the Three Powers as their equal.
How is this endowment possessed?

It must be said: Principle is its master,
The Ultimate is its depth.
Without principle, it cannot stand;
Without the Ultimate, it is not complete.
Without standing, nothing can be ordered within;
Without completion, nothing can harmonize with Heaven.
Concealment dims its brilliance,
Cultivation disciplines its conduct—
Lest one be no more than a common man.

Who strays from the empty and numinous?
Thus, stillness and unity firm its root,
Vigor and compliance deepen its source.
Fill it with benevolence and righteousness,
Enrich it with eloquence.
Returning to seek within,
In action, one bears the duty of benefiting the age;
In retreat, one guards one’s mystery.
This is called “withdrawing from the world without distress,”
“Abiding in reverence and preserving the center.”
What ancient scholar surpassed this?
How dare I rest in aspiring to worthiness?

Yet in exploring the Dao of Laozi and Zhuangzi,
Delving into the words of the Zhou and Shao,
I temper myself without bias.
Only in the steadfast practice of these words,
Must I daily hold them fast.

**Ancient Inkstone Inscription**
Su Kuai in his youth loved books,
And treasured an ancient inkstone—
Purple in substance, white in veining.
Some called it from Duān or Shè,
Perhaps it was so.
He requested an inscription in stone,
To show future generations.

**Inscription:**
Its substance’s luster arises from stillness as virtue,
Its veining’s purity arises from silence as constancy.
In its capacity alone,
Writing knows no end.

**Inscription for the Nurturing Nature Studio**
Empty and numinous, profoundly clear,
A deep pool mirroring all.
The slightest speck mars its purity,
As a raging torrent soils a mirror.
The autumn sky embraces the void,
The cold river’s moon gleams.
Who wields the dark mechanism?
Only in stillness is there steadiness.

**Inscription for the Retiring and Resting Studio**
The ten thousand things share one source,
The profound principle is utterly luminous.
Hold fast with sincerity,
Rectify the root and master the self.
When turbidity, delusion, and falsehood arise,
Thoughts and cares swirl in confusion.
In movement and stillness, cultivate together,
Return to reverent stillness.
Go forth, scholars!
Awaken to the sagely words.
Turn toward dusk to rest—
Only in benevolence is one’s true nature.

Solemnly dwell in the vast abode,
Carefully seek one’s path.
Let no month pass without vigilance.

**Eulogy for the Perfected Moon Cauldron**
His heart roams in the Great Beginning,
His traces transcend the empty vastness.
When extended, he fills the six voids;
When gathered, he does not fill a fist.
Now feigning madness in the mortal realm,
Now discoursing idly on the dregs—
These are but incidental matters.
A drop may harbor the numinous,
As thunder’s charge stuns the rivers.
Who has glimpsed his body beyond the body?
How could one capture his likeness?

**Eulogy for the Gourd and Bamboo Hat Image**
Born of an illustrious lineage,
In youth he hastened to the gate of learning,
In maturity he cherished histories and classics.
If one calls him eminent—
When did he ever cast off his cap and sash?
If one calls him reclusive—
He has indeed rested his head on flowing streams and rinsed with stones.
Was this not unshaken by poverty or success?
Indeed so.

Only in his detachment from worldly affairs,
He stands solemn as a lone crane’s cry in clear frost,
Serene as a deep pool’s cold azure.
Who would deem this the abode of his vast learning?
Or rather, does he lodge his lofty purity,
Content with gourd and bamboo hat for his ease?

**Eulogy for the Confucius Inquiring into Ritual Illustration**
Confucius and Laozi took different paths,
Yet the Dao shares a single source.
Ritual is what we practice,
Holding reverence and preserving unity.
Heaven and Earth’s warp and weft,
The canons of Shang and Zhou—
The great sound is soundless,
The rest is silence.

**Eulogy for Boyi and Shuqi**
The Shang declined, the Zhou rose,
The mandate shifted with the tripods’ passing.
The Dao resides in constant principle,
Steadfast in benevolence and righteousness.
What endures? What perishes?
Who discerns? Who obstructs?
Gathering ferns in their seclusion,
Their lofty wind endures through the ages.

**Eulogy for the Perfected Lord Wang Chongyang**
The Dao flourished at its origin,
The mandate shifted with the fall of metal.
At Louguan and Zhongnan,
The lotus fragrance spread through the lineage.
A dragon’s bearing in his youth,
A tempest’s might in his prime,
Vastly he roamed the heavens,
His immortal aura gracing generations.

**Eulogy for Perfected Lord Le Zhen of Tang**
His spirit roamed the mysterious workings,
His radiance illuminated the true lineage.
Beyond form yet conforming to the image,
Within the compass of the celestial pattern.
With wondrous mastery of wind and thunder,
His heart beheld the imperial norm.
The imperial favor blazed resplendent,
Profound and silent, deep as the abyss.

**Eulogy for Perfected Lord Xi Yi**
White clouds of Mount Hua,
Wandering as his will inclined.
He cast off fleeting glory,
Silently guarding the mysterious ultimate.
The perfected one dreams no dreams,
For he treads the path of true repose.
Guiding his chariot through the void,
His form abides in the Great Simplicity.

**Eulogy for Perfected Lord Shi Chen Wang**
Endowed with a spirit of divine essence,
His Dao harmonized with the true transmission.
Encompassing sun and rain,
He transcended images before their arising.
His radiance aided the imperial designs,
A confluence of profound grace.
Like a leaf borne on the wind, his legacy
Awakens us from ignorance.

**Eulogy for Perfected Lord Huang of Lei Yuan**
Of vast and capacious nature,
Pure and luminous in his majesty,
He blended the myriad differences,
Comprehending all without bound.
The mysterious canon and supreme Dao,
The subtle patterns of esoteric words—
Surveying past and present,
He beheld the One Origin.

**Eulogy for Perfected Lord Yan Su Lao**
A vessel of rivers and seas,
Leaping like mountains and marshes,
He manifested wondrous function,
Unfathomable in being and nonbeing.
Autumn skies and forest whispers,
Stone chambers and scrolls of mystery—
His penetrating gaze spanned the distant,
As wind and thunder rolled and unfurled.

**Eulogy for Master Zhou Zhenbai**
Lo! In the august Yuan,
His prominence reached its zenith.
The bearing of virtue and righteousness,
The command of benevolence and purity.
Great was his ancestral line,
His illustrious culture enduring.
Still as a tranquil lake,
Keen as ice and snow.

**Eulogy for Master Su of Yingyan**
A countenance of clouds and sun,
A majesty of orchids and irises,
A loyal heart spilling ten thousand words,
Literary brilliance spanning a hundred generations.
Green peaks of Meishan against the sky,
The Ying River stretching to the horizon—
A thousand years of his legacy,
Solemn and resplendent in its clarity.

**Eulogy for the Feathered Robe of Qishan**
Feathered robe of mountains and forests,
Pure elegance of rivers and seas,
His conduct keen as ice and snow,
His heart warm as spring’s sun.
Soaring cranes and a lone skiff,
Wild geese and a plain zither—
No image could capture him,
For his was the vast, soundless tone.

**Inscription for the Nine Radiance Elixir Chamber**
The Marquis of Jingkou, Lord Mian, studied the Dao on Horse Hoof Mountain. Legend holds that the Most High once descended there, leaving behind a horse’s hoofprint before ascending—one of the blessed lands. Of steadfast and sincere nature, he was devoted to the arts of Qi and Huang, transcribing texts to seek longevity.

In the early Hongwu era, after years of service as a selected attendant at the Bamboo Palace, his methods and arts became renowned in his time. He named his dwelling the *Nine Radiance Elixir Chamber*. Having sent a letter from the Yellow Bamboo Mountain, he sought an inscription from me.

The inscription reads:

The cosmos serves as the pivot, opening the empty chamber;
The grand vault radiates light, pouring forth golden liquid.
The divine elixir’s nine rays manifest without form,
Fire dragons soar as water tigers flee.
Kan and Li unite their essence, congealing the Great One—
There stands the man in towering cap, jade pendants chiming.
Sacred scriptures cast their glow, outshining sun and stars;
Wind-borne and cloud-chased, he outstrips plow and steed.

*End of Volume Five of the Jianquan Collection*
*Volume Six of the Jianquan Collection*
*Composed by Zhang Yuchu, the Non-Action Celestial Master of Qishan*

**Preface to the Green Petition for the Ji-Si Year’s Offering and Vigil**
We prostrate ourselves, for as heaven’s mandate draws near the court, we aspire to repay the nation’s grace; as the teaching’s lineage bears its burden, we rely on the emperor’s vast creation. Moreover, in presenting our concerns to forestall calamity, we dread the unforeseen changes that misfortune may bring—thus we duly prepare this vigil and prayer, trusting in its power to preserve wholeness. With pure sincerity in our offerings of water-plants, we invoke the boundless blessings of the sacred and true. Humbly we submit our inner trepidation, that our red hearts may be secretly perceived.

We pray that your wisdom and insight shine brightly, that your august compassion be vast and all-embracing. May past transgressions be absolved, all transformed by your molding grace; may peace and prosperity gather from this day forth, all nurtured by your encompassing care. May our earnest words ascend to move the divine, and may the numinous power unfold in secret. May the lineage of the teaching flourish, harmonizing with spring’s warmth to grow ever more abundant; may the imperial realm be blessed with fortune, turning the seasons to timely harmony.

**Green Petition for the Ancestral Offering by the Zhang Clan**
From heaven we receive our nature, and in our hearts we pray to the mysterious vault; from our father we receive our bodies, and how dare we forget the hidden and distant grace? With reverent sincerity we beseech the mandate, trembling as we confess our faults.

We recall our late father, who was born in an age of flourishing poetry but passed amid family hardship. Bereft of parental guidance, he early suffered the sorrow of loss; treading through brambles and thorns, he barely fulfilled the trust of the garden’s legacy. His birthday drew near, yet the day of his departure came first. The grief of failing to serve him in his final years lingers, and my younger brother’s drowning deepens the sorrow. Though we longed to present our plea through this offering, we were repeatedly hindered by worldly entanglements. Now we undertake this rite of advancement, that he may attain liberation and enlightenment.

We pray that the red writ may absolve his sins, that the green register may extend mercy. May his innate nature shine clearly, washing away all boundless depths of stagnation; may the light of the Yellow Springs illuminate widely, universally aiding those lost in past delusions. May all sentient beings equally achieve deliverance.

**Green Petition for the Early Morning Offering**
The Qian Dao governs heaven, presiding over the norms of the mortal world; the imperial center establishes the pole, opening the ladder of supplication through the mysterious rites. Holding fast to the ancient covenant, we present our sentiments in gratitude.

We recall that we, your servant, inherited the lineage before coming of age, and the burden of office has been profoundly difficult. Though disasters have not repeatedly boiled over, the near loss of heirs has occurred several times. Our resolve has truly faltered in cultivation, and we rely on prayer and exorcism for personal deliverance. Time has passed in delay, and we have circled the jeweled altar ten thousand times; yet your boundless grace has responded to our single sincerity. As we look up to your lofty and profound kindness, we especially hope for your transformative power.

The morning rites are solemnly observed, and our vows are fulfilled. We pray that your forgiveness be without limit, that your compassion abide in the immortal courts. May you soon bestow a brilliant heir like the spring sun, and may the mysterious teaching’s splendor grow daily, matching the radiance of the celestial luminaries. May vast blessings be bestowed, and great favor universally bestowed.

**Regular Green Petition for the Offering**
As heaven and earth cover and bear all, the time swiftly arrives at the birthday; the ritual standards shine forth, and the fast is duly performed to fulfill our long-held vow. Looking up to the numinous, we reverently offer our thanks.

We recall that we, your servant, have inherited the office for years, yet our self-examination reveals no merit. Driven by anxiety, we have not been fortunate enough to see the restoration of peace; in serving the ancestral peach, we feel keenly the failure to fully succeed. Our morning and evening worries are deeply felt, and our efforts at introspection and renewal are earnest. We have prepared this offering and vigil with utmost sincerity, pouring out our heartfelt devotion before your imperial gaze.

We pray that the imperial way stand firm as a tripod, that the teaching’s lineage be treasured and upheld. May days bring longevity and health, extending the spring radiance of the mother’s hall; may family and household prosper, opening the orchid chamber to the long light of day. May these blessings extend to the immortal foundation, and may your profound grace descend upon all beings. May age and youth alike thrive in peace.

**Green Petition for the Completion of the Main Palace’s Grand Hall**
As the *Yi* reveals the great strength that gives rise to palaces and halls, and the Dao shines with double brilliance as the temple’s construction is completed, we look up in reliance on your encompassing creation and reverently express our gratitude.

We recall that we, your servant, have inherited the office for years, yet our self-examination reveals no contribution. We have received the emperor’s profound favor, fortunate to be honored with responsibility; through the collective sincerity of all, we have relied on the gathering of gold. The passage of time has been long, and the work of axes and adzes has been arduous. Now that the beams and rafters stand complete, we empty our hearts in this offering and vigil.

We pray that the imperial realm be as vast as heaven, that the teaching’s standards shine like spring’s radiance. May the people prosper, bringing blessings of longevity and health to all; may tranquility and purity prevail, securing the foundation of this mountain’s peace. May these blessings extend to the lineage’s transmission, and may your profound grace abide in the tripod’s stability. May the hundred spirits guard and protect, and may the five fortunes be universally shared.

**Green Petition for the Purple Tenuity Terrace**
As the North Star gathers the celestial bodies, governing the generation and nurturing of heaven and earth, and the Central Heaven administers the world’s balance, we reverently invoke the true rites and respectfully present our heartfelt offering.

We recall that we, your servant, have unworthily inherited the lineage and look up to the grace of your edicts. Traveling a thousand *li* to attend court, we have fortunately encountered the succession of the state’s altars; with wholehearted devotion to the imperial sacrifice, we have received the command to offer prayers for our ancestors. The hardships of the journey and the toil of travel have been many, and the delays of the star’s movement have caused concern. Yet our sincere prayers at the jeweled altar have fortunately aligned with the completion of the golden register. Thus we humbly present our repentant devotion and express our gratitude.

We pray that the Purple Tenuity shine brightly, secretly turning the radiance of auspicious stars; that the Yellow Path open clearly, abundantly extending the blessings of long life. May the teaching’s model grow ever more illustrious, and may the ancestral line thrive in peace.

**Green Petition for the Early Morning Vigil at the Repentance Terrace**
As the August Emperor of Heaven governs the mechanisms of creation, and as we revere the vastness of the azure sky, we dread the unfathomable nature of calamity and have earnestly prayed in advance to avert it. The rites and proclamations are complete, and we base our repentance and thanks on sincerity.

We recall that we, your servant, have inherited the lineage for years, yet our path has been fraught with difficulties. Reflecting on the depth of our transgressions, we have seen repeated sorrows and anxieties.

May the radiant oversight shine forth, as we humbly present our confession of faults in prayer; may the descending gaze remain unceasing, fulfilling the rite of morning audience at the Primordial Altar. With earnest devotion, we submit this green petition in gratitude to the Mysterious Heavens.

We pray that the August Ultimate may shine resplendent, and the Emperor’s heart take note. May the model of the teaching grow luminous and the household way prosper; may longevity and peace abound. May the verdant chambers of the maternal abode flourish ever green, and the departed harmonize in peach-blossom felicity. May transgressions dissolve like melting ice, and blessings flow like spring’s warmth.

**Green Petition for the Completion of the Universal Salvation Offering on Jia-Shen Day**
